The Red Page
Poems by Thomas Hardy
Tiie Photograph.
The flame crept up the portrait line by line
As it lay on the coals in the silence of night’s
profound,
And over the arm’s incline,
And along the edge of the nilkwork superfine,
And gnawed at the delicate bosom’s defence-
less round.
Then 1 vented a cry of hurt, and averted
my eyes,
The spectacle was one that I could not bear,
To my deep and sad surprise;
But, compelled to heed, 1 again looked fur-
tive-wise
Till the flame had eaten her breasts, and
month, and hair.
“Thank God, she is out of it now!” 1 said at
last,
In a great relief of heart when the thing teas
done
That had set my soul aghast.
And nothing was left, of the picture un-
sheathed from the past
But the ashen ghost of tine card it had figured
on.
She teas a woman long hid amid packs of
years,
She might have been living or dead; she was
lost to my sight,
And the deed that had nigh drawn tears
Was done in a casual clearance of life’s
arrears;
But I felt as if I had put her to death that
night!
— Well; she kneiv nothing thereof did she
survive,
And suffered nothing if numbered among the
dead;
Yet — yet—if on earth alive
Did she feel a smart, and with vague, strange
anguish, strive?
If in heaven, did she smile at me sadly and
shake her head?
Transformations.
Portion of this yew
Is a man my grandsire knew,
Bosomed here at its foot:
This branch may be his wife,
A ruddy human life
Noiv turned to a green shoot.
These grasses must be made
Of her who often prayed,
Last century, for repose;
And the fair girl long ago
Whom I often tried to know
May be entering this rose.
80, they are not underground,
But as nerves and veins abound
In the growths of upper air,
And they feel the sun and rain,
And the energy again
That made them what they were!
To Shakespeare.
Bright, baffling soul, least captarable of
themes,
Thou, who display’dst a life of common-
place,
Leaving no intimate word or personal trace
Of high design outside the artistry
Of thy penned dreams,
Shalt still remain at heart unread eternally.
Through human orbits thy discourse to-day,
Despite thy formal pilgrimage, throbs on
In harmonies that cow Oblivion,
And, like the wind, with all-uncared effect
Maintain a sway
Not fore-desired, in tracks unchosen and un-
checked.
And yet, at thy last breath, with mindless
note
The borough clocks in sameness tongued the
hour,
The Avon just as always glassed the tower,
Thy age was published on thy passing bell
But in due rote
With' other dwellers’ deaths accorded a like
knell.
And pit fhe strokes some townsmen (met,
maybe,
And thereon queried by some squire’s good
dame
Driving in shopward) may have given thy
name,
With, "Yes. a worthy man, and well-to-do;
Though, as for me,
l knew him but by just a neighbor’s nod, ’tis
true.
“/’ faith, few knew him much here, save by
word,
He having elsewhere led his busier life;
Though to be sure he left with us his wife.”
— “Ah. one of the tradesmen’s sons, I now
recall. . ..
Witty, I’ve heard....
We did not know him... .Well, good day.
Death comes to all.”
So, like a strange bright bird we sometimes
find
To mingle with the barn-door brood awhile,
Then vanish from their homely domicile —
Into man’s poesy, we wot not whence,
Flew thy strange mind,
Lodged there a radiant guest, and sped for
ever thence.
From “Moments of Vision” (Macmillan).
A strange bird, Hardy, too! A novelist
all his life, yet reversing the usual order
of literary development, which starts with
verse and ends in prose, he was G 1 before
he published his first volume of poetry.
Then he burst upon the world as the author
of the most stupendous literary drama of
our age. The Dynasts, in three large vol-
umes ; and now at 78 publishes another
large volume of verse, Moments of Vision
(Macmillan, per Sydney Book Club).
Curious poetry is his, saturated with con-
templative thought, the product of an origi-
nal and philosophic mind, the wisdom of a
wise old man. touched arid stirred by the
memory of youth’s long-dead romances. The
structure of his stanzas is precise and exact,
often deliberately complicated: lla,rdy
seems to take an architectural delight in the
building of his verse.... Gothic, we might
call it, massive and straight and impressive,
grotesquely decorated with grinning gar-
goyles of pessimism and cynic devils. But
there is no music in his metre; his lines do
not flow: they bark. His words are un-
couth and harsh; they say precisely what
they mean; they do not blur into beauty;
they have no secondary import that stirs
our subconsciousness with reminiscences of
beauty and sentiment; he adds his words
together like an algebraic sum ; they do not
flow together like a lyric or a stream. (The
verses already quoted were selected be-
cause they were comparatively free from
this fault of hardness and harshness.) Yet
sometimes Hardy, in aiming at simplicity,
ends up by becoming almost Bellerivean: —
Still chew
The kine, and moo
In the meadows we used to wander through;
Haymakers
As in former years
Rake rolls into heaps that the pitchfork rears..,.
Sometimes, pre-occupied by his thought,
Hardy achieves an unpremeditated effect of
the ludicrous, as in his phrase, “that
cow Oblivion.”
These are looking-backward verses. Hardy
is obsessed with death; he sees mortality
everywhere. He asks the moon what she
thinks of Life. “Is it much or no?” The
Moon replies that she often thinks of it,
“as a show God means surely to shut up
soon.” He still wonders: “If Man’s con-
sciousness was a mistake of God’s”....
And next I meet you, and I pause,
And think that if mistake it were,
As some have said, Oh, then it was
One that I well can bear!
And even when he kisses a girl, all that he
gets out of it is the thought that that kiss
cannot have died: —
Somewhere it pursues its flight,
One of a long procession of sounds
Travelling ethereal rounds
Far from earth’s bounds
In the infinite.
Did that ever' occur to you when you were
kissing her? And who but Hardy would
ever think of making a poem out of a far-
seen flash, a reflection of the sun on the
coffin of a friend on his way to be buried?
Thus a farewell to me he signalled on his grave-way,
As with a wave of his hand.
So a landscape seen again merely reminds
him of the day -
When amid sheaves in amorous play
In curtained bonnets and light array
Moved a bevy now underground.
And when lie sees Ethleen, all pink and
white, upon a daisied field, all white and
green, he cannot help thinking that “in
some mouldering year,” all the colors would
be mixed into one: “as one they both would
lie.” And, watching a ballet,
They crush together-—a rustling heap of flesh —-
Of more than flesh, a heap of souls. ...
he is struck not by their beauty but by the
thought that though all are alike in their
tinsel livery each girl lives in a world of
her own:—
Daughters, wives, mistresses; honest or false, sold,
bought;
Hearts of all sizes ; gay, fond, gushing- or penned,
Various in thought
Of lover, rival, friend ;
Links in a one-impulsed chain, all showing one smile,
Yet severed so many a mile!
He pictures a happy scene, and then :
Ah, no; the years O!
Down their chiselled names the raindrop ploughs.
He revisits bis own school in the flesh: —
I ought to have gone as a ghost....
Yes, wrong was the way ;
But yet, let me say,
I may right it—some day.
A ghoulish bard, “Bellerive’s” only rival.
One feels how greedily he seizes upon a sub-
ject made for his hand, the skeleton of a
lady’s sunshade!
Ah—it’s the skeleton of a lady’s sunshade,
Here at my* feet in the hard rock's chink,
Merely a naked sheaf of wires !. .. .
Where is the woman who carried that sunshade
Up and down this seaside place?
Little thumb standing against its stem,
Thoughts perhaps bent on a love-strategem,
Softening yet more the already soft face!
Is not the woman who carried that sunshade
A skeleton just as her property is,
Laid in the chink that none may scan?
And does she regret—if regret dust can —
The vain things thought when she flourished this?
He seems to gloat over it, this wonderful old,
old man.
Trawlers and Drifters
This war has turned the spotlight upon
the Empire’s merchant seamen. W. Mac-
neile Dixon, in The Fleets Behind the Fleet
(Dodder, per Sydney Book Club), pays the
merchant service a just and long-due tribute;
though the history of the part the merchant
sailors have played will never be fully
written. “The ships of the merchant service
are solitary wayfarers, scattered units in a
waste of waters. The adventures of 1000
ships, the deeds of 1000 skippers, how can
these be set forth in a convenient handbook?
... .Because courage and resource and deter-
mination are everywhere, a single act or
scene is nowhere elevated above the rest.
The unit is merged in the magnificent total.”
We are apt to forget, too, that the merchant-
men were the British Navy before the Royal
Navy was founded ; that out of 200 sail that
defeated the Armada but 34 belonged to the
'Queen’s Navy.
It is a stirring story that Dixon tells.
This mine-sowing and mine-sweeping goes
on, we need to be reminded, all the time.
It is a continuous menace. “Day and night
mines are freely sown—a patch here
and a patch there—steadily, persistently. . . .
Let the sweepers work how they will, the end
is never in sight. . . . Strewn with a lavish-
ness that counts no cost too heavy, they
represent an expenditure that runs to many
millions. In one area alone more than 1000
mines have been destroyed by our sweepers.
No more necessary, no more exhausting, no
more hazardous work than theirs is done to-
day in any waters.... In certain areas
mines are laid time and again to deflect the
stream of traffic into a channel where sub-
marines may act with comparative impunity
from danger. . . . But there is a certain
region through which, since the war began,
38,000 trading vessels have voyaged, in
which not more than four have been
destroyed by mines.” Dixon asks us to
weigh these, and other, facts, and consider
the compliment that fits the achievement.
This sport must not be confused with sub-
marine hunting in armed craft, accounted the
greatest of all great games. “Lowestoft
mine-sweepers hanker after leave not to
spend by the fireside, but on this brave sport.
Volunteers jostle each other for the service.
Admirals previously on the retired list renew
in it all the zest and vigor of their youth.
Alas,” laments this Englishman, “that
after the war a pursuit which outbids in
popularity tiger-sliooting or steeple-chasing
should come to an untimely end.”
But the trawlers and the drifters!
“They’re It, absolutely It,” said the com-
mander of a destroyer. “No weather’s too
bad for ’em. They’re our eyes a-nd our ears.
They know every blessed wave in the
Channel, not merely as passing acquaintances,
but they address ’em by their Christian
names. They’ll do anything, and go any-
where and chance the luck. They’re just
simple fishermen, but they run the whole
show and they run it magnificently—guns,
semaphores, wireless, everything! They
live on kippers and tea, and I don’t believe
they ever go to sleep !”
The author tells some stories of the mer-
chant service in war-time, from the official
records, which censor the illuminating adjec-
tive—cold, bare stories of stark heroism
that will enable the reader to endorse the
encomiums of the Royal Navy.
Germany, vvitli her trident of frightfulness,
reported that the Channel had been “swept
cltar of ships.” “Why, it is more like a
maritime fair. Never was there such a
bustle of shipping since the world was
made. .. .Motor-boats, flocks of them; scores
of traders at anchor in the Downs; busy
transports on their way to Havre; up to
windward a cluster of mine-sweepers; down
to leeward a line of lean destroyers. ... Very
busy, but very much at home, are the British
vessels in that long sea-lane. Talkative,
too, for the gossip never ceases. Hoarse
megaphone conversations, rocket and sema-
phore talk, wireless chatter without end.”
The chief defect of the book is its small-
ness. But the silent Navy has its silent
merchant service. The finest things cannot
be told—yet.
Australian
Agnes Littlejohn’s Star Dust and Sea
Spray, “imaginative stories written for chil-
dren” (Edwards, Dunlop, Sydney), is artisti-
cally produced. The illustrations by Percy
Leason and Sydney Ure Smith are delicate
and artistic, beautiful specimens of decora-
tive pen-work ; and the printer, A. McQuitty,
Sydney, has given these pen-poem decora-
tions their full value. Percy Reason's pen-
and-ink work is remarkable for a beauty and
color that is quite distinct from the pen-
drawings of Norman Lindsay. It is some-
what surprising to find a pen-and-ink artist
in Australia who has developed on his own
lines uninfluenced by the overpowering ex-
ample of Norman Lindsay’s acknowledged
mastery in this medium. But Agnes
Littlejohn has erred in choosing such deli-
cate illustrations for the pictures in a book
meant for children. Kiddies prefer in their
picture-books bold colors and a clear and
simple line. The stories; too, suffer from the
same defect. They are vaguely interesting on
quite conventional lines, and written in a
grown-up diction. It is not fair to the
child to bother its small head with adult
sentences like these: “The younger son pos-
sessed the business instinct.... The young
man pondered over his strange visions of a
delicate spirit in a lonely tower; its beauty
tarnished, and its wings bruised and broken.
....At length the day came when the rest-
less little wavelets waiting for the impulse
Which should send the shallows gladly sing-
ing to the sea, and stir the depths to a low
murmurous symphony—a song of joy—gave
the sign for some of the babes to be borne
with them to the sea-deptlis... .The birds
were calling to each other with a "restless,
musical insistence. . . .Tiers and tiers of rav-
ishing pearls rose up in columns to support
the roof of it, and the rich design of it
betrayed a master-hand in the art of intri-
cate building.” The book is full of this
strange adult jargon that would bore any
healthy Australian kid. Simplicity of
thought, simplicity of images, simplicity of
diction, simplicity of illustration are fljc
prime necessities for kiddies’ books; arid
with all these there can be originality and
beauty and poetry. But it isn’t in Agnes
Littlejohn.

The Round Taiu.e for March (Mab-
millan ; 2s. 6d.) is a dull number, dealing
chiefly with dead topics or topics so over-
written that they scarcely survive. Excep-
tions are informative articles upon the
peoples of the Baltic Provinces, and the ques-
tion of re-establishing the Jews in Palestine
and the implications of that experiment. It
seems to this page that if the war is going to
end with freedom for full expression of the
ideals of all minorities, the Jews will not
need to go to Palestine ; and there does not
seem, in countries like Australia, where they
have full political and social freedom, any
desire on the part of the Jews to return to
the land of their fathers.
<!*
In his preface to his new edition of Lord
Jim, which, it will be remembered, is in
narrative form, Conrad refutes the criticism
that no man could have been expected to
talk all that time, and other men to listen so
long. He points out that men have been
known to sit up half the night “swapping
yarns” ; and though this was only one yarn,
the listener could endure if the postulate
was accepted that the story was interesting.
He computes that Marlow’s narrative can
be read aloud within three hours. And he
presumes that there were intervals for drinks.
(Possibly Conrad has never heard of our poli-
ticians’ long-distance stonewalling efforts.)
But he admits that his first thought was of
a short story—the episode of the pilgrim
ship. That short story was never finished.
Asked for a serial for Blackwood’s, he
perceived that the incident would' make a
good starting-point for a novel. But he
rewrote the small portion he had already
written. Though as a matter of principle
Conrad will have no favorites among his
books, he is not annoyed by the preference
some people give to Lord Jim. He won’t
exactly admit that it iS his favorite. As
for Jim, he is “not the product of coldly
perverted thinking. .. .not a figure of North-
ern Mists either. One sunny morning, in
the commonplace surroundings of an Eastern
roadstead, I saw his form pass by—appeal-
ing—significant—-under a cloud —perfectly
silent. Which is as it should be.”
“Varsity”: “Simplicity” and her friends have
apparently never been in the University or colleges.
If such had been the case they would find that the
slang of C. J. Dennis’s Sentimental Bloke is a fine
rendition of the slang of these places of learning. I
have heard students of all types use practically
every word of slang that Dennis has quoted. And it
does not seem incongruous! Personally, I can faith-
fully claim to have used every word but two that
is in Dennis’s book.
“Digger 7833”: I know what the lucky Billjims
who handle our mails read. I have to be content
with what they pass on to me. F’rinstance, a cobber
who couldn’t hop correctly when the Perth medico
had us on the mat promised as his share to send the
local weekly. It comes as regularly as the rent-
collector used to. A sweet Annezac of the Adelaide
Cheer-Ups posts the local illustrated. I get that.
Miss Melbourne’s illustrateds never go astray. But
there’s a paper that rarely gets through. What
about changing the color of the cover, to oblige?

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Yesterday.
Clanging their querulous cry o'erhead
The wild geese flew in a wedge of grey.
“Where have they come from, dear?” I said ;
And Lorna answered, “F’om ’esterday.”
And the scales were lifted from eyes grown
old
And I saw, beyond where the wild birds
flew,
The Gate of Night with the Latch of Gold*
And the little islands that Lorna knew.
SCOTLAND.
WILL H. OGILVIE.
Australia in England.
He called. The quiet nurse stole to his side,
Seeing how in his hands he strove to hide
Dull tears that from his mother’s breast had
sprung
And stayed in his because he was so young.
He moaned: “How long must I breathe Eng-
land’s air
When the home hills are singing me out
there ?”
She leaned to him the pity of her soul
For the Death-bells beat out a muffled toll.
April was laughing in the English lanes,
Splendidly scornful of his hitter pains.
April was chuckling of a lover-hand,
But his closed eyes were in another land.
Oh, the lark soaring up the English sky
Had sung him home to meet the cux-lew’s
cry
He smiled.... The little nurse bent over
him—■
Blue eyes unmisted with a memory dim —
And moving gently from the dead apart
She heard his green hills singing in her heart.
N.S.W.
ZORA CROSS.
The Empty Mirror.
If she were here ifportTflffa bright Spring day
Intent on fripperies, she would go forth
To buy herself hat (some ribbon ends,
A silver buckle and a flower or two),
And come home with a flush on either cheek
And small hands full of parcels, and would
say
(The wonderful confection on her head
That halo of a daring milliner!)
“You’re sure you like it?” turning from the
glass
To press a hurried kiss on my grey hair,
And back again to her own eyes-of-youth
The mirror made not half so beautiful!
She was such lover of all pretty things,
Of fragile laces, and of muslin frills.
And small enamel discs, and dancing slices
With silly heels she told me were “quite
French,”
When nothing really mattered to my love
But just herself and all her burnished hair.
A ring I gave her was her constant joy,
A tiny golden loop for happiness. A tur-
quoise set
Between four pearls. No very costly gift,
But it was worth to her a king’s reward.
Alas! A straight white gow-n is hers to-day.
They do not need gold buckles in the grave,
Nor satin ribbons; and the nodding flowers
Will grow above her sleep, and weave a veil
And cover for her little shining head ;
But she can never turn to me, and smile
“You’re sure you like it?” from her vantage
point
Before that oval of reflected joy.
Spring will return through autumn's yellow
ways
And in the city windows I shall see
The broidered silken hose she used to crave,
The filmy lace, the glory of the hats,
The high French heels. .. .while I go home
Through streets that w-aver with old mem-
ories
To find.... an empty mirror on the wall.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
The Grey Lady.
, Stealing from her river-bed,
Where the tall trees, green and shady.
Sweetest scents of morning shed
Comes the Grey Lady.
Where the new-lit river flows
(Mirrored rose and opal paling),
Over rock and reach she goes,
All the world veiling.
To the courtship of a king
In the dewtime she is bidden.
While the thrush and magpie sing,
’Neath her robe hidden.
Fine as finest gossamer
Is her garment, soft and airy;
It w-as spun at night for her
By a good fairy.
Much as one who walks in sleep,
Through the tall trees, green and shady,
Where the leaves sweet silence keep,
Goes the Grey Lady.
Here her veil is thrust aside
By a wind that, laughing, sallies
Down the river’s placid tide
Or from green alleys.
Ah, and here she meets her king,
As with light and warmth he leavens
Earth and air, and everything
’Neath the high heavens.
Far and wide his glories move
Through the tall trees, green and shady;
Loved too much and killed by love,
Dies the Grey Lady.
N.S.W.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. Cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, _ those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,

illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., amy picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS. .

THURSDAY, MAY 2, 1918.
WINNING WARS BY PRIVATE
ENTERPRISE.
Long before this war started there was an
occasional voice in the wilderness prophesy-
ing victory for the nation that was in a
position to arm and promptly use its eligible
manhood, allot a useful job to the remaining
citizens and immediately take possession of
all property for the purpose of maintaining
the fight and paying the bill. Few people
would listen to the voice after getting the
hang of the argument. Of all “agitators
this was the worst; but as there were only
a few of him and the wrath of the multitude
of other agitators was so violent against
him, his influence was about as powerful as
a rabbit’s. Socialists, strong on the property-
seizing part of the programme, denounced the
conscription of men for service under arms.
The property-owning classes were as a rule
keen on conscription for fighting purposes,
but ready to produce 175 arguments to
prove that a pooling of other resources would
be immoral, suicidal and horrible. W here-
fore the all-in person was an outcast, derided
and rejected of men. That the infrequent
individual was usually a scholar counted, of
course, for nothing. Nor did it mattei that
he was trving to convert the nation to a
new wav of thinking, which involved look-
ing at the whole truth instead of a limited
part of it. * ' .
But the trials of four years have changed
the minds of men in a way that a century ot
argument would never have done. Men who
blew the roof off with denunciation of con-
scription* for military service, being con-
fronted with the alternative of defeat, have
swung round. So, too, thousands . of
men can everywhere be found to subscribe
to the theory that all the resources of a
nation should be socialised for war, who only
the other day cursed such a notion as a
pestilent heresy. Of course the advocates
of this step were usually moved by the con-
viction that under such a dispensation there
would be no wars, and the same hope in-
spired the advocates of conscription ot life.
Both parties were looking at only one part
of their problem. One side failed to see that
war could be made upon a people as well as
by them. The other failed to notice that
their property really went into a pool on
the day the first shot was tired. The main
difference that exists between complete
socialisation of the national wealth and a
system under which the wealth-production
continues to be privately managed for the
twin purposes of private gain and furnishing
the cost of war is this—that under the latter
system the cost is enormously- increased and
that goods produced by the labor of to-day,
and services purchased b.v the capital of to-
day, become a charge upon the production of
to-morrow.
* ■* * *
Now, there could hardly be much room for
difference of opinion among neople who were
asked to choose between t\vo possible futures
—one in which they would start from
scratch with free legs and the other in which
they would set out with a heavy burden.
Wherefore eminently respectable British
citizens and newspapers, watching the dis-
appearance of the national wealth down the
gullet of Mars and the creation of a vast
debt to be wiped off in the future, are
solemnly asking themselves whether Sosh
is the devil they thought he was. And
from most unexpected places there come
regretful voices testifying to the owners’
grief at not having been enlightened before.
The huge profits made by ship-owners, muni-
tion-makers, food-dealers and others are no
longer regarded as indications of prosperity.
They are almost everywhere now contem-
plated as part of the price the nation is
compelled to pay for muddling through. And
even by the people who are making these
profits the problem of whether they are going
to be left with much more than a mouthful
of ashes at the finish is a hideous uncer-
tainty. It is the same in Germany, where,
in spite of much more complete organisation,
colossal profits have been made and a
gigantic debt achieved. There, unless the
situation can be saved by plunder from out-
side, bankruptcy is plainly waiting on the
doorstep. It is not a question now in
Britain or France or Germany whether the
war should have been the signal for con-
scription of wealth. This has been in force
all along, in a sort of way; £840,000,000 is
being raised this year by taxation in Britain,
and it is only playing with words to say that
this is not conscription of wealth. The weak-
ness in the business is that a great part of
the wealth cpnscribed is profit made out of
the war—that the nation is to a large extent
depending upon the profit-monger tor
revenues and for advances which are con-
verted into a charge upon the national earn-
ings later on. The real question is whether,
instead of conscripting wealth by calling up
annual drafts, a wiser statesmanship would
not at one swoop have made every citizen a
worker for the State in the army or in
industry and socialised the whole machinery
of production and mental and physical
equipment possessed by the community.
-X- * * *
Answer to the question why the wiser
statesmanship did not prevail and muddling
through came to be tolerated is easy. The
Avorld had not learned its lesson and had
not the vaguest notion of what was going to
happen. The fool’s paradise was as wide
as the earth. When the war started no
country had the necessary equipment. _ They
had all followed the same lines of privately
controlled and directed production for indi-
vidual profit. The machinery of government
had been adjusted to this system. The State
limited its demands to the willingness of
individuals to contribute towards the pre-
servation of the State and the assumed capa-
city of the private machine to provide for
the corporate needs. In Britain there
existed the nucleus of a defensive system.
After terrible travail life and-substance was
imparted to it. But while Britain could
improvise an army she could no more have
put the whole national industry and existing
wealth into khaki, so to speak, than she
could take a leap off the earth and fly to a
new address. She had borrowed for her pri-
vate needs. Therefore she borrowed for her
national needs. Her public service knew all
about taxing incomes derived from foreign
securities and personal effort and mortgages
and shares in companies. So taxing pro-
ceeded in the old way with a close regard
for the continuance of the activities which
yielded the income and maintained the value
of investments. Socialisation would have
turned all this upside down. The man who
had shares in a steamship company and was
living on the proceeds would have ceased to
get any proceeds because there would have
been none. The individuals who were draw-
ing big profits from coal-mines and frozen-
meat stores and mortgages on city properties
would have had to bid them farewell because
none of these institutions would have been
producing profits. The entire social and
industrial system would have gone, up in
the air and what was left would have been
no more like what had gone before than
Bogan’s Pub resembles a prima donna. Ser-
vice alone would have been demanded of the
citizen ; and the proof that society is mainly
a rabble and the State everywhere a synonym
for chaos is that the utilisation of service
for the common good is exactly what no
nation could attempt. The best that any of
them could do was to struggle to maintain
the system which rests on private gain and
run the nation into stupendous debt to itself
for protecting its own life and liberty.
BESIEGED CITIES, IMMIGRANTS,
BABIES, ETC.
It was generally felt a generation or two ago
that Europe, so long as it had the price of a
loaf about it, was insured against want by
reason of improved methods of transit and
other develooments. But the new security
vanished. It w T ent almost as soon as it
came. The birth-rate did it. Thanks to
improvements in sanitation and medical
science and civilisation generally, the popu-
lation of many countries in Europe multi-
plied four-fold in less than a century. The
increase in some American countries was
much greater —about twelve-fold in the case
of the United States. The States of Europe
were all capable of feeding themselves m
1840 In 1910 Russia was practically the
only self-supporter left. The American
republics and India went strongly for a
while, but with the growth of population
thev began to eat more and more of their
own crops and have less and less for export.
The world’s food surplus wasn t anything
stupendous in 1910. The ravages of Huns
and Bolsheviks and Unspeakables, a small
amount of sinkage done by U-boats and their
kin. a wonderfully futile Australian Govern-
ment and some other causes turned the scale
on the side of famine. Australia has really
become a place of world-wide importance by
reason of what it hasn’t done. The Allies
are in such a tight place that the wheat
which HUGHES and co. didn't ship because
they didn’t build vessels might have made
history. In this matter the Government was
a crowd of talkative ants fooling with the
fate of nations.
People are justified in wondering whether
the birth-rate gospel should be swallowed
without modification. It is a custom to talk
as if “More People” was an essential m it-
self : unless a country with five people to
feed per square mile doubled its population
in a certain number of years so as to have
10 persons per mile, then 20, then 40, then
SO, then 100, then 320, then 640, then 12S0
and so on, it was “decadent.” Now a doubt
arises. This is the first big war fought
since the great improvement in means of
communication enabled Europe to draw for
its supplies on three-quurters of the globe
previously unavailable. That was good for
Europe. ' Also it is the first big war fought
since the tremendous and unprecedented leap
in population made Europe abjectly depen-
dent on far-off supplies. That was bad. If
the war had been postponed for another half-
century and the birth-rate had kept on its
dazzling progress, it is possible that a far
worse scarcity than exists now would have
come in the first year of strife instead of
the fourth. Later on the scarcity might
have come without any war at all.
* * * *
This does not alter the fact that Aus-
tralia requires a. great increase of popula-
tion. It wants them for defence, for even
if this war hasn’t come to out doors yet in
any serious fashion it may come, or the
next one may come, and no one knows when
the next one is due. For lack of men the
defence here is miserable. Also Australia
wants more people to hump a, share of its
public debt, for the- burden is ruinous. But
these points should be considered: —
(1) Among the countries of the world which pub-
lish statistics the Commonwealth has the best effective
birth-rate (effective birth-rate represents the children
that survive, not merely those that are born), bar-
ring only Bulgaria and Roumanian Taking the
States individually, Tasmania, Westralia, Queensland
and N. S. Wales beat the world.
(2) It is impossible to increase the size of
families indefinitely for economic reasons. An
average of eight children would mean less food, less
education and more child labor. There is a limit to
what the Old Man can earn even if he attains to the
ideal of eliminating the employer and securing the
entire product of his toil.
(3) It is impossible to increase the size of
families indefinitely as a matter of health. The
ideal birth-raters were the later Merovingian princes
of old France. It became a habit or tradition among
them to marry at 13, and to die of old age at 25,
leaving 12 degenerate children to mourn tjieir pass-
ing out. The sturdy Mayors of the Palace, who had
a respectable ordinary birth-rate, ran the Govern-
ment. In the end they deposed the wonderful but
imbecile birth-raters and became kings themselves.
(4) It is impossible to adequately garrison this
immense country in time for the next probable emer-
gency by the birth-rate alone, even if it is a good
one. No big empty country ever contrived to do
that. The infant isn’t born a soldier, or a tax-
payer or an artisan. If the Commonwealth is to
move ahead —not to become overcrowded, but to be
occupied to a reasonable extent in a reasonable time
—it can’t wait for the infant to grow up.
Therefore as an adjunct to the birth-rate
Australia wants desirable white immigrants
and a lot of them.
Australia is up against several hard facts.
It must have a lot more artisans very shortly
or else go without a lot _ of things which it
regards as necessaries of life, It can’t import
them on the grand old scale because imports
and' the interest on outside debts (which
debts will probably reach £1,000,000,000 by
the time the war is over), except in so far
as we pay for them in commodities, must be
paid for in gold. The local output of gold is
going down fast, and with diminished credit
the floating of gold loans in London can’t go
on with the old joyous abandon. So this
country, unless it proposes to file its
schedule, must import a great deal less and
manufacture a great deal more. And it must
get to work on this proposition very early,
long before the new crop of babies grows up,
unless the infant is set to do a man’s work
at the age of 10 or so. Incidentally a big
immigration policy would be true patriotism
on a big scale. It is a source of weakness
to have a country at one end of the empire
which is hungry because, even (hough. it
retains command of the sea, it is possible
for its enemies to reduce its superabundant
population to the verge of famine; and at
the other end a country which can’t get rid
of its food supply and hasn’t enough popula-
tion to put up a decent defence.
* * * *
Certainly there is not much gained by
having a birth-rate if there is no place to
put the baby except Sydney or Melbourne
or some other big city, according to the ethics
of the congestion policy ; and if there is little
for him to do when he grows up except float
loans and organise strikes. And there are
few features about a big immigration policy
if there is little to offer the skilled immigrant
except a precarious job at railway construc-
tion and some vague theories about the
reorganisation of society. So the baby and
the immigrant must be considered together.
Certainly the Australian infant might be,
and ought to be, better cared for than he is.
He is the best infant and the most numerous
infant in the world, though he can’t he so
abundant as usual while the head of the
family is away at the war; and he dodges
the habit of dying during his first year more
carefully than any other infant. Still he
should have a better chance than he has.
But he needn’t be rushed. The number of
births per 1000 people is less than it was,
but the gross figure could be doubled or
trebled in one act by killing off everybody
aged less than 16 or more than 50, for when
a country gets into the habit of keeping alive
those who are too young or too old to be
parents the baby-rate per 1000 inhabitants
must suffer. Because it suffers the super-
ficial exhorter proceeds to clamor. Thirty
years ago the easiest device of this sort of
exhorter was to be anti-Chinese. Now it is
to be pro-baby. But he shouldn’t paste his
advt. all over the cradle or seek the bubble
reputation by pretending that the Australian
offspring is extinct. The offspring will be
alive and going strong long after the exhorter
is dust.
THE SWORD-MAKER.
In the happy days of old Japan, so we are
told by a writer with a name like a crinkled
chrysanthemum, the maker of swords was
allowed to try the newly-tempered blade on
anyone who chanced to pass his shop. A
head dropped lightly in the thoroughfare, and
the armorer fondled the edge of his weapon
to see how cleanly it had done its work. But
the gentle practice had a way of teaching
the traveller to walk wide of the spot where
the sword-maker was likely to make his
spring; and it is not recorded that the.green-
grocer next door did a large business. Told
of a far country in a far age, the story is full
of shuddersome suggestion, and the reader
is apt to wonder how life contrived to go on
at all with such sharp possibilities lurking at
the street corner; but as a plain fact that
armorer is living close to us all at this
moment. Nay, more—he tries a multitude
of weapons besides swords and tries a million
of them at once; wherefore the public head
is very little safer on its shoulders for all the
progress of thought and advance of civilisa-
tion. The present war, beginning with the
double slash at Belgium and Servia, lias
run on into strange places.. Children in
Britain have been murdered in their beds;
the blade has pierced hearts in Bourke and
Petrograd and Montreal; the direction of the
blows yet to come are beyond the guess of
any man. Yet there are people who still
argue that these things are necessary, and
that even after the wreckage of the present
turmoil has been cleared away wars and the
miseries of wars must still have place, in the
\i r orld. It is said that national passion is a
thing beyond control, and that peoples will
still fight with hymns of hate on their lips
for obscure causes merely because man is a
fighting animal. The answer is plainly that
war, as we know it, cannot exist without the
armorer. Nations, however great their
wrath, could not harm one another seriously
without munitions; and the invasion of
France by Germany with a dinner-knife
would not be war in the grim sense in which
the Teuton has made us understand the
term.
The king-pin of the situation, then, is the
maker of weapons. Private interest in the
means of death and destruction lias always
been dangerous because men were _ able to
look to the shambles of war for dividends.
But national interest in munitions might,
under circumstances reasonably likely to
arise, be almost as bad. A hard-up nation
with most of its wealth sunk in the machin-
ery of war might on small provocation be
expected to us ( e that machinery before it grew
obsolete in the hope of getting the only possi-
ble return for its heavy taxation and enor-
mous expenditure in material which while
peace remained unbroken was absolutely use-
less. Safety demands the closing of the
armorer’s shop altogether, or, if that be not
practicable, restricting it to a small inter-
national concern capable only of arming a
world police for the common protection. No
doubt anybody could still get a bit of steel
and hammer out a pike in his back yard, as
men did in older days; but the prospect of
murder with such weapons need not be
alarming. At Naseby, in its results one of
the most important of all British battles, 500
Parliamentarians died. The death-roll to-
day in a mere raid in Flanders far exceeds
that number; and all the armies of the
seventeenth century,- fighting with pike and
musket, could have been rounded up by a
dozen platoons of the soldiers of to-day.
Just as a single modern regiment with a few
Lewis guns would have accounted for all
the might that Ckomwell and the King could
have jointly put into the field, so an army
raised to-day on the old plan to fight with
anything it could find would be easily con-
trolled by the rifles of a handful of inter-
national police. Were the armorer put in
his place; were gun-making by the individual
made an act of high treason against the
human race, and gun-making by any State an
immediate cause for interference by all the
rest, war would fade away. Guns are made
to shoot and swords to slash; and just as
long as they are permitted, shooting and
slashing will be regarded as legitimate occu-
pations for human creatures.
* * * *
But the abolition of the sword-maker is
entirely dependent on our will to win the
present w r ar. Germany has us by the throat;
and the reason the grip is so strong is because
of her infinite faith in the right of the
armorer to try his weapons on the passer-by.
If we fail to convince the Teuton by hard
blows that our moral rightness is more than
his war preparations, we shall be forced to
subscribe to his doctrine that the art of caus-
ing death and destruction is above all other
crafts. If the war ends in his favor, we
shall be making swords or dodging sword-
makers for. a thousand years. If the result is
a draw, the world can only be saved by a
more bitter renewal of the conflict. If we
have a straight-out win and drive the horror
that lias been forced upon us clean back to
German soil, there need be no renewal of the
controversy forever. Even in the old Japan-
ese story the armorer was only dangerous
when he had the sword tempered, sharpened
and ready for his grim experiments. M itli
the mere steel on his anvil nobody had any
need to dread him. The whole murderous
business turns upon war machinery. If the
machine is not there the war cannot happen:
and it is surely in no way beyond the power
of the world’s statecraft to arrange that the
machine shall not be created. To beat. a
sword into. a ploughshare is good; to pre-
vent a possible ploughshare being made into
a sword is far better.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
An Obstacle to Recruiting.
Keith Murdoch, cabling from London to
his Australian papers, makes an appeal to
a large class of eligibles:—
Strange stories have been sent me that recruiting
has been affected by a statement that England is
keeping an army of a million in Britain. This is
a lie. England has been drained. Our own depots
and bases have been toothcombed for fighting men.
Some statements about the past are undeniable;
hut let all eligibles know that everything will be
done to see that they get a fair deal in the
future. A powerful trust of men here will consecrate
themselves to this task.
A conference of the editors of Australian
metropolitan newspapers was summoned re-
cently by the Commonwealth Government.
It would probably be impossible to get to-
gether a body of men who have done half
as much recruiting work as these. Practi-
cally every day of their lives for nearly
four years they, almost without exception,
have racked their brains for new appeals
or for new ways of making the old appeal.
Their business has been to discover, each
through his own office organisation, the
objections and the obstacles, and to answer
and overcome them. But at this point most
unfortunately the Censorship has interposed ;
and without a single dissentient this con-
ference, representing eyery section of the
loyal press of Australia, declared “that the
suppression of matter on the ground that it
was calculated to prejudice recruiting has
itself become highly prejudicial to recruit-
ing by hindering the redress of grievances.”
It may have been all very well to pretend
that everything in the garden was lovely
so long as letters from the Front were
severely censored and so long as no men
were returning. But so soon as men got
leave and could drop their uncensored letters
into any post-box, and so soon as men began
trooping back, the suppression of references
to soldiers’ grievances became an absurdity
and a danger. Had they been ventilated
most of the wrongs would have been re-
dressed ; as it vwas, the complaints were
spread just as widely as if they had been
reported in print, they were often magnified,
and those that were utterly unfounded re-
ceived no contradiction. Had they been
quite frank about it the military heads, who
were responsible for the suppression, would
have confessed that what they really objected
to was comment upon military administra-
tion. The well-worn pretext for that objec-
tion was that criticism of “superior autho-
rity is subversive of discipline.” That may
have been a good enough excuse 50 years
ago, but it is no good any longer. The Allied
armies of to-day are not the armies of any
other day. They are intelligent, educated,
enfranchised. They are w T here they are not
because they are derelicts, but because they
are citizens, and the best of citizens at that.
The ranks are as much concerned in the out-
come of the war as the field-marshals them-
selves. To say that such men are going to
become an undisciplined horde if they read
in* a newspaper a statement of things they
hold to be wrong is preposterous. They don t
require to read a newspaper to know where
the shoe pinches—they know that already.
What they do resent is that the press should
be muzzled lest incompetent leaders and
absurd methods should be disturbed. Twen-
tieth-century men, and especially mem
brought up in a democratic atmosphere, hate
this feeling of helplessness. They want to
be assured of a fair deal; and no stronger
appeal could be made to a large class who
have held back, and no more welcome thing
could be told to a still larger number who
have not held back, than this assurance
from London; —
Let all eligibles know that everything will be
done to see that they get a square deal in the
future. A powerful trust of men here will con-
secrate themselves to this task.
But something must be done at this end too.
We cannot afford to lose one possible recruit;
and the men who have done more recruiting
work than all the other recruiters put to-
gether—the men who speak for an agency
without which recruiting would be impos-
sible —who talk every day to an audience a
thousand times larger than those which
assemble at the recruiting meetings—these
have deliberately and unanimously _ told the
Government of the manner in which their
efforts are thwarted. Any Government that
declines to remove the obstacle will have
to accept a very grave responsibility.

The Most Wonderful Discovery on
Earth.
The excited quarterly document issued by
the boss Labor organisation of N. S. Wales
contains, amid much general denunciation,
one statement which attracts the eye. It
is a tremendous statement —about the big-
gest ever issued in Australia —and it is
put forward with a confidence which sug-
gests that there is solid knowledge behind
it
The most frantic efforts, coupled with all kinds of
inducements and concessions, are being made to
secure investments in the War Loans from every
patriot with money to spare. Under a sane system
of finance all the money required could have been
raised on the public credit without the payment of
one penny of interest and the principal redeemed
over a long period of years.
Well, there is more money wanted for the
war, and for public works, and for the
repayment of debts that are falling due, and
if the chiefs of the Labor movement in
N. S. Wales know how it can be raised with-
out interest and won’t divulge the method,
there have been no criminals like them since
Judas Iscariot. As for the conduct of the
party in not applying its methods (assum-
ing that they are not an absolutely new dis-
covery) when it had control of affairs (there
have been six purely Labor Federal Govern-
ments and two purely Labor N. S. Wales
Governments), it can only be said that its
infamy is beyond words. The world is very
weary of the old financial problem and the
old, futile financial devices, and craves for
light and leading. It has debased the coin-
age and enacted that the new sovereign
must have the same value as the old one;
but when there was onlv 10s. worth of metal
in it nothing ever made it realise more than
10s. worth of goods. Thus the Scottish
sovereign got down to the value of a shilling,
and staved there till the wretched thing
was abolished and a fresh start made. There
have been and still are innumerable issues ot
paper currency based on the public credit
and the public assets and the producing
capacity of the community, and so on. But,
then, tiie most successful issues of Govern-
ment paper—the ones that never depreciated
—were and are based on the public credit
and the public assets and the private assets
of the community and the producing power
of the community and the stored-up labor ot
the community plus a sufficient reserve in
gold—the one kind of coin that is good all
the world over —to make that public believe
that it can get gold for its paper if so
desired. And when the community was
offered large quantities of paper with every-
thing behind it but the gold reserve, neither
patriotism nor enthusiasm nor faith nor law
nor all the prophets nor the lifting power
of innumerable elephants ever held up its
value. The Australian sovereign, when not
worn is good for 20s. (barring slight varia-
tions) in every civilised foreign country
because it will melt down for that value for
purposes of re-coinage into local currency.
Our shilling is at the moment worth eight-
pence, or thereabouts, outside Australia (it
is very much “thereabouts” owing to fluctu-
ations in silver values), because, it is
worth that when melted down. It is kept
up to a value of 12d. in Australia by a law
which provides that it is only legal tender
up to £2, and the same rule applies, in
Britain. If silver could be made unlimited
legal tender at its face value, Australia, as a
great silver-producing country, would be
on a good wicket; but no nation has man-
aged to keep it at its face value in these
conditions. The Australian paper pound is
worth 20s. in "Australia where the . gold re-
serve is ; and while there is a sufficient gold
reserve it will doubtless remain worth —os.
Outside, and away from the gold reserve, it
has no recognised value at all, for it cant
he boiled or melted down for re-coinage.. Of
course a certain forced value can be given
inside a country to a paper currency with
.no gold guarantee behind it; thus the 4s.
note of Guatemala is ivorth 3d. in com;
and there are other paper dollars of the same
nominal value which are worth 5d., 4d. and
M. If, after thousands of experiments have
failed to make the worker give full coin-
value for paper which had no coin or not,
much coin behind it, the leaders of N. b.
Wales unionism have solved the problem,
then they are marvels of statesmanship. It
they are only relying on some of the old
busted devices that have failed so often—and
a new device seems as hard to imagine as a
new continent —then they are not marvel-
lous at all. They are merely dangerous.
Why Neglect Our Own Vegetables?
The Inter-State Commission, in a report to
the Federal Parliament about trade in the
South Pacific, explains that there is scope in
the islands for raising cattle, coffee, cocoa,
copra, tapioca, vanilla and every kind of
tropical product. Furthermore, it points 9ut
that the labor problem incidental to raising
these things is very important, that the land
question is important, that shipping is im-
portant, and various other matters are also
important. In a general way ail this may be
true of the islands of the Pacific just as it
might be of various parts of Africa and
South America. This paper has no informa-
tion that the labor problem is not important
at Chicago, or that the land question is
negligible in Russia, or that shipping is a
minor detail to the Argentine. What it has
strong reason for believing is . that Aus-
tralians have on hand far too big a _ job to
leave them any time for interfering with the
local inhabitant of distant coral islands. Cer-
tainly cattle can be raised on some of the
islands. But the place for Australians to
think about in relation to cows is the North-
ern Territory. If coffee can be grown in the
Pacific, hundreds of tons more can be grown
in Bananaland. The same, thing applies to
the other commodities mentioned; and if any
more are wanted. Australia is in possession
of Papua (90,000 square miles), which is
quite enough to go on with. There seems to
be a sort of conspiracy afoot to persuade Aus-
tralians that they must rush off to the
islands and start teaching black brother how
to toil, and settle his land problem for him,
and “improve his condition,” and assume a
sort of over-lordship from Rabaul to the
Marquesas. The Bulletin’s idea is that
the Australian’s contract is here on his own
premises, doing for himself and his country
what a lot of busybodies want him to neglect
in order to go and do for other people and
obscure localities. It is one thing to object
to truculent strangers coming and establish-
ing naval bases in the neighborhood and
quite another to get tangled up in enter-
prises and responsibilities outside the boun-
daries of our own great task. The Pacific
islanders want nothing so much as to be left
alone. Australians want no more for them-
selves. Their plain moral duty is to
respect the ideas of other people; their prac-
tical duty to put their own house in order by
making the best use of their great heritage.
The Position in Ireland.
“Ruraidle O’Conor” writes: —
The popular misrepresentation of Ireland’s present
position with regard to the war and Conscription
calls for a cold statement and a warm protest.
Irishmen are not hanging back from the European
struggle and doing a national shirk to the everlasting
shame of a fighting people. Ireland is and has been
for many months on her feet, strong, militant and
proudly indignant. The beginning of the war found
a large number of her people actually in arms—the
Nationalists to assert and uphold British law as
placed on the statute Book ; the Carsonites to defy
that law. The moment the war began the law in
question was set aside with the goodwill of the Irish
leaders, who were determined to do nothing to
weaken Britain’s fighting arm in the big conflict.
The same leaders then offered to raise soldiers from
among their countrymen to assist the Allied cause;
hut the British authorities, bent, as always, on
creating the maximum of trouble in Ireland, refused
them permission to do so. This insulting slap in
the face for men who were eagerly loyal to the
cause of civilisation probably cost Britain a quarter
of a million recruits. English recruiting agents were
sent to Ireland, where they became a laughing-stock
as local fairs and other gatherings. Unable to hold
their own with the crowds they addressed either in
logic or in wit, they began, as Englishmen of their
class generally will, to threaten all sorts of pains
and penalties. Their threats were believed. Ireland
had too many memories of the carrying out of just
such atrocities as these agents suggested to refuse
them belief. The result was a tremendous impetus
for the Sinn Fein movement and other Republican
associations which had hitherto been of little account
in the country. The Dublin fight of two years ago
was the natural outcome.
Little sympathy as I have with that outbreak, the
thing was easy enough to understand. A certain
section of Irishmen declared that it was better to
die on Irish soil fighting for what they regarded as
an ideal than to die somewhere else assisting a
country that absolutely refused to play the game
in either letter or spirit. Being Irishmen, they no
sooner came to that conclusion than they proceeded
to die in a manner so startling that it won the
grudging admiration of their enemies. England
took alarm. If 1500 men put up a battle like that,
what might he expected from 500,000?
The Irish Conference was then called and brought
in a majority report in favor of Home Rule. Sud-
denly LnoYD George declared that Ireland must he
conscripted, and at the same time offered some sort
of self-government that has not yet been fully out-
lined. Ireland was more indignant than ever. Eng-
land was plainly with her back to the wall in the
great fight; but instead of inviting Ireland’s assist-
ance through her natural leaders, she chose the old
bullying method that had proved a failure in 700
years of experience. To say that Ireland at this
juncture cringes and shrinks from her duty is plain
insanity. Ireland is up and angry and strong. She
knows that for the moment the strong arm is hers
and not England’s; and she has no intention at all
of being bullied or coerced. Much as Irish sentiment
must run with the cause of France and the Allies,
it is difficult to see what the country could lose by
German victory. On the other hand, Ireland could
supply a quarter of a million men; and with the
coming exhaustion of the existing arrfiies, a quarter
of a million men might win the war.
The Irishman’s attitude is not one of hatred for
England, but one of profound distrust. The British
Government has an obligation of loyalty to Ireland as
strong as the Irish citizen has to the British Govern-
ment ; and as no practical proof of the former has
yet been shown, why expect, the latter? Let Ireland
have her free Parliament with no hampering condi-
tions ; then ask her, as Australia was asked, to take
her side in the war. No more is necessary. Free
Ireland would raise more volunteer troops for the
great cause than any British Government would ever
dare to call up under Conscription. That is the gist
of the matter. Far from shirking and showing
cowardice, Ireland stands erect to demand that the
military bullying to which she has always been sub-
jected shall now and forever cease.
The Suspension of Popular Government
in Maoriland.
Maoriland Parliament has prolonged its life
still further, this time until December, 1919
Meanwhile Massey and Ward have fled
again to London to sell more butter and
cheese and announce that their Dominion
is behind them to the last man. The cold
fact about this insolent disfranchisement of
the people is that Massey and Ward are
frightened to let the Dominion have a shot
at them. As it is now, democratic govern-
ment in Maoriland stands suspended, and a
political oligarchy with the assistance of
its numerous heelers is administering and
financing and legislating in contempt or
defiance of the people. It provides that the
Ministry may make regulations for the pur-
pose of procuring, enforcing and regulating
national service, prohibiting any employment,
occupation, business or work, and regulating
remuneration of national service subject to
the Arbitration Act. The crux of the law
is where the caterpillar carries its sting.
“National service” means “all occupations
whether under the Crown or any other
employer deemed to be essential to the pub-
lic welfare, and includes not merely personal
service but the use of any premises, machin-
ery or appliances, but does not include mili-
tary service.” As the Government refused
to add a provision that labor should not be
transferred from any trade to. another for
purpose of private gain, the -local. unionist
has a grim suspicion that the time is ripe to
keep wide awake. There is a cartload of
argument available to justify national ser-
vice being imposed upon everybody—if the
service is for the nation. But here is no
argument that would even palliate an
attempt to compel one citizen to labor for
another. That would be an enforced servi-
tude which can be best described by one very
unpleasant word. If the Maoriland Ministry
has the wit to avoid this, it is quite possible
that Labor may gain more than it is fearful
of losing through such a law. One obvious
result is that upon the State must soon be
thrown the responsibility for all employment,
and when the war ends an obligation of that
sort would be more useful to Labor than the
old freedom to wear out shoe-leather in
chasing a job.
The Fool Comes Home to Roost.
Apparently another half-million pounds'
worth of Westralia’s good loan jobs will
eat their head off in peace this year. That
is what the Wyndham meatworks cost. The
Government has offered 3s. 6d. per head for
slaying the bullock, the man who earned over
£8 a week to get as much as he earned and
the man who earned less to get £8 anyhow.
Working- hours were to be 44 per week, and
the bullock slain in overtime was to be worth
ss. 3d. Rates for inferior artisans were to
be £1 per day, £5 per week, and (boys) £3
per Aveek, Avith 50 per cent, extra for over-
time. Also there were to be free passages to
Wyndham and back, and half-pay during the
voyage. After three weeks’ negotiation the
unions declined the terms, so the bullock
Avon’t be tinned at Wyndham this year unless
the question can be re-opened, and 500 em-
ployees won’t be employees just at present.
The canned ox is mostly for export, and it
costs a lot to get him to the market, paying
war freights on some other fellow’s ship, and
when he reaches the market Ave have no
voice in fixing the price. The works cost
only the interest while they are shut. Ii
opening them Avould mean a dead loss on top
of the interest bill, then a Government Avhich
is desperately short of money has to think
twice before assassinating the beast once.
Westralia has gone in heavily for miscel-
laneous public works. Railways, tramways and
waterworks stand for little more than half
of the largest public debt per head on earth.
The rest is seAvers, immigration, lighthouses,
harbors and rivers, roads, bridges, develop-
ment of agriculture (nearly £4,000,000 apart
from the land revenues went in persuading a
little over 2,000,000 acres of soil to grow
things), development of goldfields (even the
magnificent gold deposits were buttressed
with £1,400,000 of borrowed money in the
razzle-dazzle days, which was a case of sub-
sidising the sovereign and half-sovereign
out of loans), hotels, workers’, homes
(£540,000 or so), sawmills, meatworks, agri-
cultural-implement works, brickyards, quar-
ries, etc. The easy-going habit of mind
caused by hiving almost unlimited loan-
money to spend led easy-going politicians to
pay the highest current wages—and the loan
boom had made them the highest in Aus-
tralia —with a trifle over to make its em-
ployees feel good on election day. Now the
news has to be broken to a bigger percent-
age of the community than in most other
States that the most wonderful loan bottle
in history has run dry, that it is only possi-
ble to pay wages based on the value of pro-
duction, and that State employees will have
to feel aood for nothing on election day or
else feel bad gratis. The position requires
a lot of adjustment. And considering how
Westralian politicians have run the State on
the rocks they require a great gift of ex-
planation. The best they can say about
themselves is that they were voluble fools.
The Avorst they can say is that they were
plausible knaves.
Vine Leaves and War Loans,
Melbourne Age lately groaned in anguish
that the War Loan suffered through Aus-
tralia’s consumption of liquor and tobacco.
It had discovered that the “national drink
bill” was £16,000,000 last year and that
£6,500,000 was spent on smokes. This is
one of those cases in which a well-meaning
enthusiasm gets into trouble through leav-
ing out half of the arithmetic. It is untrue
to say that the money spent on booze and
tobacco represents cash “withheld from the
cause for which our armies are fighting.”
Of the £16,000,000 spent on drink last year
the Commonwealth collected £4,044,201 in
Customs and Excise duties. From the
£6,500,000 spent on tobacco a revenue of
£2,470,969 was derived. This means that
through these two sources a section of the
community contributed £6,521,260 to the
public cashbox. In the previous year the
contribution was £7,151,482. In the year
before that it was £6,910,443, and in 1913-
14 £6,131.790. Now, from all these figures
there emerges the inescapable fact that
since the war began Australian revenues
have derived £26,714,975 from the drink
and tobacco habits. If that income-had
not been secured in this way it would cer-
tainly have had to be obtained in another.
That if a citizen instead of spending £5
on beer and tobacco had hung on to the
money and bought a war-bond he would
have saved something is beyond doubt. It
is equally indisputable that if every citizen
did this he would not have a £5 war-bond
unless he saved about £< 13s. 9d., for the
nation would fall upon fiim before he
reached the bank for £2 13s. 9d. as his share
of the revenue previously contributed for him
by the smoker and the patron of the bottle.
This might be a good thing. But it is not
what the Age and other horrified spectators
of partial statistics are asking for. Then-
particular point is that expenditure in two
special directions keeps money away from
the Treasurer. It does not. except in so far
as it destroys or damages the productive
power cf the people who drink and smoke,
and against that has to' be set the proba-
bility that in some cases these things in-
crease production. But that is not their
point either. They call the expenditure
“waste.” To the individual it may be. But
in this matter as in all others there is a
distinction between individual and national
waste. The really sinister aspect of the
aggregate expenditure on liquids and
tobacco by Australians is the waste through
importations of these commodities. Millions
sent out of the country to buy them are un-
questionably lost to the nation. In the
case of Australia, where goods of the finest
quality could be produced on the premises,
the transaction is wildly and wickedly
absurd. But no argument could possibly be
found if Australians were buying their own
eiears and drinking their own liquor ana
selling surpluses to people who could not
provide for themselves, to prove that wai
loans would be more rapidly subscribed it
the industries incidental to the pipe and
the glass were suppressed.
Winter Flowers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The morning breeze comes crisp and chill,
The last rich flowers are all in bloom,
A thin mist shivers on the hill,
A shadow creeps o’er stair and room;
Autumn with purple pomp is here,
Gliding to winter grey and drear.
The war goes on. The thundrous spray
Of charging squadrons breaks in blood.
Flame crowns with horror every day
And Death’s broad river brims in flood.
So many graves, so little done.
And Peace, that mocks us, still unwon!
Death is God's winter; yet the glades
Of Heaven are rich with bud and flower ;
And never blossom falls or fades
Where those wide airs and suns have
power.
The holy deed of sacrifice '
Is fragrant still in Paradise !
The guns have cried their Hymn of Hate;
The rifles hiss their curses shrill —
AH sins are shrived at Death’s red gate
Where purpose fires the fearless will;
And freedom’s blood upon the sod
Blooms as a splendid rose with God.
N.S.W.
D. M. W.


WHAT CO-OPERATION HAS DONE-
There is evidence on every hand of the splendid results of loyal co-operative effort. Where a body of men engaged in similar businesses have created their own
organisation to handle their products, etc., there is invariably a considerably greater profit to be reaped under good management. Further, co-operative organisations
mean strength, giving co-operators a powerful voice in all matters that concern them.
In no other business can the co-operative principle be so easily and successfully applied as in insurance. And nowhere are such fine opportunities offering as in the
insurance of farming and pastoral properties.

The Moving Finger of Mars.
It may be F ~:ing in France, but Billjim
and Fritz were not throwing daffodils at
one another when they met the other even-
ing. The story as it comes along the cables
is that the German at the close of a perfect
day of hell had pushed himself into the
town of Bretonneux, an important posi-
tion overlooking Amiens. As night fell the
enemy settled down to enjoy the loot in com-
fort, for there was no bombardment and all
seemed well. But a division of Australians
had been brought up and prepared for a
surreptitious visit in gas masks, and the
next thing that happened was a savage,
hand-to-hand battle, which ended in Fritz
being captured, cleared out, or counted in
the casualty lists. It was, all hands admit,
a big achievement, and our old Bill has
been getting three cheers ever since. Inci-
dentally, it has been mentioned that
Australian losses in the current defensive
have been 10,000. That is a heavy toll.
It gives a much clearer indication of what
the fighting is like than daily reports which
vaguely announce “appalling” losses suf-
fered by the enemy. That the German is
being heavily punished cannot be doubted,
but there is no evidence yet that he is
appalled by his casualties. That both sides are
ready to face a continuance of heavy losses
is well established. The German, who had
hauled himself off for repairs in the La
Bassee-Ypres sector a week ago, started to
punch again in that locality the other day.
He was also reported to be massing men
for more assaults in other localities —most
probablv in the vicinity of Albert and the
village where the Australians called after
dark. At the moment of writing he was
not held. Early in the week he had seized
the summit of the hills south and west of
Ypres, and by doing this had certainly
weakened the Allies’ hold northwards. The
position there looks likely to become very
difficult, and it is not encouraging to notice
that the printed stories from war corres-
pondents and military critics are a mixture
of pap and piffle. That is never a good
sign. There is no justification for believing
that, even if the enemy is successfully held
up on a new line somewhere at the north-
ern extremities of Armageddon, the German
attack can therefore be said to have failed.
Many other things will have to happen be-
fore this is established. The strong pro-
babilities are that this northern scrap is
only an incident, and that Ludendoref will
before Iona: throw his weight at more vital
points along the Somme and the Oise.
“Dr. Nil” to “Harrison O.” (B. 11/4/T8) :
(1) It is not a question of whether the “sister-
hood” would' increase or decrease “if Miss llodt's
scheme were put in force.” My contention is that
promiscuous sexual relations are not “necessary” at
all.
(2) I have asked what would be the use of medical
supervision of the women if the same did not apply
to their “clients.” In other words, how could the
“ladies” be kept aseptic unless all their “clients”
were examined bacteriologically before being admitted
to a seance? Mr. “O.” has not answered that.
(3) My phrase, “neither honest nor necessary,”
was used because Mr. “O.” drew an analogy between
prostitutes and other persons whose work is “honest
and necessary.”
(4) The fact that men will continue to do what is
neither right nor necessary, ad infinitum, does not
affect the statement that it is not necessary.
(5) Mr. “O.” is kind enough not to directly con-
tradict my assertion that “sexual relations are not
necessary,” etc., but in point of fact he does so by
implication when he adds that “punters curse the
trainers” who happen to take my view and that of
most doctors. I have more confidence in the latter
view than in that of the “punter” who may be an
excellent barber’s apprentice.
This is old-fashioned militarism’s last chance
to justify the system of universal military
service, national organisation of fighting
force, huge cannons, poison gas, flame-
throwers, and all other forms of Frightful-
ness directed by a staff of swanking superior
officers from the comparative safety of their
headquarters behind the lines. Old-fashioned
militarism, which is Hun militarism, depends
upon masses of troops, supported by masses
of cannon and governed by strict discipline,
“tactics” and “strategy,” for its existence as
a reasonable proposition. Its ideas are of
the earth earthy. It digs miles of trenches in
the enemy country and remains there for
months and years, whilst nothing in particu-
lar is happening save preparations of armies
and materials for a further advance in the
gory future. And it is haunted by a dread of
the time at hand when no military force on
earth will avail against supremacy in the
air—the time when big cannons, gas shells,
flame-throwers and goose-step drilling will be
reduced to impotency in the decisive settle-
ment of national conflicts. William Archer
likened Hun militarism to a bandit holding a
pistol to the head of Civilisation and saying
“Be militarist or die!” But that was more
than three years ago. Hun militarism now
knows that itself must die unless it,can at
once dominate the races which threaten it w T ith
supremacy in the air. Already troop trains
in transit have been bombed off the line, and
men on the march have been shot down in
scores by intrepid airmen. A first raid on
Cologne, by only eight machines, is said to
have added more than 200 deaths to the Hun
account. The figures thusfar are mere sug-
gestions of what wall be accomplished by the
light brigades of individualism against the
heavy organised forces of Hun ambition if
the desperate onslaught of the enemy masses
in France fails in its immediate object.

Adelaide has lost a useful citizen in A.
W. Hendry, who battled through life under
a heavy handicap. As a small boy he lost
one eye by an accident, and the other became
useless while he was a young man. lie
rose superior to the infirmity, became man-
ager of the Institution for the Blind in North
Adelaide, and showed astonishing powers of
organisation. His investigations included a
European trip, and it was always difficult
to realise that he was blind. He was only
58 at the end.
The position of Vice-President of the Execu-
tive Council, a new job in Bananaland, has
been given to Lands Minister, Hunter as a
stop-gap till he goes to England as Agent-
General. The possible loss of Ministerial
salary does not trouble Hunter, who is one of
the wealthiest men in Parliament —certainly
in the Labor party. When lie does go to the
Cold Country his loss will be felt keenly by
Premier Ryan. The last time a member of
the Queensland Cabinet was called Vice-
President was as far back as 1888, upon the
retirement through ill-health of Thomas
Mcllwraith.

Ministry of South .Aus., following the de-
feat of Jackson and Blundell at the elections,

four, instead of half and half as before.
So Barwell, the Liberal lawyer who stood

The other Liberals, Peake, Butler and Bice,
remain. So does Harvey, the Nationalist
in the Leg. Council. The new man is
Anstey, who has held a city seat for the last
10 years. He takes the Lands Office.

thought that trousers make the best execu-
tive wear. For several years the governing
body of the P.L.C. contained a few women,
but last year only men were chosen to con-
trol the movement outside Parliament. For

Nobody need be surprised that the local
Legislatures of the three most important
Russian provinces occupied by the Kaiser’s
troops have voted unanimously for annexa-
tion to Germany. Our own numerous Legis-
latures will do the same if the time comes; if
they don’t the Hun authority whose job it is
to see that the right sort of men are in these
Legislatures will have a bad time. Oliver
Cromwell used' to ryn his Parliaments on
somewhat similar principles. The elected
member who thought as Oliver and bis Mili-
tary Council thought he ought to think was
provided with a certificate and took his seat.
The member who hadn’t a certificate took
his departure. The trouble was that some of
the members who got in by thinking as the
Lord-General thought proceeded to think
differently when they were in, and in conse-
quence Parliaments were short-lived.

enlist, although eager to do so, was “para-
lysing news” to him, and that he would
“use his influence” to see that they were
allowed to go. Who rules the Defence
Department—-Pearce, who “uses his influ-
ence,” or subordinates who hinder recruiting,
with scarcely a word of protest?

“Henry Horsecollar” butts in:—
Those wharf-laborers who wanted the Court to
forbid one man running two winches so that the
same amount of work might be spread over more
men voiced a very old objection to machinery. Yet
this world wouldn’t be much of a place without it.
Time was, not much more than a century ago, when
the artisan couldn’t afford a handkerchief. He could
only earn it by the toil of a week or more. So it
was better to use grass. The cotton-planter in the
U.S. wasn’t rich then as he afterwards became; his
dignity and pedigree were his chief assets. His
employees, mostly niggers, were cheap enough. The
manufacturer in England and elsewhere was in a
small way, and was doing well enough if he made
the £S per week which the Westralian slaughtermen
have just refused. His employees kept body and
soul together—not much body and a minimum of
soul—rby the whole family working, even the six-
year-old infant. The principal trouble lay in the
appalling job of cleaning the seeds out of the cotton
bv hand. Then an interfering person, one Eli
Whitney, invented the cotton gin, a fairly cheap
machine which did the work of 2000 niggers. Later
another party who couldn’t leave well alone im-
proved the spinning apparatus so that one man could
do the work of six. An individual of the kind that
is always touching the paint made improvements
which doubled the previous fellow’s improvements,
and other prying inhabitants followed this up. The
planter grew so rich that he by-and-bye started a
civil war out of sheer pride. When slavery was
abolished the cotton-picking job proved able to pay
white men’s wages. The manufacturer became
wealthy and began to go into the House of Lords.
The artisan, though he isn’t a wildly prosperous
individual, is at least able to keep the family by
his own efforts and to do it better than when the
whole crowd toiled much longer hours. The infant
is able to go to school and actually learns to read
and write. And the household can afford to mop
its brow, and even some other features, with a
cotton wipe.
