Melbourne’s first impression of Gny Bates
Post in “The Masquerader,” produced at
the King’s last Saturday, must have been
that the actor was a better importation than
the drama. Not that the play isn’t remark-
able in its domestic bearings, apart from its
interesting opportunities for histrionics.
But this dramatisation of “John Chilcote,
M.P.,” is unconvincing in its unnecessary
up-to-dateness. It starts the story at the
outset of the Hun invasion of Belgium, and
puts Chilcote in the obviously fictitious posi-
tion of a Parliamentary orator who was
expected to bring the Asquith Government
to the war point, and was afterwards sup-
posed to be the instigator of the' Munitions
Department. In its connection with war
politics the play is false as a fairy tale. As
an attempt to realise the impossibility of a
morphined politician being impersonated
successfully in his home and the House of
Commons by an incredibly deceptive sub-
stitute, “The Masquerader” is at least a
daring novelty. And some of its few comedy
incidents are quite convincing.
* * * *
Guy B. Post’s dual-character contract gives
him no chance to seem an impulsive, pleas-
ing sort of person. In his Chilcote capacity
he is a morphia-maniac with a repellent,
petulant manner, who presently becomes
stupefied and eventually achieves an appall-
ing decease on the other fellow’s sofa. In
his ambitious John Loder capacity he has
to be such a miraculous duplicate of cousin
Chilcote, in all respects and to all appearances,
that everybody mistakes him for that cold,
stand-offish, monocled politician. In each
character in turn he is bound to wear the
one semblance with a subtle distinction of
style. By preserving people’s sympathy for
the fraudulent Chilcote, whilst increasing
their dislike for the real Chilcote, the
performer proves himself an artist
equally adroit and powerful. His acting
might be termed stagily natural because it
is naturally stagey in its air of restraint.
He has nothing of the effusive, ingratiating
manner of the modern melodrama star, but
much of the austere mannerism of, say, Henry
Lee, who played Cyrano de Bergerac here,
THE MAGIC CIRCLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The playhouse is for me a place
Of memories the sweetest;
I muse on years that sped apace—
The happiest are fleetest—•
When women’s lures, adroitly schemed,
Made beautiful the dreams I dreamed,
And each new pair of ankles seemed
The absolutely neatest.
Sometimes a cynic dares to raise
A question none can answer:
Do I speak truth of former days,
Or am I a romancer?
Is this young damsel on the stage,
Or she who glorified an age
Recorded on the yellow page
Of yore, the better dancer?
Is acting what it used to be
When Irving, king of mummers,
Appeared unorthodox to me,
A boy of sixteen summers?
Comparing modern times with then,
Discussing stars within my ken,
As is the wont of white-haired men,
I criticise new-comers.
Ton heroine in gauzy dress
Is slim and graceful, very;
Appealing if she feigns distress,
And charming when she’s merry;
In time she ought to make a name
And well deserve a passing fame:
I like her style—but, all the same,
I think of Ellen Terry.
The circle is to me a place
For pleasantly reviewing
The years when any pretty face
Set passion's storm a-brewing.
No matter how the play is cast,
Or going slow, or going fast,
’Midst visions of the golden Past
My youth I am renewing.
VICTORIA.
O. K.

The long, hairy arm of coincidence has
served the dramatist well; but the clever
author of “De Luxe Annie,” or, rather,
Scammon Lockwood, the clever author of
the story, has ' amputated the long hairy
limb. “There is no such thing as a coinci-
dence,” he asserts- —and sets out to prove it.
But he merely replaces the arm by an
artificial claw that has just as long a reach.
In this play, presented at the Palace in
Sydney on Saturday, a doctor, meeting some
friends in a Pullman car, tells them the story
of a series of what look like coincidences
which, when the clue is furnished, show the
chain of cause and effect that inexorably links
the smallest apparently-haphazard incident to
the next, and so on till the climax is reached.
He purposes telling the story by showing one
disconnected picture after another; and
then, as it were, pouring the developer upon
the negative and bringing out the complete
photograph. As he begins to tell his yarn
the. curtain descends —a device that is not
new in dramatic craft. And at the end he
concludes his story by, producing the heroine.
For those who have not read the story the
first scene is full of excitement and growing
perplexity—not lessened, on the first night,
by an obvious mistake in the mechanism of
the theft of a brooch. Then the long arm
gets busy till in the penultimate scene of the
play proper its mechanical substitute works
overtime. The doctor does not attempt to
explain why Jimmie chose a suit, out of
many suits, the pocket of which contained a
letter that was (necessary for the play-
wright’s purposes. The playwright crosses
his t’s very cleverly in this scene; and he has
used with considerable dexterity the theme
pf the emergence of a secondary personality
in an individual as the result of an injury to
the brain. That much must be given away
else no real criticism is possible. If the
dramatist will hang his play upon a surprise
as a climax, keeping the 1 secret from his
audience, he cannot prevent his secret get-
ting out after the first performance. But
even with this vague clue there are surprises
in store for future audiences, and the play is
clever enough to hold the attention of the
house right through. It is, of course, a crook
play: very little happens on the American
stage nowadays that isn’t crook. It is melo-
dramatic, too, in spots; but the character-
drawing and the comedy are excellent. It
is a play, too, that will bear examination after
the curtain falls, because of the ingenuity
with which the author has fitted every tiny
part of the puzzle together. But the play is
flawed by the initial assumption. Would, a
husband who discovers his wife in the com-
pany of a notorious crook, apparently living
with him, allow her to remain with him one
moment longer, no matter for what reason?
And would a wife fall in love with her hus-
band twice over? This is an assumption of
the novel and the stage, no doubt; but the
experience of the world is that a widow’s
second husband is usually as far away from
the type of No. 1 as she can get; while a
man, in the delicate business of choosing a
co-respondent, does not become infatuated
with his mistress because of her resemblance
to his wife. Still, without these assump-
tions there would be no play, which would
be a pity.
* * * *
The factors making this production a
triumph for the Taits are, in order, Emelie
Polini, the admirable cast, and the artistic
and ingenious staging. Einelie Polini has a
voice of great charm, and her petite person-
ality is appealing; she is every half-inch a
woman. Also, she knows her work down to
the most delicate nuance of expression in
gesture, manner and voice—is a competent
actress who instantly captures her audience.
Cyril Mackay’s well-known methods suit him
well in the part of a good-natured crook.
Olive Wilton’s matter-of-fact and insouciant
manner is quite in place in the perplexing
character of Mrs. Archer. Harmon Lee is
another of those calm and sneerful Ameri-
can detectives who must be turned out in
droves by the correspondence colleges of New
York. Their first lesson is to keep their hats
on in ladies’ boudoirs. A Mrs. Wiggs
character is cleverly accentuated by Georgie
Harvey, who introduces to us the boarding-
house-keeper of American fiction. Maurice
Dudley’s old man is a brilliant bit of charac-
ter-acting. G. Kay Souper does sterling
work in a minor but important part. Finally
Clarence Blakiston is a slightly unconvincing
sort of husband.
** * *
“The Willow Tree,” at Melbourne Royal,
attracts the eye, but never touches the imag-
ination. The scene is exquisitely delicate,
but the transitions from the fanciful to the
everyday rob the legend of half its national-
ity and sometimes bring it close to farce.
The beauteous Tree-Spirit that awakens to
life is especially abrupt. She hardly finds
her feet in Charles Waldron’s residence
before she is renouncing her semi-deity in the
man’s strong arms. Also she betrays a
marked interest in the facts of human origin,
and later makes her bed beside her lofd’s
with artless' matter-of-factness. It is in
these episodes that the poetry of “The
Willow Tree” is infused with “Madame
Butterfly” melodrama, and there is a subse-
quent development on “Butterfly” lines that
continues the parallel. Instead, however, of
knifing herself like Butterfly when she learns
that her man has another female familiar,
Miss Willow-Tree returns to her wooden
state and resumes the celebrity of an idol.
But before she becomes a lump of timber she
mentions that her suicide is necessary to
release her boy for the war —which is in-
congruously introduced at this stage of the
story—though the probability is that she
died of grief at the sudden discovery of the
Other Lady.
* *. * *
Kathlene Macdonell plays both the Other
Cat and the Image on the shallow pretext that
there is a physical resemblance between the
two. She makes the most of her few minutes
as Mary Temple, and is extremely pretty as
the Image; but her distance in spirit from
the .Japanese conception lends m> further
enchantment to the view. The idea of the
Willow Goddess is too fugitive and foreign
for her somewhat direct powers of illustra-
tion. Charles Waldron, stricken with the glory
of canoodling with a divinity, is the same old
sugar-monopoly who charmed girlish inno-
cence in “Daddy Long Legs,” “The Rain-
bow” and “The Cinderella Man.” As a gro-
tesque and forceful Jap Cardinal Roy Red-
grave represents the established profes-
sional’s jealousy of the mere amateur in
miracle-working. Tomotada, the image-
maker, might be one of Lafcadio Hearn’s
wonderful old men of the pre-Meiji era of
Japanese history; but' George Barnum
nevgr gets the character into focus. The
two servants, Plumpton Wilson and Kenneth
Brampton, have every imaginable humilia-
tion of speech and gesture in dedicating their
ignoble selves to the service of “Honorable
Master.” Redge Carey plays one of the
heartless voung knuts of modern Japan with
considerable humor; Styan is a trumpet
blast of British materialism ; and Eily Mal-
yon, Fred Cambourne and Frank Hatherley,
minor features of Jap life, intrude into the
fantasy at odd moments.

** * *
Sydney Tivoli is filling-in a fortnight with
a programme of vaudeville. The best turns
are those of Truly Shattuck and Emma
O’Neill and Mason and Gwynne. Murry
Livingston, in a protean turn entitled “The
Dreamer,” recites doggedly with his head
stuck through a curtain while unseen hands
rapidly re-costume him. During these change-
ful moments he looks like a cheerful
soul in the stocks. Except for the
pawnbroker and the green-lit miser, there is
a strong family likeness among his charac-
ters ; also he has the dear old actor’s trick
of adding “ah” to the end of his sentences.
Belle Sylvia, the lady baritone, is a novelty;
but there is nothing captivating about her
voice. Aubrey and Riche are a well-dressed
girl-team wiGi a mild dancing turn sur-
rounded by artistic scenery. Graham’s
Marionettes rattle cheerfully, and Hughie
Ogilvie gets back to his right line of Lauder-
ing briefly in kilts. George Edwardes and
Rosie Parkes supply a light comedy turn,
and Hugh Laurence, a solemn small boy,
throws his robust voice all over the house.
The Wirths are holding Sydney Hippo-
drome against all odds, and when they
evacuate the position the elephants intend to
lead the way staggering under the weight of
the proceeds. Meantime new stunts are on
tap and the lions roar in a louder key. A
feature of the revised menu is the water
pantomime, in which, after a city has been
washed over Niagara from the stage into the
bottomless depths of the arena, the remainder
is a hilarious episode of mermaids afloat and
ashore. All this wetness provokes great
applause and leads to many bets being made
about how long it will be before , the entire
company las a cold in the head.

“The Bing Boys” continue to bing in the
dollars at Melbourne Majesty’s. The varie-
gated show is not merely a success; it is a
triumph—from a business point of view.
Perhaps the dancers—Clyde Cook by him-
self and Clyde Cook and Maie Baird in com-
pany-score topmost notches among the
principals, though the decorative art of
Minnie Love, the valuable voice and broad
humor of Cannot, and the smile of Nellie
Payne are remembered as matters of pri-
mary importance apart from the multitude of
girls who count as indispensables. After
all, a revue is mostly what the chorus girls
and the costumifere make it, and even the
connivance of the costumihre is not always
necessary. In one or two of the most thrill-
ing episodes the clothing carries no weight
to speak of.

At Melbourne Tivoli the programme is still
“Samples,” and the Devil still offers a lame
apology for the loss of his tail. Also the
black-and-white ballet scene, and the pre-
cocious kiddies and the cheerful frivolity of
it all are carried on with the old industry
and perseverance.
Alexander Watson has re-arrived in Mel-
bourne, and on Saturday will appear
again at the Athenaeum Hall with a pro-
gramme of “Patriotic Purple Patches” of
monologue, including a patch of C. J. Den-
nis. Next week he returns to Shakspeare,
Dickens and other subjects duly stated in
the advt. For those who have once heard
him in the past any recital by Alexander
Watson is an attraction. For the benighted
section of the populace which has never
heard him it is an opportunity with a capi-
tal O.

Billie Seaton, who appeared a while back
at Melbourne Tivoli, is doing a few nights’
hard at the Bijou, in a song recital that con-
tains more Billie than the circumstances
warrant. Ray Traynor, her pianist, is also
ragging furiously. Near him Belle Verne is
singing on a bicycle, and a young man
named Russell is yodelling in a costume that
is Tyrolean at the top but turns Dutch
towards the feet. Of other new-comers
Madame Lorenz, trapezist, puts a lot of
shape into the show, whilst Bertram and
Raymond contribute a wayback sketch that
was not written by Steele Rudd.

The wicked Baron Duisanti did the
Scarpia act in “Panthea” at Melbourne Bri-
tannia on Monday. This is the film wherein

a figure that the rich baron almost has to

Jack Pickford and Louise Huff stand high
in the estimation of Hoyt’s advts. in Mel-
bourne this week. The young couple are
starred as the chief conspirators in “The
Ghost House,” a creepy spook-drama that
makes several spectres look almost authen-
tic. In the other section of the bill comely
little Blanche Sweet reappears, after a long
absence, in a Lasky production, “The
Sowers,” from the novel of Seton Merriman.
:f: % %
“Canary Cottage,” running a successful
week at Adelaide Royal, reveals itself as
perilously like the old Yankee “sketches”
that Irving Sayles used to play. There is
strong internal evidence that Field Fisher’s
part was originally pieant for a nigger. Still
the public has come at the knock-about
foolishness with every sign of complete enjoy-
ment. There is a personal triumph for Connie
Ediss: her rendering of a fat woman undis-
guisedly and fiercely in love is powerful act-
ing without ceasing to be funny. At the
Tivoli the Wiliiamsonian “Girls’ Cross
Roads” is chiefly notable for fine work by
Ida Gresham as tile dipsomaniac wife. The
Fuller pantomime at the Majestic has an
extraordinary amount of personal violence in
it. Whenever a funny man is at a loss, he
threatens the nearest person with “a slosh
on the gazooka,” or if words fail him he does
the sloshing without the notice, on the
gazooka or elsewhere. The assault and bat-
tery is in every case received with delight
by a lawless audience. Nellie Kolle is aston-
ishingly popular as principal boy, Victor
Prince and Zoli work hard and effectively,
and the interlude of dog and monkeys, goes
well. Pictures this week are mostly on the
“intense” side, and patrons who like inten-
sity signify the same in the usual manner.
Hughes’s talk about prohibiting the im-
portation of luxuries was like the rest of his
achievements. The other day Senator Keat-
ing was told that in the six months ending
March 31, 191 S, no less than 5229 pianos
had been imported into Australia. Of these
800 came, from England, 3921 from U.S.A.,
223 from Hong Kong, 179 from Japan, the
other 100 were possibly German: counting
in the freight, close upon a quarter of a
million sent away at a time when the job
of keeping gold in the country is almost as
important as the other job of sending fight-
ing men out of it.

While his powers for toil endure,
And to loosen the hold of a cough or a cold
With Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.

MINCHINBURY
“Pint Pot”: When the Australian Light
Horse came across a mosaic pavement in a
captured Turkish trench down in Southern
Palestine last year, Padre Maitland Woods,
of Ariah Park (N.S.W.), superintended fur-
ther excavations. Finding an inscription re-
lating to St. George, the padre reckoned that
the saint’s relics might be underneath,
and digging proceeded. The officers’
mess mostly scoffed at the likelihood of any
STILL HOPE.
“So your father and mother both object to
me?"
“Yes! But don't worry. They never agree
long about anything."
human remains being found, and the sceptics
gave the enthusiast a bad time. When at
length the mosaic was lifted, and fragments
of a skeleton were discovered beneath it, the
principal sceptic had moved on to a staff-billet
at headquarters. Woods triumphantly wired
him: “Saint’s body discovered.” The wire
got into the hands of a puzzled Red Tab at
11. Q., who hadn’t heard of the mosaic affair,
and a few hours later Woods received a
reply wire: “No record here of Trooper
Saint being reported missing: kindly for-
ward identification disc.”
“Catkin”: When you go on leave now
you might easily starve in London if you
didn’t take a “ticket” with you. It is the
first thing the waitress asks you for when
you sit at a table. If you haven’t a ticket
to show that you are away from camp you
get no meat and not much of anything else.
This is to prevent double-banking. You
carry your sugar-ration card with you, too,
and hand out a check for every lump. Food,
food, food —it was never so talked of since
the first bandicoot was knocked over with
a gibber. But, on the whole, in the most
good-tempered way. Thousands of women
who might have their fifteen-pen’orth of
meat a week like the rest are foregoing the
lot in some cases, and all but a cat’s? meal
in others, so that the men at hard war-toil
may have a double portion. And everybody
gets comfort, as usual, out of the greater
misfortunes of his neighbors. In Belgium,
they tell one another, and it is probably •
true, butter is regularly a shilling an ounce,
and sometimes as much as eighteenpence;
and even in Denmark, which hasn’t felt the
foot of the invader, but only the fist that
threateningly grabbed the livestock, there is
greater privation than even England knows.
“Quartermaster”: As “Bombardier” (B.
25/4/T8) says, troops, whether going to the
Front or returning, have, to pay a levy “to
make good the shortages discovered at the
end of the voyage.” These shortages pro-
bably do not exist. As a quartermaster of
three years’ experience in the present scrap,
I found the biggest grabbers to be the
shipping companies. When the ship that
took us away from Gallipoli after the Evac-
uation landed in Egypt, a gigantic bill of
shortages was presented to the C.O. for
settlement. After an exhaustive inquiry it
was found that most of the articles had been
missed about five trips previously. I sup-
pose the last five C.O.’s had each been pre-
sented with a similar bill. _ Every ship’s
quartermaster should immediately on em-
barkation handcuff himself to the chief
steward, and personally make a checked
inventory of everything issued to the troops.
On re-checking at the end of the voyage the
shortages will be found surprisingly small
and can always be sensibly accounted for.
I was Q.M. for 1400 troops returning in-
valided from England. The barrack fee at
the end of the trip was nil. But being old
at the game I had taken good care to have
the inventory checked immediately we got
aboard.
* * * *
“Success”: Thomas Barrett, thq first of
Australia’s scraggees, whose name I find in
a very recent Bulletin, may be usefully
recalled for the benefit of those people of
independent mind who are apt
to say, “Oh, but they can’t do
it!” Mr. Barrett, aged 19,
transported for stealing, reck-
oned that Governor Phillip was
not invested .with the power to
hang people, but had to obtain
the King’s permission before
taking such arbitrary action.
Accordingly he and some four
others who held similar views
broke into the store and stole
food, of which they and every-
one else in the settlement were
much in need. Being caught,
Mr. Barrett and his friends did
not conceal their contempt for
the Governor; nevertheless that
hard-worked and conscientious
naval officer did his best to
avoid scragging them. The posi-
tion was explained clearly to
the independent Barrett and his
friends, and the necessary pre-
parations made for finally con-
vincing them. The others real-
ised that, they were mistaken,
and went to work ; but the con-
tumacious Barrett, strong in
his conviction that Phillip
couldn’t do it, persisted. Hence Phillip,
faced with the necessity for maintaining
discipline in the hungry little settlement,
duly and faithfully hanged Thomas Barrett,
pioneer of a long line of sus. per coll.
“Hamer”: Uncle Sap, with the /evident
idea of drawing attention to the emancipa-
tion of his military forces from the formali-
ties of older civilisations, has abandoned the
forms of the English drill-sergeant and
adopted a new lot. The Yankee soldier does
not “stand at ease” ; he “slackens.” When
his sergeant permits him to stand easy he
gets the order “Stand slack.” He springs
to attention at “Stiffen !” I have also heard
the command: “Into fours —git!” But I
am told that that isn’t regarded as regular.

HOSTESS: "Why didn’t you bring your husband along to dinner?”
GUEST : “Oh, you see, he’s the goose that jays the golden egg, and I never take any

the attentions of Jerry Fritz. As >putties when
working in mud empty sandbags beat the
drum. As carry-alls they are indispensable.
Their use for parapets, pillows, rubbish-
boxes, camouflage, aprons, cooks’ saucepan-
lifters, sponges and towels are well known to
the army. I’ve seen strips of them used for
socks or Prince Alberts, while the string in
them is used for bootlaces and wind-gauges.
These wind-gauges are a big help in the
event of gas attacks. Last, they are used in
the field post-office as mailbags. Any Billjim
who can nominate a more useful ally is wel-
come to my ration of army stew.

A danger signal accompanied by affidavits:
The selection of cadets for the Australian Navy
is apparently regulated by the size of the parent’s
belltopper. bf nine boys lately taken from Vic-
toria four are known to have come from prominent
Melbourne colleges; two were the offspring of
affluent country gentlemen, whilst there is strong
reason for supposing that the remaining three satis-
fied the social standard, which is an insidiously
growing factor in the choice of our sailors. To own
a parent in a lowly calling is horribly damaging,
and is quite ruinous when the distracted' elder asks
the Navy Office to explain the rejection of his
child: his polite 'inquiries will be met with diplo-
matic evasions, and personal application will only
wrench the information that the Committee’s
decision is secret and final. A vast amount of un-
deserved responsibility rests upon this Examining
Committee. Up to 1916 its choice was confined to
the top-markers in a competitive -examination, but
now its power of selection has been increased by
lowering the standard of success to 40 per cent, of
the marks, which on a paper set for the mental
level of a third-form boy is calculated to bring
everybody but the numskull into its presence. The
possibilities for favoritism by this system cannot
be sufficiently emphasised.

New reading of old saw: There is plenty
of room at the Front.
Cairns is waking up to the fact that some
of John D. Rockefeller’s dahlers are about
to be spent in the Juicy North. An ener-
getic Amurrican doctor, filled to the chin
with the latest information on ankylosto-
miasis (he calls it hookworm for short),
and sponsored by the Health Department,
shook the slumbrous Town Council recently,
and shouted in its drowsy ear the glad tid-
ings that John D. had arrived. The Coun-
cil talked uneasily of the cost of these medi-
cal fads, but was assured that it cost nothing
—John D., with a little help from Ryan,
footed all the bills. Public interest being
aroused by the chance of getting medicine for
nothing, Cairns is blossoming into hearty
appreciation of the kerosene magnate and
his envoy, and reckons that it is about time
the drains were seen to on the same
terms. Also the malaria, which is begin-
ning to draw uncomfortable inquiries from
tourists. Waite, the medical envoy from
Wilsonland, is a keen young man who talks
like one of Jack Lait’s short stories, only
faster.

WINING THE WAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“At the Sydney Millions Club luncheon to-day”
List to the rhythmic rattle of each plate,
The sharp staccato of each knife and fork!
And who can doubt our nation will be great,
Fostered in youth on tucker, booze and talk !
Come 1 pass your empty plates, for time is fleeting-.
And win the war, by constant, strenuous eating!
We have some speakers here to tell you all
About the way the Millions Club has toiled,
Winning the war with luncheons great and small,
Killing the Kaiser oft with throats well oiled.
Ho, waiter, thou most consummate of asses,
Pass round the wine, and, patriots, fill your glasses!
New Mister Cook will tell you of the ships
Built in Australia since the war began,
Making to England most successful trips,
And every man on board Australian.
Clean up your plates 1 Leave not a spot of dripping!
And drink a toast to Mister Hughes and Shipping!
And, now that we have lunched, respect the Crown
And sing “God save” with lowly heart and meek,
Praying that Germany will be put down,
Never to rise again our doom to seek.
Yes! beat her down with shot and shell and trun-
cheon,
And, if that fails, we’ll have another luncheon!

“Dale” : Billjim isn’t hard to handle when
you know him. One troopship which arrived
here recently started out with 30 prisoners in
the clink. The cell, which was built on deck,
was a flimsy affair, and when the vessel was
leaving England the team leaned against one
of the walls and it fell out. When they had
seen all they wanted to, 29 of the “prisoners”
returned and put the wall into some sort of
order, with themselves inside. The other
formed himself into a deputation and waited
upon the C.O. asking for the release of his
mates. The Old Boy was . a sport, and, on
hearing the details, announced that if £2 was
forthcoming to pay for the damage the
prisoners would be free for the rest of the
voyage, so long as they behaved themselves.
There was no trouble about the £ s. d.—nor
with the nine-and-twenty birds thereafter.
» * * *
The East-West railway has hatched a bright
new idea in connection with long-distance
train travelling. The train that leaves Kal-
goorlie on Saturday at noon or thereabouts
doesn’t arrive at civilisation until Monday
morning. It has to drag along enough food for
all aboard for the whole journey; but experi-
ence shows that on some trips half of them
come in for one meal a day, and picnic on
hampers for the rest of the time. As a result
those who do come have to be charged high
rates, and that, again, doesn’t tend to im-
prove the attendance. So the East-West
has decided to do what any steamer co.
does as a matter of course, and include
meals in the price of the ticket. Fares are
to be raised on the basis of half a crown per
meal all through, with a one-third reduction
for children.
“F.T.” : A beautiful thing in the way of
Win-the-War economy happened the other
day in our Melbourne suburb. The name-
piece was missing from a corner post, and
the council had been asked to replace it.
So the street, two miles or more from the
municipal depot, was visited by a party of
three employees one fine sunny morning when
it was-a pleasure to be out. They drove up
conveying the new crosspiece, with a liam-,
mer and four nails. It wasn’t necessary to
bring a ladder. Tom just drove up on the
sidewalk against the post, Dick the offsider
handed over the board, hammer and nails to
Harry the carpenter, who did the tacking-up.
Then they all drove off again to lI.Q. The
vehicle was the corporation’s steam-roller!

“Tenansix” : Little can be expected from
the side-trabking commission which has been
sooled on to settle the dispute between the
doctors and friendly societies of Victoria ;
but it is possible that the conference which
sat under Judge Moule may be resurrected
shortly. Both sides are getting that tired
feeling. The societies are finding that the
supply of non-union doctors has just about
run out, while funds are being depleted in
the effort to pay sick members’ half-guineas.
In some quarters matters are right enough, if
not exactly, rosy ; but lots of lodges find the
outlook extremely blue. The doctors, too,
have forebodings of their own. The war may
not last forever. At the finish there will be
quite a flock of young, war-experienced mods,
drifting back to complicate the position and
give the lodge-member a new attack of that
argumentative feeling; and there is always
the prospect that the financially weaker
lodges may presently fall to pieces and be
taken to the free public hospitals, with con-
sequent loss of trade to the medical profesh.
A few quiet talks may fix matters, once the
parties get within earshot of one another
again.
“E..T.M.” : The first decent drink I had in
France was the issue-rum in front-line
trenches after our successful counter-attack
at on April 15. In the dismal
dawn, after we had been given the
order to “Stand to,” a lieutenant,
who has since lost an arm, strolled
across from another trench with a
stone jar under his wing. There is
a curious lull after a big fight, and
no attempt was made to snipe this
young officer as he came over and
sat down near a dead young Fritz
at the entrance to our cold,
wet and miserable home. We
enjoyed a good stiff nip all round,
and there was a peg over, which
was awarded to me as the oldest
ruffian in the trench. But a man
cnn’t counter-attack every time he wants a
drink.
“D.”: On April 25, 1915, the Anzacs
splashed ashore at Gallipoli and took coffee
with the Turk. On April 25, 1918, some of
the surviving Sydney Anzacs who took part
in the memorial march through the city
cheerfully accepted the invitation of a patri-
otic hostess to light refreshments—and found
themselves in a restaurant kept by a Turk !
If it was a joke it was a grim one.

The explanation of the last lost mailbag is
so simple that almost anybody might have
thought of it before. The bags were placed
in the van at Sydney. Then the guard “had
to leave the van and walk down the plat-
form in the exercise of his duties.” Appar-
ently he always has to do that. When he
came back from exercising his duties the bag
was missing, which had often happened
before. It went because somebody wanted it,
and nobody was there to prevent anybody
taking it. If it can’t be arranged that the
guard won’t exercise his duties out of sight
of the mailbags they will continue to go,, just
as a bank’s reserve fund might go if left
alone on the doorstep. Perhaps the guard’s
duties might be brought to him so that he
could exercise them in the van. Or Webster
might travel with the mails himself. Or a
decoy bag full of snakes might teach a
lesson.

At last the famous Commonwealth Egg
Police have found a case. After months of
vigilant searching for hidden stores of ex-
plosive fruit, they have run a culprit down.
It was the quiet of Townsville that was dis-
turbed by one member of the C.E.P. wanting
to arrest another member for assault. After
much violent resistance and destruction of
uniforms they both succeeded in running
each other in. Next morning a very puzzled
P.M. decided that they were both complain-
ant and defendant alternately, that the ques-
tion was a political one, and they had better
refer it to the swollen-headed little man who
made the farce possible.

When Private Smike gets to London: —
Far away to the east a silvery flash leapt across
the housetops, bringing the gables and chimneypots
into sharp relief. I had put it down to summer
lightning, when I heard a sound like the invitation
of a champagne cork. But the dainty popping
quickly swelled to a fusillade, taking a rapidly
deepening note. Now the searchlights were on, just
skimming the starlit sky with a thin vapor of light
—not much use, I thought, to pick out an object in
that maze of shimmering points. I could easily
mark the advance of the enemy ’planes, step by step,
constellation by constellation —the fingers of the
searchlights following them, feeling, feeling among
the stars. Single or double rays, some transparent
or entirely vague, crossed and recrossed, faded anil
glowed and doubled one upon the other, performing a
strange aerial death-dance high up in the heavens.
In a moment or two. a brilliant spark glowed
suddenly far overhead, and one and all the search-
lights concentrated their rays on the zenith, while
the whole sky was lit by the red sparks of bursting
shells. For a moment there was absolute silence;
then there stole down the faint drone of a circling
’plane, and from somewhere near at hand came a
shattering roar, followed at once by the same
ghastly quiet. And that was all I heard of the
raiders, for our own guns were now drowning every
oilier night-sound with the fury of their barrage.

THE FEAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the raid upon Zeebrugge the Germans almost
with one voice shouted: “It’s the Americans. It s
tli; Yankees !” —Daily paper.
Up through the darkness of the night they came,
Grey ships, grey men —the mists were torn with flame.
And, in the drawing of a sudden breath,
The Teuton grappled hand at throat with death.
They cried; “It is America at last!”
And, as they cried, the tumult raged and past,
The flames died down, a silence held the shore,
And Zeebrugge cowered behind a close-barred door.
Her harbor throat was choked in that swift raid
Where headlong valor valor’s price had paid.
Yet it was not America that spoke
Through the black night in flame and battle-smoke,
But Britain, ancient mistress of the deep,
She with the keys of all the waves to keep -
Britain sore-wounded, but undaunted still,
Whose arm is steadfast as her changeless will.
Soon other guns shall bring their word of fire
To call kind peace to match a world’s desire.
Tremble behind those misty flats and bleak,
For soon America shall also speak !
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.

SIC TRANSIT GLORIA MUNDI
THE Viking who rilled the ocean waves
Made all the pretty girls his slaves.
He stole them from near, he sneaked
them from far;
He won ’em in peace, he grabbed 'em in war.
Oh, he was a whale for pretty slaves,
The Viking who ruled the ocean waves!
N.S.W.
Hut when all the slaves desired new hats
They gave his old Nibs, the Viking, rats;
They nagged by night and they worried by
day
Till his hair went white and his wits astray;
And he knew himself the slave of slaves,
That Viking who ruled the ocean waves.

There is many a dinkum Anzac mourning
gallant David McFie MeConaghy, who, leav-
ing as a lieutenant with the Third Battalion,
was acting as its commander before the
Evacuation, and on April 9, while still under
30, died of wounds inflicted by a bomb near
Amiens. He was twice wounded —once at
Lone Pine —and the D.S.O. in France fol-
lowed his Anzac C.M.G. For a time he held
a brigade command, and was more than once
mentioned in Haig’s despatches.
Rupert, son of J. J. Sainsbury, of Dulwich
Hill (N.S.W.). has been a busy young Aus-
tralian since 1915. He was then 19 and a
smart wireless hand. Enlisting, he was given
24 hours to get on to Melbourne and join a
party of 14 operators going they didn’t know
where. They found themselves in Mesopo-
tamia, Sainsbury being attached to the artil-
lery, and later to the cavalry. He was at
the taking of Kut-el-Amara and Bagdad and
was in other shows. At the end of 1917 he
volunteered for service further afield, and
was attached with three others to the Rus-
sian cavalry serving in Persia; but at latest
he was back in Mespot. He’s a Tech. High
School boy.
Buried at Williamstown (Vic), C. W.
Harrison, of Windsor—-one of the Sydney’s
boys who licked the Emden.
Heinrich Markwald, of Car-
lisle Mansions, was sumnjoned
in London the other week on
the charge of being an alien who
hadn’t registered as such. The
defence of Heinrich was that
“he was horn in Berlin, of Ger-
man parents, in 1859, and for
many years carried on business
in Australia. In Melbourne, in
1908, he obtained a certificate
of naturalisation, and thereby
became a British subject. Con-
sequently he had refused to
register.” The magistrate found
that the Melbourne naturalisa-
tion didn’t hold good in Britain,
and fined him £5 and costs. But
there is no sympathy coming to
Markwald from Australia. On
his own showing, he didn’t go
through the process of fraud or
whatever it is until 1908, by
which time a German law had been passed to
enable adult sons of the Fatherland to re-
tain their legal connection with Kultur
whilst professing to naturalise themselves
in a foreign country.
The Commonwealth Government lias given
Frank Gellatly, financial editor of the S. M.
Herald, a job as chairman of its new
Bureau of Science and Industry. Gellatly
hasn’t any science to speak of. but he can
contribute the industry, and as he is still in
the forties he will probably be able to do it
for many years. As a reporter on the
Herald he swatted finance; and then he
swatted law and took the rare LL.D. degree
at Sydney Uni. He will probably make a
bi tter king-pin of the new organisation than
a scientist: for the scientist rarely knows
how to make the best commercial use of
himself. It might have paid Australia better
to offer a man like G. I). Delprat, or C. F.
Courtney, of the Sulphide Corporation,
£lO.OOO a year; but even Courtney or
Delprat couldn’t take tricks without cards,
and the trump in this case is a tariff 12
storeys high.
Extract from a cobber’s letter from
France:—
Poor old Courtney loved his bullocks better than
women. He was shot last week. I was with him at
the end. Before he died he started up, cracked an
imaginary bullock-whip and cried in an emotional
voice, that touched us more than anything: “Brindle!
Captain! Spot! Steady, beauties, steady!” Then he
fell back and died like the real dinkum Australian
that he was.
Died while visiting Sydney the other day,
Frank Harney, brother of Ned, K.C., who
showed some brilliant form in the Federal
Parliament until ambition led him to Lon-
don, where lie hoped to shine among the
bigger luminaries of polities and law. He
has hardly been heardkof since. Frank was
a flourishing lawyer in Coolgardie during
the early boom, when Finnerty bossed the
local court. In later days he was among
the battlers in Perth, suffering from a
broken constitution brought about princi-
pally by quack anti-fat experiments with an
abnormal avoirdupois. His vast weight
of 22 stone once provoked Cobb and Co. into
charging the travelling lawyer for two seats
on a coach doing the Lawlers-road, but
Harney contested the matter in court and
won. A narrow gallery in the labyrinth of
Yallingup caves bears the title of “Harney’s
Pinch” because here the big man was jambed
during a visit and required some extricating
before they launched him on surface again.
Mrs. A. J. Thynne, who was a politician
when politics seldom wore petticoats, died in
Brisbane the other day. Her husband was
in tiie Mellwraith Government 30 years ago ;
and if the wife of the Minister for Justice
had had her way there wouldn’t have been
any need for a military and naval expedition
to Rabaul in 1914. But Downing-street
knew Germany better, and the Mellwraith
Government’s plans with regard to New
Guinea were turned down. Mrs. Thynne
was one of the early settlers in Brisbane :
she was born there in 1848, 11 years before
the State was given a Government of its
own.
SOMETHING TO GO ON WITH.
SHE : “How is it that you've never been to
the war?”
HE: “Well, I did intend to go with the
Pioneers, but there were too many boun-
ders among them.”
SHE ( acidly ): “That needn’t have stopped
you. There’s always room for one
more, you know.”
Denny, Labor M.L.A. for Adelaide, re-
cently re-elected during his absence on
active service, is making a good recovery
from his wounds, and has been given leave
to cross the Atlantic. He cables from N’
York that he is going to lecture on Aus-
tralia’s part in the war. It will take all
Denny’s eloquence to explain to the Ameri-
cans, now in dead earnest about the war,
why Australia twice said No.
John Small, dead at Kapunda (S.A.) at
88, had been a farmer for 60 years, but his
pet _ memories_ were of journalism in the
’fifties. He joined the Adelaide Register
the day it became a daily paper (January 1,
1850) and two years later, having failed to
strike anything on the Victorian gold-
fields, found himself on the Argus. Long
after he used to tell how he had
to do three inquests a day on an average.
“Everyone was mad and money was like
dirt. We had the scum of the world there
—and no police to speak of.”
This war has stretched the arm of coinci-
dence longer than ever. Not many weeks ago
two brothers named Maggs—Walter and
Thomas —were killed in France, and this is
how the long limb con-
trolled their lives.
They were left with-
out parents and
brought up in the
Printers’ Orphanage.
Both went to work
for the same firm, and
in time married on
Christmas Day, 1915,
two sisters. The two
couples lived in ad-
joining flats until the
following year, when
the brothers enlisted
together in London,
and went to the Front
together. Th e y
swapped compliments
with Fritz side by side
until last December,
when they went home
together on brie f
leave. On the 29th
they Returned to the
fighting zone, and on
January 19 they were
both blown out by the
same shell.
D. G. McDOUGALL.
M.A., BCL. LL.D.
Professor of Law,
Tas. University.
Irene Lowe, of Melbourne Uni., the first
Australian girl to become a bachelor of
agricultural science, is a strenuous young
thing of 22, whose father has been store-
keeping these many years at Cassilis, up on
the roof of Gippsland. Until the time is
ripe for her to take on a farm of her own
she is demonstrator at the Bacteriological
School.
Eighty-seven years on April 30 since Capt.
Collet Barker, who discovered the Adelaide
Plains and was the first white man to ascend
Mount Lofty, was murdered by blacks at
the Murray mouth. A memorial tablet is in
old St. James’s, Sydney.
“Iron Pot” introduces his friend :
C. M. Dj'er, inventor of the petrol-saving device
which has just been adopted by the British Army
after an open-world competition, is a longish, lean,
slow-speaking Tasmanian, with a speculative eye
and a faint stammer. Before leaving the Apple
Isle to become manager of Dalgety’s motor busi-
ness in Melbourne, he had a varied career. His
first public appearance was as a racing cyclist, in
which capacity he was a tough customer, but not of
the first flight. Then smallpox smote Launceston,
and after a series of fierce feuds between- the
afflicted city and the unspeakable Central Board
of Health in Hobart, a specialist in public health
was imported from the mainland to deal with the
disease so that it would not interfere witli the
local squabbles. Dyer became chief clerk, and
received considerable praise for his work when the
outbreak was quelled. Various mechanical inven-
tions occupied his spare time for the . next few
years, with interludes of commercial touring on a
motor-bike at a most un-Tasmanian rate of speed.
One of his journeys earned him the dislike of the
Railway panjandrums of the day. The far-seeing
Dyer, having sighted a business opponent leaving
Launceston for Hobart by the morning express,
wheeled out his buzz-bike, beat the train by a matter
of two hours, cleaned up all the business in sight
and Was on tiie station to meet his astonished and
disgusted competitor. There is nothing at all
slow about C. M. Dyer, and if his petrol-saver is
even half as good as the cables sav, it will put a
new complexion on the internal-combustion engine.
GOING OVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“One minute more!” I whispered it and felt
The quick fear clutch my brain and pass. I smelt
The mingled smokes ; and in an instant all
The parapets of dead rose up to fall.
(One! Two! Three! Four!) Each counted second
died ;
And one scared mate crept nearer to my side
With superstitious dread of eyes too blue
And lips that looked as if their hour were due.
(Twelve! Thirteen! Fourteen!) Leaden was each
face,
Calm, strangely holy, in that fearful place,
So close to Death. Soft curses bit the blaze
Of inner courage. (Thirty-five!) Old days,
White thoughts of home, within our breathing blent
An instant (Forty! Forty-one!) and went.
I turned to cheer the kid who muttered “Mother,”
And all liis boyish terror still and smother.
(Fifty!) I started. (Fifty-one!) I itched
To fire —to leap the unshelled wire high-pitched
Between us (Fifty-three!) and break their line.
Feet moved mechanically. (Fifty-nine!)
Eye shot a swift farewell to eye. Heads stirred.
(Sixty!) A rifle shrieked the first red word.
N.S.W.
V. C.

The WILD CAT COLUMN
These Prophets of Pleasant Things who
remark blandly about how well the pros-
perity of this country is holding up despite
the war are a weariness to the flesh, and
also to the fowl and the fish. Here, for in-
stance, is a very big feature of the case
which is seldom spoken of (it is almost the
only feature, in fact) :
Commonwealth Notes
Issued, less Gold
Reserve. Increase.
December, 1912 .. £5,546,936
„ 1913 .. 5,466,969
„ 1914 .. 11,843,891 £6,376,922
„ 1915 .. 26,800,806 14,956,915
„ 1916 . . 30,059,851 3,259,045
„ 1917 .. 30,359,793 299,942
This is absolutely legitimate business. The
gold reserve against the note issue was more
per-cent, at December, 1917, than it was in
1915 or 1916. The margin of safety was
larger. But it isn’t necessary to make a
drunken boom out of the business. There
exists in the Commonwealth to-day, over
and above the gold reserve, £24,892,824 more
paper money than in the year before the
war started. This is of no use for paying
any of our vast liabilities outside Australia,
for it doesn’t circulate there; but it is a
most useful thing for dealings inside Aus-
tralia. It doesn’t, prove that we are bearing
up in any special way against the war; the
same amount of paper could have been issued
-if there had been no war, or if there had been
six wars, or if we hadn’t been bearing up.
There is nothing to make a boom over: it
is only our own lOU issued by ourselves to
ourselves as a matter of convenience, and
it isn’t wealth—it is only a matter of con-
venience. The increase has been of late at
the rate of over £6,100,000 per annum, but
the rise isn’t going on at the old rate because
the community has about as much paper as
it requires for the moment. Therefore the
“prosperity” isn’t likely to grow at the old
rate. Nobody but sanguine fools called it
“prosperity” or expected it to grow indefi-
nitely. This isn’t pessimism. It is merely
an invitation to the public to keep its head
and beware of flapdoodle. Flapdoodle is a
curse.
* * * *

The improvement in the foregoing figures
would be incredible without this comparison :

And they would be incredible, of course, with
normal conditions. When war broke out the
Commonwealth Government began to talk in
millions where formerly it had prattled in
hundreds ; and on top of direct war expen-
diture fell very large trade transactions
through- the commandeering of Australian
products' for Imperial purposes, Denison
Miller, who had been appointed governor
of this bank, missed no chances. Certainly
he didn’t grab everything; the private
banks were invited or required, whichever you
like, to take up some of the work, and share
the profits, in connection with exchanges and
loans. But this bank is the Government’s
bank, and has stood where it could get a
rake-off from practically all the Common-
wealth’s big financial operations.
Of course, it won’t last; but meanwhile
the institution is building up a solid reserve
which, if all goes well, will enable it to face
the changed conditions with confidence. Pre-
cisely what the nature of its business in
future will be is not yet clear; but as it is
more or less certain that in the days to come
there will be considerable intimacy between
the Government and the producing interests,
with an extension of the State’s interest in
trade and manufacture, the opportunities for
the bank to serve the public will be vastly
greater than was expected a few years ago.
Even the rich pastures on which it is at pre-
sent browsing would last a good while if the
war ended at once. Already a million of
reserves (which are the bank’s capital) are
in sight, and another two or three years of
war would bring two millions on the horizon.
But there would be nothing in that to make
Denison Miller very cheerful. From the
banking point of view, as from any other, the
sooner the disturbance is over the better, and
the more reality will there be about every-
thing, including the safety of the bank and
the profits; for, after all, the profits which
this bank and the rest of us have made are
only profits provided that things go right.

This co. has three departments, which have
contributed to the past year’s figures in this
way:—
Income. Expenses. Claims.
Ordinary Life .. ..£31,708 £17,306 £4,268
Industrial and Provident .. 16,036 9,010 6,509
Accident .. .. .. 1,554 909 653
During the year the obligations were valued
by the co.’s consulting actuary, who reported
that as at December 31, 1916, the net lia-
bility to policy-holders was as follows: —
Ordinary department, £2r>,182 ; Industrial
and Provident, £11,705; total £36,887. A
year ago the co. issued a balance-sheet show-
ing that, as at December 31, 1916, the assets
(apart from £7OOO paid as goodwill for
the National Provident business) were
£37,343. Thus at that date, though the
shareholders had put up £44,000, there was
only just, enough to meet the obligations
under the policies. In other words, the
whole of the capital had gone in establish-
ing the business and in purchasing the
Provident connection. To what extent the
money thus spent is an asset The Bulletin
is not prepared to say. The actuary makes
out his case as follows: —
It is quite evident that any new life co. starting
out to build up a business must spend in its early
years more than the amounts provided in the pre-
miums for expenses. Capital is. subscribed and used
for this purpose, and the sum so spent is in evdry
sense a capital expenditure. The co. has during the
period under review written off all expenses of this
nature in connection with the establishment and
organisation of the co. against revenue. But I do
not consider this should have been done so early. I
have therefore advised that Establishment and
Organisation accounts should be set up in the books
as follows: In the Ordinary department, £16,495 ;
in the Industrial and Provident department £21,542.
This has been done.
But it won’t put a penny back in the till.
The money has gone. Whether it will be
recovered in the future not even an actuary
can say. As a matter of theory, however,
this paper quite agrees with the actuary that
the outlay—some of it, at any rate —has been
capital expenditure. As capital guarantees
the policies, the policy-holder is entitled to
observe that all the funds necessary to meet
the co.’s obligations are there, waiting for
him. The shareholder has to observe, on
the other hand, that his money is for the
present gone, and that the best he can hope
for is that as time goes on it will be recov-
ered.
It probably will be. Money isn’t going
out now like it necessarily had to go out at
the beginning. In the first 17 months, though
the claims were iiaturally small, the total
outgoings were £48,792, while the total in-
come was only £34,840: in the last two
years, while the total outgoings were
£68,967, the total income was £89,470.
Whether the income in excess of the out-
goings—over £lO,OOO last year—was equal
to the year’s increase in policy liabilities
The Bulletin doesn’t kfiow; probably it
wasn’t, because the expense rate in the
Ordinary department is still very heavy—ss
p.c. of the total income, whereas the actuary
in making his calculations finds that “the
proportion of office premiums reserved for
future expenses.... on the whole works out
at about 21.7 p.c.” Still the movement is
in the right direction, although the business
will have to be got at a much cheaper rate,
and when got will have to be managed at
a cheaper rate, if the shareholders are to get
anything out of the business in dividends.
The position of these infant offices is ex-
tremely difficult—so extremely difficult that
it is doubtful whether we shall see another
purely life office established for very many
years to come. The public when it is taking
out a policy naturally wants security above
all things. It has to be assured not merely
that its insurance office has enough funds to
pay its policies to-day, but that it will have
enough in 20 or perhaps 40 years.* With
old and proved offices in the held the task of
the canvasser for the new office must be
almost heart-breaking; and the task of the
office to keep the business after the first
premium or two have been paid must be
nearly as bad. And when a war falls on
top of the normal difficulties, this paper can
only offer everybody concerned its sympathy.
For the greater part they must rely upon
young or comparatively young men for their
probable clients. Most of the older ones are
more or less attached to older offices. Hence
the young office suffers excessively from
claims on account of a war : the directors of
this co. say that in the Ordinary department
last year over 82 p.c. of the amount paid
out to meet claims was on account of deaths
on active service.

But it is still not as good as it looks. The
reserves (apart from provision against doubt-
ful debts, etc.) amount to £14,747 less £1971
for the purchase of the Reinsurance Co.’s
goodwill. Now, the net premium income for
last year was £144,917; and assuming that 40
p.c. of the year’s premiums should be in hand
to cover unexpired risks, then the co. ought to
have an unearned premium reserve of no less
than £58,000. In other words, the co. has
thus far reckoned as income about £58,000
which really isn’t income at all, seeing
that it has to meet the losses on all
the co.’s risks for an average of pro-
bably six months. Last year there was an
increase of 'premium income of nearly
£30,000; which probably means that about.
£12,000 of it was unearned as at December
31, when the accounts closed. Hence pro-
bably not one farthing of the £7202 of the
amount nominally earned in excess of the
dividend last year was true profit; all of
it and more was required for the unearned
premium reserve, which the co. has not yet
created. Twelve months ago it seemed to this
paper that the directors should not have paid
alO p.c. dividend. It now seems still more
certain that the 10 p.c. was premature.
This, curiously enough, is so not because
the co. has done poorly, but because it has
made such remarkably rapid progress. Look
at these figures
Net Loss Expense
Premiums. Losses. Expenses. Rate. Rate.

In five years the co. has trebled its business.
It has, to mention another highly-respectable
office for purposes of comparison, more than
double the premium income of the Australian
Mutual, which has been in business 45 years.
Of course, it has cost money to get-these
figures. That cost has been wholly charged
against revenue; in other words,' the co.,
besides having the reserves mentioned in the
first table, has all its capital (£96,291) in-
tact. The policy-holder is therefore reason-
ably well protected notwithstanding that pre-
miums (and therefore risks) have increased
so fast. Capital and reserves combined total
£109,067 = about 75 p.c. of the year’s pre-
miums. In the case of the Australian
Mutual, quoted just now, capital and reserves
total £215,472, which is equal to no less than
336 p.c. of its year’s premiums?. That, how-
ever, is a position of very exceptional
strength. When an insurance office has funds
(capital and reserves) equal to 150 p.c. of
its annual premium income it is generally
reckoned to offer quite satisfactory security
to its policy-holders without making its own
shareholders feel uncomfortable. From all of
which it is fairly evident that by reason of
the very rapid increase of premiums (which
mean risks) this co. Ought to go slow in the
matter of dividends and build up its reserves
as fast as possible. Simultaneously it might
not be amiss if it combed out some of its
risks with a view to reducing its loss rate,
which is at present high.

Elder-Smith’s are probably the best-known
people in S. Australia. The firm was estab-
lished in 183!) —within a couple of years of
the foundation of the “Province.” The
original, by the way, wasn’t Thomas Elder,
as most modern S. Australians seem to
think, but A. L. Elder, brother of Thomas.
A.L. returned to London 65 years ago and
established himself there; Thomas then
came to Australia, and became the head.
Robert Barr Smith, who had been for some
years in Melbourne with Hamilton, Smith
and Co., crossed over to Adelaide in the
following year and took George Elder’s place
in the firm, which became Elder, Smith and
Co. The Elders have long since gone ; but
R. Barr Smith remains, and is part of the
pride of S. Australia. Between the partners
individually and the firm, afterwards turned
into a public co., they did great things for
the development of their country: they took
a leading part in the opening up of the
Moonta copper-mines; they equipped a num-
ber of exploring expeditions; they introduced
camels to backblocks Australia. Thousands
of S. Australians, growing up with the name
on their lips, passed their stock and station
and farm business, as a matter of course, to
this co. A few years ago the co. reckoned
that it might as well provide all the machin-
ery for doing the business for these people,
after their death as well as while they lived;
and this trustee co. was the result. That it
was wanted is proved by the fact that the
value of the trust funds in hand has moved
along in this remarkable way:—
1911. . £53,562 | 1917.. £2,460,347
1915.. 1,405,146 | 1918.. 2,779,039
So for a trustee co. it has the unusual experi-
ence of having paid an 8 p.c. div. in its
seventh year; and the 8 p.c. last year con-
sumed barely half of the profits. Of course
the capital is small; but clients are further
protected by the shareholders’ liability for
£BO,OOO of calls. And they are a very solid
lot. Still it isn’t merely because this and
other similar cos. put up money guarantees
of good behavior that they ought to command
the attention of all persons with property to
leave behind them. In particular (1) the
trustee co. makes a. business of being a
trustee. It doesn’t have to go to a lawyer
before it moves. It therefore does things
expertly for less, as a rule, than the amateur
trustee does things amateurishly. (2) It
doesn’t die. Anybody who has had experi-
ence of a private trustee dying in the midst
of liquidating an estate knows what that
means.

This society wrote down its capital by one-
half in order to meet the market depreci-
ation of assets not otherwise provided for.
It used the pruning-shears rather too
severely, with two results: (1) it was able
to presently show substantial surpluses on
sales; (2) the amount called for by the
dividend was comparatively small. Thus
reserves have grown nicely; and. though
five or six years ago £9560 was handed out
as a bonus, the total is still equal to nearly
two-thirds of the capital. It is this which
makes it possible for the co. to pay a 10
p.c. div., notwithstanding that it has the
use of only £31,142 of the public’s money.

has got out of the old bog:—

This co. had a gruelling time in the early
’nineties, and 10 years ago the share capi-
tal was only £135,352: it had been written
down with an axe. The remarkable capacity
of Australia and Maoriland for getting out
of a tight place then came to the co.’s res-
cue; and capital which looked as though it
were lost beyond redemption was recovered
by the hundred thousand. To-day the share
capital is no less than £1,823,514. the in-
crease being due partly to transferred re-
serves, partly to the conversion of debenture
stock.
Doubtless the co.’s bitter experience in
past years is responsible for the most
remarkable feature of its history since the last
reconstruction. Rather more than half the
present share capital is 5 p.c. preference;
the balance is ordinary. But the dividend
on ordinary shares has been kept so very
modest that in five years the visible reserves
have been strengthened from £257,250 to
£636,135, and in the same time £130,000 of
profit has been written off in reduction
mainly of a premises account, which had
already been written down severely. Thus
in five years the profit, including surpluses
from the liquidation of the N.Z. Land Asso-
ciation, a co. which this one used to own,
has been £766,135 in excess of the dividends;
and meantime a very substantial sum —
apparently £30,000 —has been handed over
to an officers’ provident fund, which the
directors hope to make the basis of a pen-
sion fund presently. Meantime also tax-
ation has simply bounded forward. Ten
years ago the outgoings under this head were
£8845; last year they were £86,122. That
the co. can show such very fine profits in
these circumstances is due partly to the fact
that it lias the use of £2,000,000 of 4 p.c.
debenture money ; partly also, of course, to
the war-prices realised for wool and other
products which the co. handles. The co.
itself, though at one time a very large
station-holder, now produces little on its
own account; but its commissions and other
earnings have increased with the price of
products. In 1907 —but take this compari-
son first: —
Liabilities.
1907. 1916. 1917.
Debentures . . £3,296,344 £2,000,000 £2,000,000
Other debts .. 837,799 586,786 614,149

Notwithstanding that the balance-sheet fig-
ures in 10 years have increased by less than
the amount of the undivided profit, the
gross earnings have increased from £839,417
to £617,079. That the figures of the last
few years are abnormal the directors seem
to fully realise; hepce their close rein upon
the dividend. The business of pastoral
finance is, as they know, an uncertain one;
but, given good management, it is only dis-
astrous if the co. has to realise in an adverse
market—which means if its own creditors
press it. This co.’s debts apart from deben-
tures are so small that it is exceptionally
well situated in that respect.
The co. is one of those that are hit by the
double income tax. Shareholders are mostly
in England; the properties are at this end.
and the Australian and M.L. taxes last
year called for £52,860 of the total of
£86,122 paid out. But that was not the
aggregate of the taxes payable ; for the divi-
dends are paid less income tax. In other
words, portion of the British income tax is
paid out of the dividends. And at this
point an interesting little difficulty has
arisen. The British income tax on this co.’s
dividends last year was ss. in the £; but
the Finance Act of 1916 gives the co. relief
to the extent of Is. 6d. in the £ in respect
of income tax on profits earned in Aus-
tralia or Maoriland. But the question has
arisen: Who gets the eighteenpence? The
♦ co. or the shareholders? And if the latter,
what shareholders? A pref. shareholder
in an English co. carrying on business in
England pays ss. in the £. But if the co.
carries on business in Australia, he would
pay only 3s. 6d. if he were entitled to the
rebate. Certainly the co. has paid the Is. 6d.
in Australia; but the money has come out
of the general revenue, and the pref. share-
holder has only a limited interest in the
general revenue and the profits. At present,
though there is a diversity of opinion, the
bulk of it appears td be that the co. is en-
titled to the Is. 6d., and if the shareholders
are to benefit by it it can only be by cutting
it 'up in the shape of a bonus or extra divi-
dend to the ordinaries. The question is,
however, to go to the Court of Appeal;
though it is not being taken by this co.
* * * *
“Profiteer”: Things are not looking any
more cheerful for 4 Australian insurance
offices. Following the blow they received in
Queensland, the Victorian Government In-
surance Department is causing them
trouble. Having accumulated profits that it
didn’t think it needed, it recently decided to
give its policy-holders the benefit, aud distri-
buted a cash bonus. Employers began to sit
up and take notice (the Government omce
only does business under the Compensation
Act), and the cos. counter-attacked by cir-
cularising their clients, offering a quarter of
their last year’s premium back provided they
renewed their policies. The Government
officials retaliated by publishing a compari-
son of both benefits on a policy of similar
size, which didn’t make the offer of the cos.
look dazzling. The latest development
is the issue by the cos. of a revised lot
of premium rates undercutting the Govern-
ment rates. An Autocrat of the Kitchen
formerly cost ss. per annum for insurance.
Now she costs only 45., not counting the
bonus or rebate, which was Is. 3d. for last
year. It is very interesting for the policy-
holders; but it is going to hurt somebody in
the dividends —especially if it doesn’t stop at
workmen’s compensation.

The story told in London, and reported in
The Times, was that “the real cause of the
sharp advance in the shares of the Broken
Hill Prop, to the highest level recorded for
years past is that the Commonwealth
Government is arranging to acquire the eo.’s
steel-works in connection with its shipbuild-
ing programme.” The market is being affected
rather too often by tales of things, the Com-
monwealth Government is going to do or not
going to do.
* * * *
This paper would be very glad to discuss
the sale of Australian zinc * concentrates
which is supposed to have been made by the
Commonwealth Government to the British
Government. It is not permitted to say why
it does not discuss the sale or alleged sale.
But it will say this: that when announce-
ments are made which have the effect of
booming already over-boomed shares, and
when steps are thereupon taken by someone
to prevent the discussion of the business, the
public is entitled to ah explanation. Failing
an explanation, the public is entitled to its
suspicions.
Kingsgate (N.S.W.) has its mill at work,
and bismuth and molybdenite concentrates
are being bagged for market. During a
run of 308 hours the mill treated 101 tons
crudes for 38041 b. bismuth concentrates and
12971 b. molybdenite concentrates. A pound
of molybdenite 90 p.c. pure is worth about
4s. 3d. (at £4BO per ton). Assuming this
parcel assayed 96 p.c., the gross value would
be £275. The value of the bismuth would
probably exceed that of the molybdenite.
Kingsgate is 17 miles from Glen Innes, and
last year produced 104 tons of molybdenite
valued at £4876.
Melbourne-owned Y Water (N.S.W.),
after muddling along in ground as tough as
alabaster, is sending its plant to hunt for an
easier patch up the lead. If the co. hadn’t seen
black tin run uo to £264, the past half-year’s
1054 tons oxide from 350.000 cub. yards of
drift might have about ended things. As it
is, the co. made £2776 on working account
and netted £l6OO after settling its interest
bill and feeing its city panjandrums. To its
big creditor, the bank, it owed £15,959 on
January 31, and smaller chaps £2309. These
amounts are about covered by tin (£11,420)
and stores (£7472). The rest is in plant
and the softer ground ahead. Owing to high
costs and tough wash F. G. Wilson intends
to discard 54 acres previously marked as
payable.
* * * *
There are still some tin-mines that don’t
pay dividends: probably they are waiting
for the metal to get up to £5OO. Hart’s, at
Torrington (N.S.W.), owned by a Melbourne
co., had been in the hands of tributors_ for a
couple of years prior to the end of 1917, and
they didn’t do what the co. hadn’t been able
to do for itself —pay the vendor £5OO out of
the first profits. On January 1 the co. took
over the mine and is now crushing a bit of
ore; but there is very little developed. Net
profit last year (to February 28.1918) was
£414, which reduced the P. and L. debit to
£4002.
* * * *
\Y estraliaix State Mining-Engineer Mont-
gomery’s report on the water trouble at Wes-
tonia is out at last. Montgomery reckons (1)
that the flooded area is limited: (2) that it
will not extend very far in depth : (3) that the
immense quantity of water that is being
pumped out of the mines is not, as some
local experts allege, finding its way back
again by soakage, and (4) that the one
thing needful to keep the flow in check is
the establishment of a central pumping
station. If the four cos. immediately con-
cerned don’t settle their differences and
erect one the Government will probably step
in and compel them to do so. Mick Wil-
liams, who bosses the principal sufferer, the
Edna May, put the position at that mine
the other day. lie said the pumping capa-
city at the 385 ft. level was very limited, and
when stoppages occurred the water soon
rose in the shaft. A reserve plant for use in the
event of stoppages is to be installed at
300 ft., and the 385 ft. level is to have a
couple of centrifugal pumps, each of 00,000
gallons per hour. Pending the arrival and
erection of the first of these pumps the
water will be allowed to remain at the 300 ft,
level, and only a limited tonnage, drawn
from the upper levels, will be treated.
* * * *
Great Fingall (W.A.) has decided to give
it up. The last yield from the mine left
the tributing employees with only 7s. 6d. a
shift as a return for their labor, and to
enable the co. to pay the difference between
that amount and the rate of wages fixed by
the award, the Government has agreed to
give the co. further financial assistance.
Some time ago the directors got the Govern-
ment to guarantee them at the bank up to
£15,000 and the overdraft is now stated
to stand at about half that amount. It will
probably be wiped out altogether by the time
the co. realises on its assets. The only
way the Fingall could be further worked
is by sinking a new main underlay shaft
to a depth of at least 2000 ft.; but if the
co. had found the money to do this it would
have been heavily taxed on the outlay. There
is no taxation on money spent on driving,
crosscutting, winzing or rising done in the
course of development work ; but for main-
shaft sinking there is. This is another
sample of Hughes statesmanship. It would
be humorous if it did not have such tragic
results.
* x * * *
Two Warden Court cases of interest to
Eastern speculators were disposed of at Mt.
Magnet (W.A.) the other day. The first
was an application for 12 months’ exemption
on the Empress. The attorney stated that the
total expenditure to date had been £35,000;
the value of gold won £12,000. As there was
no opposition the co. got what it wanted.
In the second case three former owners suc-
ceeded in having the forfeiture of the
Sirdar > lease recommended. V. F. Shall-
cross, who appeared for the present holder,
the Star Syndicate, Ltd., of Melbourne and
Adelaide, did not offer any opposition, but
will probably appeal direct to the Minister.
This he has successfully done in other cases.
The latest about Langi Logan South.
Ararat (Vic.), is that the western ground
off Nos. 10, 11 and 12 rises has been very
uneven. Off No. 12 is a bonser crab-hole,
and though George Bryant talks of good
wash on the fringe of all these dugouts, the
fact that they extend for the length covered
by three rises means excluding a big block of
wash from No. 6 rise onwards. It needed a
Philadelphia lawyer to get this much out of the
weekly mine reports, but there it is at last.
Now the talk is that the co. is again into
regular country. Work done round Nos. 6
and 7 rises and in wash drives up the lead
resulted in 915 fathoms averaging over loz.
2dwt. In pre-war days that would have spelt
divs. and a big jump in the market. Now
there is no profit in such dirt, with the mine
only half-manned and wages sky-high. Thus
far Langi South has chewed up £90.000 of
good cash paid in as calls and £17,000 of
gold, and it has never paid a div.
* *• * *
Bendigo Amalgamated so far hasn’t picked
up trumps at Windmill Hill. The first 30
tons from the west leg was poor stuff, but as
most gold was to be seen in the east-leg
stone no one lost heart. When quartz thence
only panned out a 3dwt. average there was
a scatter. However, optimism over Windy
Hill brought in the call and enabled the co.
to move easily for another quarter or so.
But that won't calm down the shareholders
who got wild with the 1.0. A. Board for
underwriting 50,000 shares. However, the
last fortnight’s Amalgamated yield went an
improved 6dwt., due to things looking up at
the Carlisle, so the directors perhaps feel a
bit better about it.
* * * *
Charters Towers produced £10.852 for the
first quarter of this year: the first quarter of
1917 gave £25,249. and the first quarter of
191(5 £52,007. Except for the Papuan Co.'s-
sink on the Bramble reef hardly any pro-
speeding is being done in new ground: but
tributors are trying to pick up a payable
shoot in the old Queen Cross lease. Clark’s
mine continues to haul 100 tons of stone per
fortnight, and the stopes carry a reef Sin.
wide. It has been a good little show to pay
six dividends on 90,000 shares. The last
crushing averaged over £l3 per ton, but it
followed £23 per ton. These rapid changes
are characteristic of this rich little streak.
Kampong Kamunting, one of the tin shows
which the Austral Malay Co. introduced to
Australia, started with 10 years’ life. Now.
though it has been hard at work for a couple
of years and has already paid 13s. per share
in divs., it reckons it still has a bit over
10 years’ work in sight, acquired areas
alongside having more than counterbalanced
the 84 acres that have so far been worked
out.

HOW THIN PEOPLE MAY PUT ON
FLESH.
GREAT DISCOVERY BY EMINENT
SPECIALIST,
Judging from the countless preparations
and treatments which are continually being
advertised for the purpose of making thin
people fleshy, developing the arms, neck and
bust, and replacing ugly hollows and angles
by the soft curved lines of health and beauty,
there are evidently thousands of men and
women who keenly feel their excessive thin-
ness, and it therefore gives us real pleasure
to publish herewith a simple prescription
which, by correcting faulty metabolism and
stimulating the activity of certain sluggish
vital organs quickly produces a marked
transformation in the appearance; the in-
crease in weight frequently averaging from
three to four pounds the first week, and
very rarely less than two. This increase
in weight also carries with it a general im-
provement in the health. Nervousness, sleep-
lessness and debility, which nearly always
accompany excessive thinness, all quickly dis-
appear, dull eyes become bright and' pale
cheeks glow with the bloom of perfect
health.
The prescription, which is absolutely *
harmless, directs you to take before each
meal a 5-grain tablet of Bitro-phospliate, such
as you may obtain from any chemist, at a
cost of about 2d. a day. Eat all you
want, but chew your food thoroughly.
CAUTlON.—Although the above prescrip-
tion is unequalled for relieving nervousness,
sleeplessness and general debility, it should
not, owing to its recognised flesh-growing
properties, be used by anyone who does not
desire to put on flesh. —Advt.
