Sydney, April 29, 191 S.
.My Dear Arini, —
Anzac Day, brilliantly fine in the after-
noon, brought us a march of returned sol-
diers through the city. And there was a
laughing contingent that couldn’t march—
crippled boys who pushed their crutches into
cars and hopped in after. No whimpering
there! The khaki dots began to take shape
opposite the Gallery 7 at half-past two. The start
was timed for three; but it was later, and
when the tail of the procession, wearied with
the waiting, got into Macqjuarie-street, here
and there a war-worn soldier stumbled or
fell from exhaustion. The dismal line of
mourning coaches, filled with memorial
wreaths, was a shade too theatrical for
that spirited gathering of game Australian
soldiers who were making the best of their
damages. The Governor’s cocked hat was,
at the saluting point—a green patch oppo-
site the Australian Club. Here Consuls
and Parliamentary and civic deities had a
few blades of grass a-piece.
Over GOO soldiers passed in and out of
the Welcome Club (St. Andrew’s Cathedral
casts its shadow on the roof) on Anzac Day,
and great was the washing-up thereof. For
the Voluntary Workers, however, there’s joy
in paying even this tribute to Billjim. And
a similar rite at the Soldiers’ Club, decorated
in its party rooms with white and purple
blooms, kept Dr. Mary Booth’s girls up to
their elbows in hot water. The sensation of
the evening was the Belgian National
Anthem sung by a native of that heroic little
country, Miss Lintcrman.
If Old Australia had voluntarily provided
the Allies with as many flying-machines as
Young Australia has voluntarily provided
them with fliers, there would by now be 500
pairs of Australian wings in Europe, instead
of something well under 40. The A.I.F.
has sent 300 boys into the air service;
and a couple of hundred others, or there-
abouts, have gone into the R.F.C. In this
matter the .young men with their precious
lives to give have been far more generous
than the old men with little more than then-
purses to lose. For £2700 any citizen can
have a machine of his own laying eggs in
France 12 short weeks after he has written
the cheque; and Alma Baker continues to
mention that the Australian Air Squadrons
Fund is still taking subscriptions. There is
no limit, up or down, and any bank will
accept offerings on behalf of the lion,
treasurer, F. E. Bryant, manager of the
Union Bank in Sydney. Thus far, besides
contributing to “district” battleplanes, the
Fund has presented the War Office with 22.
The matinee at Her Majesty’s threw about
£2OOO into the Red Cross blanket. The
Davidsons, who had a strenuous day, got in
late. With Hugh Ward as guide, they felt
their way into the darkened theatre while
Marie Tempest was dabbing her nice little
nose and weeping carefully over a brand-new
marriage-license. They saw the rest of the
act from the back of the d.c.; then, with the
two A.D.C. soldier boys in the rear, they
slipped into their seats. In a programme
which explored every theatrical hole and
corner, the thrilling khaki display w r ith a
hundred of the genuine Australian article
got the heartiest cheers. The auction inter-
lude brought a £125 bid from John Hunter
for the white-satin flag with the Red Cross.
Flaxen-haired dolls changed hands several
times, and George Marlow, in a stage-box,
became the joyful owner of one at an exorbi-
tant price.
The crowd opposite a fashionable club
was thrilled when an affluent-looking indi-
vidual at a lower window began counting
an impressive roll of notes. In the street a
lorry-load of girls took a top G under the
shade of red paper hats. Here was a haul—
a fat wad of Commonwealth paper! But
Dives put the roll back into his pocket and
tossed two sixpences into the street. “Can ’e
spare it!” asked the shabby man with the
dog.
The War Chest’s “hidden treasures” drew
blood early. An unshaken desire to get a pearl
necklet for 12d. brought Woman crowding
to th,e scene. Whether it was quite worthy of
the 'Chest to put a small and pimply-green
lemon in a shilling “treasure” is not a vital
matter. The money goes to the right place,
and the prizes 'Weren't all pimply lemons.
Indeed, I know a girl who got half a packet
of pins.
The march of the Y.A.D.’s was the picture
of the day. White as milk, 2000 strong, the
voluntaries were preening their plumage near
the Art Gallery at early morning, with an
occasional yelp from a male in khaki or the
wail of an itinerant musician for diversion.
The country units were a strapping lot.
Those who expected the ladies from out West
to be lean and wilted were surprised at the
sturdy, bright-eyed girls who came from the
sunburnt plains. Killara's commandant,
Edie Fisher, got a special ’ooray for her lot
as they stepped out in approved military
style. Mrs. Chris Bennett, the Y.A.D.’s
State mother, hovered near her fluttering
flock of pigeons. After the march the Bed
Cross fed the country delegates in the Town
Hall, the Kitchen providing the baked meats.
And just there my next-door neighbor (sick
wife and three children) dropped in with
something he d signed ’‘Honorary Washer-
up” :—•

David Jones’s corner was a seething mass
or women trying to light their wav nearer to
the bargains. Not much short of £7OO was
the glittering wash-up of this stall, with Mrs.
Siutchbury and Mrs. «T. J. Cohen in.charge.
But Wyn.vard-square, where Mrs. Bennett
counted the notes, beat even that with £BlO.
At the Quay the Pickle Depot girls, with
Mrs. Jocelyne in charge, had sold out before
noon and hurried reinforcements were sent
for. Even before the Day had dawned this
little lot had gathered in about £2OO from
badge and button sales.
The Red Cross Book Depot girls—Mrs.
Galbraith, her head in flowing white drapery
with the healer’s cross in front, was a busy
woman the day—had several young beauties
to pass round the tea. “Cracked cups! But
someone must get the chipped crockery,” said
one charmer affably, and whisked off the
derelict with a smile. Farther up, ih Moore-
street. Miss Kate Egan and helpers poured
tea and coffee and washed-up till late at
night. _ Over £2OO exchanged for the day’s
thirst in that plot. Mrs. Brittain, with other
’Murkan ladies, coined money at the U.S.A.
st:all near by. The American-Australian
Dengue, another band of raiders, swooped
down on travellers in the Imperial Arcade.
Mrs. Plogsted mothered this lot. The Edith
Cavell Memorial stall, with Miss Davey and
Eileen Teece to count the change, had as
extra bran-pie assistants Diana and Daphne
Davidson. As there’s no mayoress on the
spot, "Mrs. It. D. Meagher camped in her
usual gunyah at the Town Hall, with the
Nesbitt girls to help.
China took a hand in the robbery. One
of the most charming efforts in Martin
Place, with Mrs. Chuey and the Yinson
Lees to help, brought in over £5OO. And
raffles will swell this total.
A shop window, however big, is a breath-
less cage after half an hour or so. and the
Red Cross demonstrators were frequently
changed during their folding-down and pack-
ing-up exhibition at Farmer’s. When Lady
Davidson had snipped the ribbons' that held
the window draping, the expectant public
glued its nose to the panes. The spinsters
at their wheels had the most spectacular
job. The toy-makers also caught the eye,
and the weavers got the respect of those
present. The dull but useful task of folding
shirts behind plate-glass went on till sunset.
After a tine morning a heavy rainstorm
made pulp of a few r perishable goods on Red
Cross Day. In the Macquarie-place “vil-
lage” a leaky tent was responsible for more
water than tea. With supreme foresight a
few hardy wights had dragged umbrellas
round town all day. These were now
opened, to the chastened joy of those who
got the drips down their necks. But Mrs.
Alfred Lee’s damsels mopped up the ruins,
the sun came out again and the impromptu
bridges were soon kicked aside.
St. George is an unobtrusive saint, nearly
forgotten this year. But Lethbridge King
took his usual seat as chief carver at the
George Society’s annual dinner. The com-
pany having drunk the healths of the Brit-
ish royalties, Justice Edmund Barton, Aus-
tialian-born, got on his feet to add another
glass to the account of St. George, England
and the Allies.
Jean Ross Munro, daughter of a well-
known Queensland squatting family, was
married to Lieut. Jack Chandler, M.C., of
the R.F.A., at St. Paul’s, in Brisbane. The
bride had three soldier brothers. One is with
the R.F.C., one was killed in the early days
of the war, and one is missing. The brides-
maids were Vera Cotton, the fiancee of the
aviator brother, and little sister Margaret.
The ’moon will shine as far as Honolulu,
tor Mrs. Chandler is accompanying her hus-
band so far on his return to England. He
came from Warwick, where in later days
Hughes’s hat broke an egg and hatched a
policeman.
Walter Marks, of the Royal Naval Volun-
teers, and a lieutenant with three years’
patrol-work and mine-sweeping behind him,
gave an interesting tale of the sea in war-
time. The Naval House was crowded for
the yarn, and Governor Davidson took the
chair. The Sydney Yachtsman—he’s Com-
modore of the R.P.A. Club—was on his way
with the racing Shamrock to England when
a wireless ordered them to the nearest port.
It happened to be Bermuda. With Lipton’s
famous yacht was the Erin, a smaller
pleasure craft. From Bermuda —this was
early in August, 1914—they went to New
York. Nothing doing. So Marks and the
crew of the Erin filled up with coal and made
a dash for England. Since when Walter
Marks has mostly lived amidst the spume of
the North Sea, half-frozen in many a bliz-
zard, and has been one of the lucky seadogs
to keep a whole skin. He’s sunning himself
in Sydney on a short furlough.
The J. 11. I*. Murrays’ only daughter
Mary, the slender, dark-eyed girl who left
here with mother for brother Terence’s wed-
ding in England, has had a similar party on
her own account. The man is a Victorian—
Captain C. R. Pinney, M.C. Mrs. Murray
stays on the other side, for it’s not easy to
get a passport, and, anyhow, she admits to
being “nervous” of the submarines. Papua’s
Lieut.-Gov., who usually comes this way in
winter, has one of his small brood left on
this side—Pat the schoolboy.

This week-end a
Victorian pianist,
Jessie Masson, gives
her first Sydney re-
cital in the King's
Hall, though we
heard her play last
year with the Phil
Society, and again
lately when Howard
Hadley performed.
Born in Melbourne.
Jessie grew up in
Maoriland till she
was old enough for
the Melbourne Con-
servatorium, over
which, in those days,
waved the mane of
Marshall Hall. After
that the wander-
lust. The Australian girl has had a peep at
the chief cities of Europe, and in Berlin
studied under a Dutch master—James
Ivwast, who once showed the way to Percy
Grainger. Though due to give a recital in
Berlin at the beginning of October, 1914,
the young pianist was dawdling in Paris
when the war broke loose. So she started
for home, spending some time in Canada and
U.S.A. on the way. Since then she's had a
tour through Maoriland. Here is a photo.
The Davidsons have promised their Presence
at Saturday night’s concert.
To look at them play bowls you wouldn’t
think there was an ounce of venom in a
generation of it. But the outward honey is
all camouflage. In a moment of extreme
affability, president Spence, of the N.S.W.
Assoc., said he would give £lOO for every
£lOOO the clubs raised for the Red Cross.
Now, after professing profound regard for
the president, every club is consumed with
the one idea of stinging him. The hat passed
round the Warringah green came back with
nearly £5OO. Strathfield held up its chin,
and word went forth that its lot would be
between £7OO and £BOO. Mosman on Satur-
day heard of this, and when the congregation
met at afternoon-tea (it’s afternoon tea on
most greens now, though tell it not in Scot-
land) the plate was sent out with the presi-
dent’s hint that it wouldn’t be welcome back
until Strathfield’s figure had been beaten.
Neither knows exactly what the other’s figure
is; but by all accounts there isn’t much to
choose between them. Each will sting the
president for close-on a hundred pounds.
Captain Jack
Allport, of the
R.F.C. and Mosman,
has just got -the
M.C. for some job
in the air about
which he says noth-
ing, though, a few
months ago, he bril-
liantly described for
The Bulletin the
joys of air training.
His record in the
R.F.C. shows how
well he learnt his
lessons. He was
made acting flight
commander two
months after arrival
in France, and has
been a captain now
for about three months. His record of 11
months’ flying at the Front entitles him to
be regarded as a winged Methuselah.
Originally one of the 7th F.A.8., he was
tent-mate with nine other fine Australians.
Of the number three were wounded early
and put out of the running. Of the remain-
ing seven, two are sergeants, four lieutenants
and one a captain. Allport’s makes the
fourth M.C. won by the commissioned group
of five. Roland Allport, one of the heads
of the Sydney firm of Sayers, Allport, Ltd.,
is his proud dad.
A favorite young Sydney singer, Daisy
Sweet, a Watson Bay lass, has united her
lilt to that of Frank Meßachern. They were
married in St. James’s Church the other
evening. White silk and georgette w r ith a
dash of pink—every other wedding gown
lias a blush-rose lining these days—helped
the pilgrimage. The ’groom is almost a fac-

bit. Allie Affording, in dove grey, was

Kurrajong orange-groves.
Justice Duffy and his only daughter are
in Sydney. The treasure is father’s Associ-
ate, and shadows her gonial dad in his
wanderings.
Walter Watt, the charming if no longer
youthful bridegroom of last year, is settled
down with his wife at Knellerpore, his old
home, which has been rejuvenated and deco-
rated in the latest brocades.
The war has hit the A. 11. Champion
family again. This time it’s voung Lieut.
Chris., who goes to meet his elder brother
Geoff. in Shadowland. The lad went down
in the recent offensive.
Nellie Stewart came out of her retirement
on Red Cross Day, and had a rush of
matinee customers for her sweets.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

Melbourne, April 29, 1918.
My Df,ak Yakkie, —
Federal Government House gave an infor-
mal party last Wednesday. It was quite
comfy. The G.-G. and Lady Helen liad
invited some hundreds of recently-returned
wounded from the military hospitals. The
guests wandered about the lawns and garden
and listened to the band and the birds.
Then there was a tea-party and a concert
in the big drawing-room. An artistic gent-
help had arranged a stage entrance between
the big blue-and-white tapestry curtains and
a brace of enormous velvet screens. Out
from the depths came Dorothy Low in a
white serge frock and did songs-at-the-piano.
Weston Pitt brought his most amiable grin
from the background and sang humorous
songs. Leslie Gaze High Jinked and Ger-
trude Johnston supplied some grand-opera
extracts. A girl from Sydney named Mamie
Punch rolled big black eyes and sang songs
in a contralto voice. All the French win-
dows in the drawing-room were opened wide
to the terrace lawns. Sir Ronald, in khaki,
and Lady Helen, in a dainty house-gown
with lace cuffs and collar, hosted the, happy,
informal party. Majah Stewart helped hand
teacups and was heard to cry in an agitated
way: “Oh, do please be greedy; we’d love
you to be greedy!” Energetic aide Haskett-
Smith, also in khaki, shepherded the concert
party.
His Ex. took interested people round the
library to show them his Lindsay and Low
cartoons. In the concert-room shaded elec-
trics picked out the beauties of the big
family portraits, amongst them handsome
Hugh Munro in plum-colored clothes, and
the full-length portrait of a Vandyke-bearded
ancestor in a fur coat. The ancestor has
his jacket flung back and two fingers grope
in his waistcoat pocket. He is after tup-
pence for a tip.
Richard, the younger son of flourmiller
Minifie, of Brighton, has been caught by the
Huns and lodged at Karlsruhe. Fritz must
have been glad to capture the boy, since he
was a persistent thorn in the side of the
German air service, bombing their aero-
dromes, Lewis-gunning trains, staff cars and
infantry, and performing other feats of a
reckless character. The young man—he is
barely 20—-has gone into retirement with
the rank of flight-commander and a couple
of bars to his Military Gross.
Young Franklin Peterson, son of the late
Professor and bosom pal of Willy Kelly, has
found his way into khaki, which means that
Justice Higgins has lost an Associate and
the Argus its brightest “Oriel” contributor.
Peterson was also suspected of putting some
good stuff into The Bulletin when not too
busy choosing a new tie.
During this month’s Hun offensive, Ser-
geant Ernest Vicars-Foote, with three years’
service to his credit, attained to final honors.
He was a son of Major Richard Vicars-
Foote, of the Bank of Victoria.
One of barrister O’Hara Wood’s soldier-
sons had a joyous furlough just before the
big defensive. He was sent to Monte Carlo
with some of his pals, and they played tennis
and had joy-rides to Nice. Lots of Aus-
tralians have passed that way of late. It is
oil one of the routes by which troops are
sent from Egypt to England.
Nurses Johnston and Leah Rosenthal have
been decorated. The last letters from
them came from Bethune, where they were
on special duty. The two used to run a
private hospital in Melbourne; they enlisted
together and have worked together nearly all
through the war.
Our Anzacs marched and were motored
in procession through town on Friday, which
we called Remembrance Day. The heavens
wept (probably at the recruiting figures)
and Collins-street was roofed with black
clouds and swept with a fine mist as the
men reached the block for disbanding. Here
there were military shouts, a quick furl-
ing of sodden ensigns and dripping banners —
and a lively break for cover. In the procession
were shell-torn banners, two of them from
dressing-stations on the Anzac beach. A
hundred Anzacs carried a huge silk collect-
ing sheet marked with a plan of Gallipoli.
There were 100 head-holes in this banner,
which was spread out across the shoulders
of the jolly beggars. Some of the flung coins
just missed collecting an eye. or a tooth, or
even an ear-tip. It isn’t much of a trick.
Dusk found the Block a bazaar full of damp,
slow-moving crowds, through which crawled
clanging trams with profane gripmen. Auc-
tioneers shouted hoarsely outside each
swamped kiosk. Decorated cars with theatri-
cal songsters supplied the relief. The
colors of the drenched decorations had run.
and the performers’ make-up was in patches
and streaks.
Kitty Jackson, once upon a time Kitty
Boyes, has returned after a couple of years
spent in nearly every part of the world
except Germany. Her husband has charge
of a transport which has been carting troops
backward and forward across the seven seas,
and Kitty lias had a strenuous time rushing
from port to port to meet him.
Mrs. Norman Hodges, aforetime Wanda
Clarke, of Mount Schanck, is helping to
keep the flag flying in England while her
Norman fights ’cross-Channel. A recent
photograph shows the young Australian
missus in corduroys, holding up a plough-
team in the midst of a tru]y-Britisli rural
landscape. She used to be spoken of as a
double of Mrs. Vernon Castle.
Dr. Ted Fyffe, who, with his wife set out
some weeks back on a trip to Java, went
beyond that island, and has been held up in
Singapore. The Dutch boat by which they
were travelling has been taken over. The
pair will steam back to Australia by the
First Chance.
Mrs. Embling and her horse-girls have
been busy on a hunt for band instruments.
Some of the returned Anzac musicians had
no instruments and borrowed a set from the
Sportsmen’s Thousand. When the Thou-
sand asked for their brasses back again the
Anzacs were left with nothing to blow. The
horse-girls in a very little while collected
£l5O, and Allan’s fitted the bandsmen out
completely, allowing the girls two years in
which to pay. With £l5O to start with they
feel quite confident.. The new drums and
brasses were used in public for the first time
on Anzac Day. The girls rode down Collins-
street, with the new band playing ahead, and
raided the Stock Exchange for £2O or £2O.
A young mascot, Eddie Stirling, rides with
them everywhere. The youngster—he’s
about eight—has collected £l5O for various
funds.
Mrs. Harry Charsle.v, once Laura Stead-
man, is visiting from Ceylon, where her hus-
band is a planter. Ceylon also shelters Mrs.
McDonald Soutar, who, long ago, was our
local beauty, Mrs. Maesmore Morris. She lives
now the quiet life of Mrs. Flanter with two
sturdy sons to keep her company. Rose
Musgrove Garrick adorns the green hills
near Kandy, and livens up the inhabitants
with private theatricals.
Mistress Adelaide’s memoranda:
A great recruiting conference of women is billed
for Sunday in the Exhibition Building. It is being
convened by the Mayor and has the support of prac-
tically every organisation, patriotic, political or edu-
cational, that has a sound feminine membership. The
Governor is to be in the chair and Lady Galway
will move the principal resolution —a message of
admiration and devotion to the men at the Front.
Dr. Cavanagli-Mainwaring, who has been on fur-
lough after active service that dates from the Land-
ing, has departed again for the battlefields.
Mr. Mitchell, our new Insolvency Commissioner,
has had news of the death of his son in action in
France.
The libel action which was the outcome of
unpleasant comments on the land purchases of the
Vaughan Government, and was instituted by Craw-
ford Vaughan and his brother-in-law Clarence Goode
and R. P. Blundell, has been abandoned. The
Vaughan party was tendered an unconditional apology
and costs.
Lieut. Dow, who has been recruiting, has had
word that both his sisters, nurses in Adelaide before
the war, have been awarded the Mons Star. Which
reminds me that a recent London Gazette gave par-
ticulars of the winning of the D.C.M. by the sons of
two well-known South Aus. parsons. One was Sgt.
Gifford; the other Sgt. Rayner, now lieutenant.
R. V. Melrose has been elected president of the
Royal Agricultural Society following upon the resig-
nation, owing to illness, of R. H. Crawford, who has
filled the position for six years.
Illness is becoming a more expensive luxury than
ever. From May 1 nurses’ fees are to jump from
£2 2s. to £3 3s. a week, plus fares to and from
the patient’s house.
The Port Adelaide Seamen’s Mission’s annual
meeting gave the Governor a chance of paying a
fine tribute to the “magnificent courage and stead-
fastness of seamen during the war,” and of appeal-
ing for more generous support for the organisations
that are working for them. You know, I suppose,
that his Ex.’s renewed offer to serve in any capa-
city himself has again been turned down by the
British Government.
This has been “Soldiers’ Fortnight” with a ven-
geance, and the public responded in a way that
should bring the men a good deal of the £50,000
they want for their club fund.
Doris Egerton Jones managed to reach the other
side of the Pacific a while ago; then, with the help
of Uncle Sam, she landed in the Old Dart, where,
so a recent cable tells, she changed her name to
Mrs. Reg Callaghan. The girl in the case is the
authoress of “Peter Piper” and some other fiction,
and the ’groom has been doing his bit behind an
18-pounder in France for the last year and a half.
He belongs to Windsor in X. S. Wales, and gives a
hand at the Permanent Trustee office in peace times.
Another Australian soldier to marry
abroad is Captain Littlejohn, of the Army
Med., a hefty son of the principal of Scotch
College. But/fe picked an Australian Mary—
the youngest of the William Andrews girls.
Another Andrews daughter is married to
Lieut.-Col. Edmund Drake-Brockman, of
Westralia, whose sister Deb is now 7 Mrs.
Hackett-Moulden. Dad Andrews is a stout
stick among Hawthorn's tall financial timber.
Carmen Delprat, whose gorgeous wedding
party was postponed from April 10, was
made Mrs. Teppema with the least possible
fuss last week.
Marion Campbell managed to get a pass
from ’Murka to the Fog, after all, and has
been duly married to her naval fianc£, Frank
Couldrey, who in pre-war days was a P. and
O. officer. Mrs. Campbell is letting her
Toorak home, Ottawa. The place has too
many rooms and memories for the sorrowful
widow after the loss of husband, daughter
and two khakied sons, all within a few
months. The youngest chick. Helen, is a
nursing trainee, and son John is leaving
Melbourne Grammar after a final sprint in
the public-schools race. Then with his pal
Ford Guthrie he will steam away to do his
bit with the Naval air service.
Dr. Marshall, of Scots’ Church, is now
grandpa to a little Britisher. News of grand-
daughter’s arrival in London came by cable
last week from dad, who is Lieut.-Col. Nor-
man Marshall, D. 5.0., M.C.
Dr. Balls-Headly, who passed out last week
at Vancouver, had been sheltering there for
many years with his second wife, who was
one-time nurse-in-chief at his private hospital
in this city. The late medico was famed for
the correctness of his bedside manner, his
immaculate tailorings, his artistic taste and
his handsome first wife.
Having conquered Brisbane, the Calfill-
Brooke Concert Co. is driving north and
getting a welcome everywhere. The show is
“represented” by a woman —Miss Madeline
Clark. She has just returned from the
“East,” where she went ahead for Freddie
Shipman.
When members of the Melbourne Literary
Club assembled outside The Playhouse for
their meeting on Wednesday night the
building was in darkness and no key forth-
coming. After waitimr half an hour they
marched round -to a room at the Fiinders-
street station, where Enid Derham held
forth, with an anxious look in her weather
eye, on Minor Poetry. Vance Palmer, brown
as the soldiering garb he wore, was there
with his wife. t
Artists Dora Wilson, May Roxburgh and
Ruth Sutherland are at this moment putting
the finishing touches on their picture show at
the Fine Art Galleries. They have been
painting hard all summer in the hills and
valleys, and the results are all .ready for
exhibition this week.
Specially lighted, hung with flags and
with probably a larger area of boiled shirt-
front on view than at any previous time in
its history, Melbourne King’s presented a
festive appearance on Saturday night, when
Guy B. Post arrived in “The Masquerader.”
U.S.A.-citizen Burnett was with Mrs. Eve-
lyn Muret McEvoy in the stalls, and other
starred-and-striped visitors were scattered
about the house. Bert Bailey, who had in-
terrupted a leap from Sydney to Adelaide to
be present, beamed from a box, and actress
Margaret Sutcliffe was in the circle. Mrs.
Kitty Jackson, just back from foreign parts,
and tenor Walter Kirby, who took his seat
after removing numerous wrappings from
the throat where he keeps the lost chord,
were also observed. The house was enthusi-
astic, and Guy B. had to make a few re-
marks at the finish. Leading lady Thais
Lawton displayed several striking gowns
and a masterful chin. Adele Ritchie —the
blonde who takes the part of the naughty
lady who employs morphia as an aid to love-
making—is Mrs. Post.
Bates Post has been married four times and
is happy. He says he is always happy. He
can even be happy w T hen the people in the
streets stare and gape at his walk-about
make-up. Though he is an actor-manager
and the whole box of theatrical tricks, he
admits he is still stage-struck. His experi-
ence in choosing wives shows up well in
Mrs. Bates Post. She is of the dainty ash-
blonde type—as natty, warm and friendly
as a well-bred Persian kitten.
The leading “Masquerader” lady spreads
some wonderful chiffons. One is flouncy,
white and transparent —it quivers all
through her tremolo speeches. She speaks
her lines in a suppressed soprano quaver
somewhere about high C. Her voice and
her thick black coil of hair were built for
tragedy—Fedora brand. She agrees to re-
tain the pseudo John Chilcote in the last
act in black velvet. Mrs. Bates Post, who
plays a lady-of-title at dinner parties, wears
gorgeous clothes on her small plump person.
A dinner dress of cloth-of-gold, with a giddy
sideshow of gold silk-stockinged leg, has a
Cleopatra sweep of green feather fan and a
shawl of green ninon to enliven it.
Fayette Perry has taken herself and her
American accent for a trip to Japan,
humor suspects a honeymoon-trip.
Actor Guy Bates Post, who has been going
through a round of receptions, carefully
stage-managed, met his first Australian in
Canada. He was, he says, walking in the
street when two gloved hands applauded him
from a taxi. The driver was ordered to stop,
and the owner of the gloves beckoned to Post
and congratulated him on his perfoxmance,
which she had seen the night before. The
lady, whom the actor had not previously met,
was Melba.
Some people are born lucky. Theatrical-
manager Johnnie Fuller was driving his wife
and a party of friends back to Melbourne
from The Lakes in a big touring car when a
mob of horses on the road misbehaved and
John emptied his party, car and all, into a
culvert. Not a scratch or injury to anyone or
the ear l More horses and ropes got the
vehicle back on to the highway, and the
party sailed home.
“The Willow Tree” brought a sprinkling
of last season’s frocks and crush hats to the
Royal on Saturday. Grace Palotta, who
keeps the secret of perpetual infancy for her
smile and discourse, seemed a sombre note of
complaint against an iridescent blonde in
the lower box. On .the next floor were Mrs.
Wenman, ma and a damsel who would pro-
bably be identified as Maggie Oickinson
when she emerged from her coat. Kathlene
Macdonell was in a rich orange kimono with
the national obi bisecting its glare. She also
wore a creepy green thing which made one
think of lizards.
Guy Post is just glorious. One rigout in
which he has been dazing the multitude
lately can be seen and heard at Healesville.
Its apex is a tweed hat which seems to have
been sat on by an alderman. Below are coat
and pants copied from a trellis, and these
supported by an exuberant waistcoat, deli-
cate spats and a monocle make it unneces-
sarv to send out a band.
Different soldier-boys have different esti-
mates of the glory attaching to the Military
Medal. One of Hal Gye’s surviving brothers
in the war had a horse shot under him,
and pr. sumably did rather more than a
fair-sized bit. Writing to brother Hal
about his strange adventures the damaged
warrior said: “By the way. I’ve been
awarded the Military Medal, but I didn’t
tell them at home. You needn’t mention
it.” Apparently he didn’t want to alarm
anybody by suggesting that he had been
seeking the bubble reputation even at the
cannon’s mouth.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGINALITIES
Bad Australian”: The boys who did the
packing in the roaring days on the West Coast
(Tas.) were hard to beat when they hitched their
long-sleeved boots up to their waistbelts,- shed their
“blueys,” and with 801 b. or 901 b. up started on the
weary splodge through the awful quagmire misnamed
“the corduroy” to Zeehan, or the long crawl through
the “horizontal” to Dundas, Mt. Reid and the other
mountain camps. But good as they were they
wouldn't hare a look-in with the cargadores who
carry the mails and a hundred and one odds and
ends over the Andes when the passes are closed in
winter. These cargadores are generally half-breed
Auraucanian Indians, well under average height, but
built on the same lines as a wombat and just about
as hard to kill. They' take to the transport business
as soon as- they are able to work, and never do
anything else in winter, except a little robbery with
violence. They earn about three or four times as
much as ordinary peones, with a square feed and a
litre of rotten wine every three or four hours. Their
work is always at an altitude of from 9000 ft. to
13,000 ft. < above sea-level, over snow that is rarely
hard enough to carry them, and the passes are so
broken up with rock slides and moraines that ski
or snow-shoes can only be used when travelling light.
In order to make the going as easy as possible they
move in Indian file, taking it in turn to break trail,
and they leave a track like a camel pad. Their
foot-covering is peculiar to the Higher Cordillera
and consists of about three or four “Prince Alberts”
encased in a big rough moccasin of cowhide, with
the hairy side out to prevent slipping. Travelling
on these la mangos the new-chum feels as if .he
were walking on a pair of badly-inflated football's—
all right in soft snow, but a soul-harrowing piece of
gymnastics on anything solid. The usual load is
40 kilos (about 901 b.), which is carried West Coast
fashion, with the addition of a strap reeved through
the shoulder-straps and buckled up fairly tight
across the chest. The carriers average about two
miles au hour and do 14 or 15 miles a day, rising
about 3000 ft. or 4000 ft. in that distance. Although
40 kilos is their regular load they frequently carry
more, and I have seen a quarter of beef weighing
0 - l kilos (1301 b.) carried ,12 .miles in one day, the
eargadpr starting at 8500 ft. above sea-level and
quitting at 10,000 ft. But the snow was fairly hard,
and the cargador, in addition to his wine allowance,
got away with four stiff nips of pisco on the
journey. His cargo of calamity-pop alone was
enough to kill a plainsman at that altitude.
* * * *
“Leo Lear” : Let me explain to “Douglas
I. (B. 14/3/’18), who calls for sudden death to
cormorants as fish-eating pests, that the birds do far
more good than harm. For example, they feed their
young on yabbies, which consume more fish eggs in
a day than cormorants eat fish in a week-. Besides,
yabbies are pests in irrigation banks, perforating
them and starting breaks. -Some Abo. lately wanted
Nankeen herons slaughtered as fish,murderers, but
they, too, more than balance accounts as yabbie
catchers. Again, all sorts and conditions of hawks
are blazed at if they appear near a poultry-yard; yet
for every egg and chick a goshawk or falcon (the
only real stealers of the family) gets, a dozen times
the value is slain in mice. Several of the honey-
eaters are addicted to eating fruit, yet kill them off,
as some advocate, and the fruit-eating insects they
destroy would strip half the orchards in the land
before Hughes christens his first locally-built ship.
The white-eye, another fruit-eater, is the best aphis-
destroyer extant, so it will pay anyone to stop before
pulling a trigger on the next one. I admit some of
GETTING BEYOND THEMSELVES.

Government as wants to RUN the- country?"
our birds are at times a pest to the bloke on the
land, but do away with them and we’ll be relieved
of the mortgage by an egg-sucking bug or something.
(N.B. —I exempt the crow from any mercy ; he’s a
German.)

“Silas Snell” : We reached the Ace in time
for lunch, and wasted half an hour outside Ward’s
Hotel in momentary expectation, believing' Mclntosh
and Ladd were about to kill each other. They were
disputing heatedly- over the ownership of a goat.
Their malignity was something frightful. They
called each other everything five times over, and
many times seemed right on the point of man-
slaughter. Disgusted at length, and made sick with
hope deferred, we went in to our meal.- Having spent
half an hour over lunch, we returned to the open,
where Ladd and Mclntosh were still exchanging
recriminations, and still almost bi*ing each other in
their anger. We waited another 20 minutes, hoping
against hope, and then walked out to the Horseshoe
Fall, We reached Ward’s Hotel again at half after
five in the evening. Our progress in the last half-
mile was greatly accelerated by the dust and skurry
of a great battle in front of the pub. Elbowing
through the crowd, we found Mrs. Ladd and Mrs.
Mclntosh fighting like maddened Amazons. Mclntosh
and Ladd were still talking. Ladd was saying:
‘‘What’id I tell yeh! What’id I tell yeh! I knew
you’d go too fur.”
* * * *
“Country Ped”: In a few years’ time it
will be worth travelling along the Victorian railway
lines. The Commissioners, some years since, appointed
a gardener to superintend improvements (I hope not
out of loans) to various unsightly embankments and
cuttings in the suburban area—and it has been well
done. Now the expert is making the country lines
picturesque. So far, native trees have been planted
along the Sydney and Adelaide lines (Victorian sec-
tions) . From Essendon to Seymour is the best show-
ing at present, with its rows of sugar gums, flowering
gums, various wattles, etc. What it will be like
some day in the Spring to whiz through miles of
gold and scarlet beats imagination.
* * * *
“Spragge”: Re “E.S.S/s” remarks about
the lead-boring beetle (B. 14/2/’18). Am in
the game, and have had a few of these line troubles
to contend with, and if these holes are bored by a
beetle from outside the cable would , “E.S.S.” kindly
explain how it is that in every case that has come
under my notice the paper insulation round the hole,
and in one case three inches each side of the hole,
had been blackened b,v smoke, while lead splinters
were distributed through the cable? The holes in
the sheath of the cable were the size of those made
by the wood-borer, but I would sooner say that
lightning was the cause of the trouble.

“Gordartk”: The Salv’army roars some
strange Hallelujah choruses sometimes, and I’ve even
heard “Oh, Jesus is Square Dinkum” to a modern
tune. But the other day in Miles (W.Q.) an old
lubra jolted them all when she hopped into the happy
circle and squeaked in a tearful voice, “Thank Gawd
I’m saved, but someope’s stole my plurry pup!”

HOW HERMAN WON THE CROSS.
The WILD CAT COLUMN
Once in a blue eternity they gave us dabs of rum
To close seams ’if keep the flume in liquor-
tight, condition ;
But, soft ’n’ sentimental, when the long, cold evenin's
come,
I’d dream me nibs was drinkin’ to the height of
his ambition,
With rights of suction over all the breweries there are,
Where barrels squat, like Brahma gods, in Mother
Hardy’s bar.
1 had me fit of longin’ on the night the Germans
came.
All breathin’ like a gas attack. The air was
halcoholic. ,
Me smelt ’em in the darkness, ’n’ our rage went up
i n flame.
It was envy, squealin’ Envy, put the ginger in the
frolic.
We shot ’em full of spelter, then went over it to spite
The swines what drunk the liquor that was ours by
common right.
'‘lf this ain’t stopped, ’n’ quick,” sez we, “there
won’t be left a drop
To celebrate the vict’ry when we capture their
position.”
I’m prowlin’ blind, when sharp there comes a fond,
familiar plop—
Swung round a post, a German in. a pitiful condition
Looms over me. lie’s sprung a cork, and shakes a
flask on high,
’N’ sings of beer that touchin* it would make a
butcher cry.
Sez he: “Berloffed kamarid, you haf some drinks mit
you.”
I’d meant to spike him where ho waved, but altered
me intention.
’N’ “If you puts it thus,” sez I, “I don’t care if I
do.”
We has a drink together. There’s a tempor’y sus-
pension
Of hostilities to sample contraband V other stuff
In the enemy’s possession. Which I think he’s had
enough.
That Hun had thirty pockets, ’n’ he’d stowed a flask
in each,
’N’ presently I’m thinkin’ I could love him like a
brother.
He’s talkin’ fond ’n’ friendly in outlandish parts of
speech. *
“You’re prisoner of war,” I sez; ’n’ then we has
anothW.
Ten flasks he pours into his hat, V fills it to the
brim,
’N’ weeps ’n’ sez his frau she will be waitin’ up for
him.
We drink each other’s health, ’n’ know no henmity
nor fear.
I see I’ve got to pinch him, but he’s out to do
his div. in,
’N’ don’t care if he don’t go home till daylight doth
appear.
Sez he: “I pud you home to bed upside dot ’ouse
you live in.”
He shakes his finger in me eye: “Mein friendt, you’re
preddy trunk!”
Then arm in arm through No Man’s Land we does a
social bunk.
There’s Hell afoot. Comes more than once the glug
of sudden death.
We’re rockin’ fine ’n’ careless where the rifle fire
is breakin’,
’N’ singin’ most uproar’ous, in the bomb’s disgustin’
breath,
Of girls, ’n’ drink, ’n’ cheerful sprees, ’n’ ’Erman
thinks lie’s takin’
A cobber home to somewhere in a subbub damp ’n’
dim,
Whereas I know fer certain it is me is takin’ him.
Somehow, sometime, I lands him where he’s safely
put to bed.
I wake nex’ day, ’n’ holy smoke! I’m prisoner with
the German.
Me mouth is like an ashpan, there’s hot fish-bolts in
me head,
’N’ through the barb-wire peerin’ is me foreign
cobber ’Erman.
Ve capdure each lasd nighd,” sez he, “you home haf
bring me, boss.”
For gallant conduct , selp me cat, he got the Iron
Cross !
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.

FOSTER’S LAGER
“Ibis”: “E.S.S.” mentions (B. 4/4/’18)
the new Ma State town of Griffith, with its appro-
priate street names —a town, he might have added,
without a single building on it to date! On the
other hand, Hanwood, a settled village three miles
off, has numerous well-formed streets, and nary a
name among the lot. This is in the Mirrool subdivi-
sion of the Murrumbidgee Irrigation Area, so named
after Mirrool Creek, the only, (natural) watercourse
within 47 cooees. But the very name of Mirrool was
long ago snared on behalf of a little railway town
near Ariah Park. Behold, then, this geographical
insanity: Mirrool and West Mirrool public schools are
60 miles apart as the crow flies, and East Mirrool is
50 miles ivest of Mirrool.

“Wart”: In 1843 Philip Oakden. of Laun-
ceston (Tas.), who started the Bank of Australasia,
imported a blackberry in a pot from England, lie
gave six little cuttings to an old gardener of his
named Broadway, who had taken up ground on the
Forth River, North West Coast. Broadway halved
his share with Fenton, the Tasmanian historian and
the first man to settle in that part of Tasmania.
Two or three years afterwards the plants astonished
the natives. The birds had seen to distribution, and
they were everywhere or thereabouts. This year
Jones’s jam-factory cheques for sproutings about
£lO,OOO have gone to the district, most of the
money earned by women and children. As much as
£5O has been earned by one family.
“Ernest Osborne”: “Malaria’s” (B.
14/3/’18) pertinent question re use of quinine is one
difficult to answer even by men with more than a
nodding acquaintance with fever. In Santos, Brazil,
I* have seen scores die, nightly, of yellow fever and
malaria; and once, of a ward full of yellow fever
patients, I was the only man to walk out. My
recovery, I was informed, was due to my abstinence
from intoxicants. In other deadly countries I have
been told by experienced men that it is injudicious
not to take stimulants in moderation. At Rio de
Janiero I never suffered, nor in tropical American
river deltas, black and awful with mire, looking as
might Styx itself, and full of fever cases. In India,
up rivers, through jungle and low country, when
jungle fever was active, I passed and escaped again.
And I never took quinine. Then, under the'Equator,
in the Indian Ocean, nearly a month oUt from the
Irrawaddy, fever broke out. Some had it severely ; I
had it lightly. Since then I have had fever in
the Central Pacific and all but died-r Less than
two years ago I had malaria severely in the N.W.
Pacific, yet, making a 200-mile dash for assistance,
the fever had left me with a normal temperature
before the journey’s end. And I don’t suffer any after-
effects, i.e., the fevers have never recurred. Yet I meet
Island-men in Australia, away from the Islands for
years, quite sick with fever. I have seen swamps abso-
lutely packed with mosquito wrigglers, including the
anophele, yet remarkably few mosquitoes were seen
in the clearings. The developed insect undoubtedly
leaves the clearings for the jungle. Many more
than in the open are seen and felt in the houses and
about the labor lines, and therein lies the gravest
danger to the white. Escape malaria on even a
moderately-healthy plantation he never can —I am
speaking of countries such as most of New Guinea,
Nevv Britain, New Ireland and adjacent islands.
The laborers, recruited from widely-separated locali-
A POINT IN ITS FAVOR.
BILLJIM (from North Queensland) : “ Blimey,
it’s a good job there’s no alligators in
these parts. This caper wouldn't be too

ties, but always from the river deltas and coastal
lowlands, are full of fever, are often sick, and many
die. From these active Carriers of the anophele
parasite infection is conveyed by mosquitoes of any
kind, gnats, sandflies, and, I think, lice, fleas and
bugs, to healthy persons. The antidote, quinine, is
most effective in liquid form, in combination with
hydrochloric acid, generally in five-grain doses as a
preventive, and 10-grains for curative purposes. Sal-
varsan has been experimented with, and, while not
claimed to prevent malaria, it is said to have
ensured a rapid recover in some cases.

“Hellish Reef”: Tell “Suwarrow” from
me that porpoises travel any old way in a calm, and
when a wind arises they travel any old way also.
When they travel against the wind it is because they
happen to be going that way, and jf they travel
otherwise that happens also. The porpoise is a
happensome and unconcerned animal, and the idea
that he steers on fixed principles is out of date. Only
ancient mariners and deck-hands on ferry-boats hang
on to the old belief. <
“Balliang” : How’s this for luck? As M.
Domascheny was driving home at Rainbow (Vic.), in
the dim hours of the morning, a magpie, startled by
the glare of the buggy lamps, dashed into his face.
Its bill pierced the eyeball and the sight is lost.

“Umfaan”: An extraordinary case came
under my notice in France. An Australian mare
which had been with our forces since we left Aus-
tralia —a light draught—was put to an Arab stallion
before we left Egypt. In the military it is quite
simple to keep tab on mares. Nevertheless this mare
produced a foal which had (and still has) many of
the characteristics of a mule—the lengthened ears,
flat shoulders, and, when he got older, an unmistak-
able imitation of a bray. Our veterinary experts,
including the veterinary officer, diagnosed the case as
telegony from some gentleman-donk in Australia.
The foal was undoubtedly not a mule.

“The Lizard”: I don’t agree with “Eloc,”
who advised turps for a dog phosphorus-poisoned.
I d rather recommend an emetic of ashes and water,
and then give Condy’s Fluid sufficient to make a
dark pink mixture, followed by egg and milk or
gruel. For strychnine bait, arsenic, cyanide of
potassium and prussic acid, give an emetic —table-
spoonful of mustard or handful of ashes in half-
pint of water, or salt and water, followed by castor-
oil or a couple of packets of Epsom salts. In cases
of prussic-acid poisoning, which is the most deadly
for Fido, after an emetic has been given and the
hound has lost consciousness, artificial respiration
will go a long way to help bringing him back to
life. In all cases keep the patient warm.
“Byjoe”: “6262” (B. 14/3/’18) suggests
that by taking advantage of the earth’s magnetic
field one can put a fine edge on a blunt razor. This
result may be obtained by an analogous but less
cumbrous method: Leave the razor in its box, then
rest the case on its narrow face and place a bar
magnet in a line with it, so that the north pole of
the magnet is about an inch from one end of the
box. The bar will rearrange the molecules in the
steel blade so that they extend all in one direction,
even more effectively than will the terrestrial field.
The blade will acquire an edge as keen as Billy
Hughes’s appetite in the tart-shop.
“FY7”: “Diomed” Hi. 4/4/;I8) is right
this far: the young kangaroo grows on the mother’s
teat; but it is not born there. The birth processes
of the kangaroo have been observed many times in
captivity, and amount to this beyond all possible
dispute: the youngster is born in the ordinary way
and is placed by the mother on the teat in the
pouch. Since, however, the baby, like all
the non-placental mammals, comes to life in a very
undeveloped condition, it has to complete its develop-
ment attached to the mother's body by the teat
instead of to a placental cord, as in the case of the
higher mammals. If Joey's capacity to hang on
was left to himself he would soon perish, so Nature
makes all safe. The entrance to the mouth at birth
is merely a circular orifice, opening into an inner
cavity. The point of the teat is inserted by the
mother, often after some trouble, and, once enclosed,
the fine point of the teat swells up, filling the new
arrival’s mouth and fastening him as firmly to
mamma as if he were riveted on. Since Joey is too
young even to suck, his mother is provided with a
special set of muscles with which to inject nourish-
ment into her offspring. When the right stage has
arrived, the young ’un’s mouth opens to its normal
shape and size and he drops from his anchorage.
Henceforward he must pick for himself.

“Melos”: Tell “Azack” (B. 14/3/T8) that
the music of a tin-can band, the report of a gun or
any other startling noise invariably causes a colony
of absconding bees to cohie earthward and swarm
again. I have seen them stopped by these means
almost immediately after leaving the hive. I would
like to know how entomologists have determined that
the bee has no, sense of hearing. The absence of
well-defined auditory organs? This does not prove
much. Personally, I think that the insect has an
uncommonly keen apparatus for sensing vibrations.
Bushmen, when locating a sugarbag in a tree, give
a few raps on the trunk, and immediately some of
the more inquisitive honey-makers rush out to learn
the cause of the disturbance. Again, the bee working
in the field packs up and makes for home when
thunder happens.

“Wandi”: Talking of bird names, the
bobala has probably more aliases than any other
Australian bird, being variously known as friar bird,
monk, pimlico, four o’clock, poor soldier and
leatherhead. Bobala' is Binghi’s name for him. He
is a jolly scamp, a feathered comic acrobat, with a
roguish naked black head and a prominent knob on
the top of his long bill. Now and again, between his.
clarion calls, he utters a low chuckle, as though well
pleased with himself and the world. He is also a
pugnacious character, and being rather aggravating
about the end of the year fruit-growers generally
call him a blanky something. Which is one more
name. He lives principally on insects, berries and
the pollen of the eucalypts.
“D.F.”: Victoria’s first crop was put in
on Churchill Island, Westernport. When Lieutenant
Grant visited Westernport at the end of March, 1801,
he had a patch cleared on the island. As he had
neither hoe nor spade he made shift to have the
ground turned over with a coal-shovel and sowed
wheat, iriai7.e, peas, some grains of rice and coffee-
berries, with onions and potatoes and the stones and
pips of various fruit-trees. When Murray put
into Westernport in December, 1801, on the voyage
in the . course of which he discovered Port Phillip,
he found the wheat a fathom high and ripe. He
had it reaped and got about a bushel out of it, to
be used to feed some young swans that he had on
board. He also found some potatoes arid onions, as
well as maize. He left no news about the rice and
the coffee-berries.

“Skeeter”: “J. Pannikin” loved a kiddy
to think. One clay he walked into a small bush
school and started off in his unconventional way:
“I’m the inspector. I know everything, don’t I?”
The kiddies bleated dutifully, “Yes, sir!” But
one youngster hesitated, and, Pannikin’s eye, catch-
ing his hesitation, lit on that one. “Well, what do
you think ?” he asked. The kiddy stammered,
“You don't know everything, sir.” Donald was
delighted. “Now, what is it I don’t know?” “You
dunno how many sheep my father’s got,” said the
kid in triumph. That was the introduction; and
soon they were all at it like a happy family.
THE SOUL OF A COW.
The WILD CAT COLUMN
I was walking in the country near the town of
Seeyersoon,
And the lady who was with me was a thin and
dreamy type;
She was yearning, she was burning, when —“Oh, lool:
at the balloon!”
Broke the thread of our sweet converse, which was
sentimental tripe.
With a single rosy finger pointing at cerulean space,
She directed my attention to a soaring globe. 1
sighed,
For it showed me that a milker, in some silent, leafy
place,
Had lain down and kicked the bucket, or, to be
exact, had died.
“Is that Captain Penfold swinging on a bar there
underneath ?
Some big fGte is being held here, and I wish you d
take me, dear!”
Yes, her white chin was delicious and" I loved her
firm white teeth
As she gazed up at that cow’s soul o’er the tree-
tops floating clear.
“It is no balloon,” I muttered; “it’s a cow's soul
full of air.
There’ll be bitter strife and grieving if it's torn
by boughs or roots,
For my old .Encyclopaedia tells me nothing can com-
pare
With a cow's soul for repairing simple country
people’s boots.”
She was kind and so poetic, she would rather read
than eat;
But she flew across the paddocks till she caught
that floating soul,
And she pricked it with a hatpin and she pressed it
with her feet
Till it lay all flat and empty. “This will make a
lovely stole!
Then I think, with beads and ninon, and two yards
of grey Georgette,
I could make a frock for dinner. Yes, I’m sure it
would look fine!”
And all happened as she planned it. She is wearing
that frock yet,
And her cow-soul costume knocks ’em when in
restaurants. we dine.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Rewolf”: The Tas. Railway Department
motor on the Strahan-Zeehan line takes
beating. It is about the size of a packing-
case, and is supposed to carry passengers
and mails to connect through to Lyell. When
not undergoing repairs it starts early in the
morning for Strahan, and sometimes (how
no one knows) it completes the trip and
there are great rejoicings. Usually it jibs,
and then all hands push it to some obscure
siding and a special train is sent to bring it
home. After three years of'this sort of thing
wise passengers wear good, strong boots, and
take a blanket and two days’ rations. But
they still have to pay full first-class fare for
pushing. The jibbing is so regular that
once the vehicle didn’t do a single trip with-
out a breakdown for six weeks. The Coast
has now asked the Railway Department to
give the well-founded accusation to the
Museum and put on a perambulator.

“W.H.E.”: When you happen to bump
Webster’s infallible department don’t rush
the ’phone officials. In fact, it’s good policy
to pocket your troubles. Webster claims to
be helping to win the war, and this is one
of the ingenious methodL For three weeks
in January my family rusticated by the sea-
side and left the suburban mansion in charge
of an old pian who is quite incapable of
using Webster’s nerve-wrecking contraption.
But the telephone office swears that I owe
for 12 calls during my absence and insists
on charging 6d. for supplying the informa-
tion. As there is no appeal against Augustus
Post-office Caesar I agree with the bill lest a
worse thing should befall me.
“E.S.S.”: The Queensland Pine Co., which
hangs out its shingle at Yarraman, is get-
ting busy to make use of some of the good
material which this prodigal country has
been making bonfires of longer than the old-
est inhabitant can remember. Its works are
in a good timber-getting district, and its
object is to utilise the tree-tops and other
discarded stuff in the manufacture of paper.
By the way, why is it that corn husks, thou-
sands of tons of which are burnt every year
on farms, are not used for the same purpose?
Then there is the bark of the tea-tree, which
can be stripped off in sheets without injuring
the body. It is very light, resembling cork
in appearance and is known as paper bark
and cork bark.

“Plumber”: Here's an instance of
“national efficiency” that even Hughes can
hardly beat. A liingwood (Vic.) man is
building a villa, the bricks for which come
from Northcote, 22 miles away. Price, be-
fore the war, 425.; to-day 575. (Id., plus
cartage. There is a brickyard at liingwood,
another at Tunstall (three miles), another
at Blackburn (four miles), but all have
been closed ever since Bent failed to smash
the combine. There are also two live brick-
yards much nearer to liingwood than North-
cote, but they don’t “belong,” and the
builder simply didn’t dare to get his bricks
from them. He’d have had trouble about his
timber and tiles if he had.

“Fax”: There’s a Sericulture Association,
“founded to establish the raw silk industry
in Victoria,” and by this time it ought to
be aware of its 'own utter futility. Among
kids who keep silkworms—and every kid
keeps silkworms, or wants to —there is a
fond delusion that the product is worth a
guinea an ounce, which delusion appears to
have remained with such as grew up to be
members of the V.S.A. Recently a Lilydale
lady and her daughters came to town with
the raw silk the daughters had wound off
from 1000 cocoons after weeks or months
of gentle industry. It weighed 340 z., and
its worth, in a correctly-reeled condition,
would have been about 3s. 6d. But it wasn’t
properly reeled. A silkworm issues its deli-
cate thread without the least regard for con-
sistency, and in unwinding, say, half a dozen
cocoons at once the expert reeler makes
breaks and joinings in order to keep the
thread of uniform thickness. The silk in-
dustry starts with the slow-growing mul-
berry tree to provide food for the worm, and
an expert operator has spent 12 hours an
reeling the 15s. worth of silk from cocoons
created by the worms that ate 2501 b. of
leaves from the slow-growing vegetable.
Yet the Yic. Sericulture Association has
been exhorting people on the land to plant
mulberry trees, breed silkworms and grow
rich!
* . * * *
“Smoko”: Australians should take another
look at the tobacco industry. Latterly the
white man has almost dropped out, leaving
the field to Chinese; but the improved
prices, if accompanied by a willingness to
listen to the requirements of the manufac-
turers, will enable the baby to sit up. In
Manilla and Barraba districts (N.S.W.)
this year what crops there are are giving
good yields, and the price is right (up to
Is. 9d. a pound). But it nearly all goes to
the Chow. One of him will net a sum
running into the thousands (unless a frost
comes) and at least one Australian will
collect £llO from an acre. Australia can
produce the weed, but it has not as yet
grown anything equal,to the American pro-
duct. The tendency here has been, especially
with Ah Fat, to produce the dark leaf. But
the market requirement of dark in propor-
tion to bright is only as one is to four. The
Chow is hopelessly conservative. lie does
things for the sufficient reason that his
grandfather did them. His tendency is to
stick to the heavy river fiats, place the
plants with wide intervals, keep the leaves
meagre and so produce the rank growth. He
is learning, but the Australian could beat
him every time. It is quality that counts.
The public has been brought up on the Vir-
ginian product. We demand high-class
stuff, and to be successful the grower must
produce it. With more attention (to business
we could get very near the Virginian. Later,
fine-curing would raise the standard higher
than sun-curing can. Let Billjim listen to
the buyer, know what he wants and pro-
duce it. Prices are likely to continue good,
but the buyers must have quality. The weed
is fickle. A great deal depends on . aroma
and flavor, and these qualities are due
largely to climatic conditions. The climate
to meet all requirements for high-grade leaf
has not yet been located in Australia. But
a great deal can be done to improve the
present leaf by planting in lighter soils,
planting closer, allowing more leaves to
remain on the stalk and by the curing pro-
cess. There is a large and growing con-
sumption of the weed, and we cannot afford
to go on fattening the account of the over-
seas gardener.

“Simon”: William Holding and Robert
Overall, two working mechanics at Drum-
moyne, Sydney, have succeeded in producing
a beautifully-turned little motor-ear, Aus-
tralian from the carburetter and the four-
cylinder engine to the number-plate at the
hack. They only completed "The Acme” in
time to exhibit during the last two or three
days of the late show. Front and back
splashes and footboard are all shaped from
the one sheet of metal, and take the eye with
the grace *bf the extended wing of a flying-
fox. It may be that this car’s durability is
not yet proven, but from the standpoint of
symmetry and finish few trimmer machines
are included amongst the 16,000 registered
in N.S.W. or the 40,000 doing more or less
intermittent service in Australia.
“Y.Z.”: Have just been put wise to
another trick of trade. This is a feature of
the poulterer’s gentle art. Never buy a
trimmed duck, for instance, without sub-
mitting the bird to weights and measures,
because appearances are deceitful and the
world is still deluded by cheap schemes. A
plump duck that looks like an eight-pounder,
if tried in the scale, may, if you’re lucky,
hold four pounds of the true goods. The
rest is just wind. Ten to one your promis-
ing bird has been blown up. I don’t know
how the process is accomplished, but the
bird-man’s assistant assures me it is a
favorite device. I have seen the disaster
often, the bird bought yesterday looking this
morning the collapsed balloon of its former
self.
* * * *
“Araminta”: The average Australian
housewife is ready to pay 4d. a pound for
French beans, which are one of pur most
valuable foods. Usually, since the grower
gets paid by weight and not by quality, the
French bean of commerce is old and stringy
because it has been left on the stem too
long; and it is often withered by the time
the householder gets it. Now, one single
bean-plant, occupying 2i square feet of
ground, can be encouraged to produce as
much as 21b. of green beans. The ripe
Ffench-bean, moreover, is nearly as good as
meat. Soak the goods overnight and simmer
them next morning, well and truly, with a
strip of bacon-fat, and you have one of the
staples of the Allied armies in the field—
“pork and beans.” Yet how many house-
wives can be “bothered” to stick in a row
of seeds and watch the plants to maturity,
not to mention extending fheir operations
to other back-garden stuff? People say that
every penny saved nowadays is a shove
towards Berlin—and they’re saving the
pennies in England’s back gardens. But
the Australian yard is mostly reserved for
one lone pepper-tree, the washin’ o’ Mon-
and four wandering fowls.
LUCY’S BOOTS.
The WILD CAT COLUMN
Miss Lucy Lee has pretty feet.
As all the district knows.
She keeps them wonderfully neat,
And silken is her hose.
She’ll give a guinea for a dress,
And in a hat will put
A erow-n, then pay for shoes no less
Than one pound ten per foot.
Her face is well enough, her hair
A fairy well might choose;
Her lips are passable—but, there,
She takes a two in shoes!
My Lucy Lee is very prim,
And she demurely goes,
Her eyes cast down in sober whim
Are ever on her toes.
Her figure’s not sublime, but it
Is to my simple taste.
She cares not for her corset’s fit
If well her boots are laced.
I try in vain to win a smile;
The wooing bears no fruits,
My Lucy’s cold, for all the while
Her heart is in her boots.
VICTORIA.
S. S.

SPECIALIST EXPLAINS CAUSE OF
STOMACH TROUBLE.
VALUABLE ADVICE TO SUFFERERS.
“There are' many different forms of
stomach trouble,” said a well-known special-
ist recently, “but practically all are trace-
able to excessive acidity and food fermenta-
tion. That is why the results obtained from
the use of drugs are usually so disappointing.
Admitting fermentation and consequent acid-
ity of the food contents to be the underlying
cause of most forms of indigestion, it
naturally follows that the use of a reliable
antacid, such as bisurated magnesia, which is
so. frequently prescribed by physicians, will
produce better results than drugs or combina-
tion of drugs. Accordingly I almost invari-
ably advise those who complain of digestive
trouble to get some bisurated magnesia (note
the name carefully, as other forms are not so
suitable for this purpose) from their chemist,
and take half a teaspoonful of the powder, or
two 5-grain compressed tablets, with a little
water after meals. This, by immediately
neutralising the acid and stopping the fer-
mentation, removes the cause of the trouble
and insures normal and healthy digestion.”
—A D VT.

POLITICAL
POINTS
At the conclusion of the Governor-
General’s conference on recruiting in Mel-
bourne Hughes gave the Labor representa-
tives assurances of the total and absolute
abandonment of the idea of compulsory
service, and the Labor representatives gave
their support to a resolution urging the
community “to unite in a whole-hearted
effort to secure the necessary reinforcements
b.v the voluntary system.” In other words,
Samson won’t pull down the building on his
own head and make himself an unrecoguis-
"FANCY NOT WANTIN’ TO GO, BILL?”
able squash among the ruins. On the other
hand he won’t do anything compulsory to
prevent the building falling, but he will
urge himself to a whole-souled effort to
keep it up voluntarily. And if the temple
is steadily canting over despite all volun-
tary propping. Samson pledges himself not
to rush in compulsorily even at the eleventh
hour and shore it up. It may just fall on
him and all his female relations and the
baby and the cat and the other belongings.
There is method in the Hun’s barbarity.
The losses in killed and wounded may be
about equal on both sides. Bqs the Hun idea
is evidently to return his prisoners so ruined
that they will be of little use as soldiers or
workers or as the producers of a healthy new
generation. When the general exchange is
made he will swap as far as possible diseased
idiots who will be a loss and burden to their
people for healthy human beings. The Hun's
telescopic eye looks far.
BRIDGING THE GAP.
THE KAISER : “Hang the cost! The ques-
tion is shall I have enough material?"
Last week some of the papers said either too
much or too little. There was a vague refer-
ence to “the realities of war” being brought
right before the eyes of Australia, and then
the preacher shut up the book with a bang
and the remark that nothing more could be
told at present. All sorts of wild rumors
flew around, and the pacifist and the Ger-
man propagandist said to the slacker who
was beginning to feel uncomfortable: “See
what may happen! After all, you are
doing your duty to Australia by staying
where you can defend it.” The whole
truth, of course, is that what the Wolf and
the See Adler have done other raiders may
do —and perhaps a little more. Nothing
that the Fleet can do can prevent a raider
escaping now and then from the fogs of
the North Sea. But if there were half a
dozen raiders in the Pacific and Indian
oceans it wouldn’t provide a single elegible
with a sufficient excuse for remaining iu
Australia.
From the Gallery
Whereas - the bitterest Laborite is invariably
young, the angriest Nationalist nearly always lias
white whiskers mingling with his fury. The most
implacable opponents of Hughes are the Labor young-
sters Catts, Blakeley, Nicholls and Considine; the
old hands like Tudor, Charlton and Page seem always
willing to allow a fair margin for human error in
any member of any Government. On the other side,
however, yon must go to a promising junior like
Rogers for the large-minded tolerance that, even dis-
sembles pity in friendly laughter, while from the
ancients, like Corser and Foster, you will get those
provocative personalities that set the young bloods
opposite combing their vocabularies for suitable repri-
sals. Falsely presuming that age has made heroes of
them all, the patriarchs are constantly asking the
youthful enemy why they are not at the war. It is
an impertinent question when they are so extremely
guarded about their own efforts in lieu of active
service, but it is difficult to convince them that
"shirker” is a term that can apply to anybody but a
ycung man.
Probably no one was more surprised than
the Ministers themselves at the re-election of
the Bananaland Cabinet. A dead set had
been made against Coyne (llailways), Hux-
ham (Home) and Hardacre (Education),
but they survived the onslaught. The biggest
set-back received by the “industrialist” block
was the defeat of Speaker McCormack for a
portfolio, though he easily retained his wig
and gown. His rejection and Assistant-
Minister Fihelly’s elevation to full port-
ftlioed rank are probably explained by a
split in tbe anti-Ryan section of the party.
The Ryan section consists of the moderates.
The militants, led by Theodore and Fihelly,
have acted together up to the present, but
with the possibility of Ryan’s move into
Federal politics there are two men keenly on
the scent of the Premiership. Theodore is
cold but thorough; Fihelly is more brilliant
but rash. Ryan possesses rare qualities for
leadership in a measure which the others
don’t approach.
Hughes on the Censorship:—
A great deal of the trouble had arisen, not because
of the regulations, but because of the way they were
administered. Had he been a member of the Opposi-
tion possibly he would have said things about the
Censorship a great deal more effective than he had
heard from the other side.
As usual, the Prime Minister is prepared to
sacrifice anybody but himself. For that
part of the Censorship to which the greatest
exception is taken —the political Censorship
—Hughes is almost solely responsible:
Hughes and his Shadow Pearce. And so far
from the administration of this Censorship
being worse than the regulations, the truth
is that the position would have been utterly
intolerable had not the Censorship been
blessed with a large capacity for using its
blind eye.
Brennan of Batman (Vic.) has this propo-
sition before- the Reps.-
In order to assert and maintain the status of sister
Dominions and dependencies whose position is, or
ought to be, analogous to our own, this House records
tlje opinion that the application of Conscription to
Ireland by any authority external to the Irish nation
would be subversive to the settled principles of
democracy.
To which The Bulletin begs to add: —
And inasmuch as the Western District and River-
ina are integral portions of Victoria and N.S.W.
just as Ireland is of the United Kingdom, the
Governments which keep their ports closed by the
denial of adequate railway connections, and con-
script their trade at great loss to themselves in
order to assuage the greed of Sydney and Melbourne,
and thereby cause the population of these districts
to diminish just like that of Ireland, should not
do this sort of thing save by the direct authority
of the people concerned. And by doing it these
Governments show themselves to be base, brutal
and blanky tyrants, and ignorant of the simplest
principles of democracy.
Why should Brennan of Batman make two
bites of a cherry?

fiendish conspiracy” (just as if he had
horns and a tail and blew out flames through
his nose), of planning to establish “industrial

Thus the Sydney Sun, inflating its chest
till the buttons stick out into the next
parish:—■
A few years ago the person who suggested the
raising of a local loan in Australia of £40,000.000
or even of £10,000,000 would have been ridiculed.
But the exigencies created by the war have forced
a realisation by the coUununuitv of its own riches.
Or a realisation of its own wealth in paper
money. The daily papers seem to think that
war and dead bodies have raised the country
to unexampled prosperity.
During the past 3.3 months the Canadian
shipbuilding yards have taken orders from
the British Government for 4(> wooden and
43 steel ships, which means £13,222,000 of
good Bull money to spend in wages. Aus-
tralia has just made a start to build one
ship, which it thinks it may finish in a
couple of years. The difference between
Canada and Australia is that one has High
Protection and the other has Hughes.
Higgs, M.H.11.: —-
The British Government should be told that if the
German colonies were going to keep tiie war going
for an hour longer we did not want them.
Fight over the dog? Certainly not! Hand
the dog back. He is too tired to bite us
to-day. And to-morrow we shall be dead.
So why trouble about to-morrow?
DER POTSDAM GOTT.
The WILD CAT COLUMN
The Gott that we builded at Potsdam was fashioned
a marvel to see.
\Ve framed hinj of Blood and of Iron, no Gold in his
corpus had he;
The tears of the widow and orphan were blent in his
veins, as was meet,
And high was his glance and unfearing, and mighty
the spread of his feet.
We put not a heart in the creature—what use for
such lumber had he?—
But scorn was his gift in abundance and hate for the
land of the Free;
We said to him “Baal, be schrecklich ! Sweep down
on our foes as a flood,
And them who would flout thee and fleer thee, wipe
out the foul sin in their blood!”
And millions and millions adored him and bended
them down at the throne
And hailed him as Moloch and Odin, with tribute of
tear and of groan ;
And children went through the red furnace that
burned at his altar so grim,
And mothers insensate and fathers swelled high the
glad sound of his hymn.
He looked to the East and a ravage swept over the
land as a flame;
He turned to the West and the cities were ashes and
smoke and a name ;
He flashed a red glance on our dearest, they died in
the blast of his breath;
And they who had fashioned and framed him, their
meed was a portion in Death.
The Gott that was fashioned at Potsdam—bei
Himmel, was never the like!
We know, not the day of his fury, the hour when
his vengeance will strike;
One arm hath he hared for the smiting of freemen
with pride overblown,
And one hath he lifted to chasten the serfs whom he
calleth his own.
And day with the smoke of his altars is troubled and
murky and brown,
And night with a winging of horrors drops hell in
the heart of the town ;
While he who was builded at Potsdam looks down
on the mile upon mile
Of ruin and pitiless ravage—and greets his work with
a smile,
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

IN THE SUPREME COURT OF
SOUTH AFRICA.
(Transvaal Provincial Division.)
Before Mr. Justice Bristowe in Chambers.
In the matter of —
HARRY HERBERT ROE Plaintiff
versus
HELEN ROE (born Aulton) Defendant.
Pretoria this 6th day of March 1918.
Upon hearing Counsel for the Plaintiff
and the. evidence adduced in this action for
the restitution by the Defendant of conjugal
rights and in default thereof for dissolution
of the marriage,
The Court orders the Defendant to return
to or receive the Plaintiff on or before the
Ist day of August 1918 failing which to
show cause, if any, to this Court on the 15th
day of August 1918 why the bonds of marri-
age now subsisting between the Defendant
and the Plaintiff shall not be dissolved and
that service of this Order be effected by one
publication in the Union Gazette" and
twice in a Sydney paper Australia.
By the Court,
(Sgd.) F. L. C. BICCARD JUTA,

TAKE NOTICE that this action was on the
Twelfth day of December 1917 commenced against
you and the abovenamed Florence Maud Grasset and
that the Plaintiff by his writ of summons claims a
dissolution of his marriage with Florence Maud
Grasset the defendant herein AND by his petition
filed in this Honorable Court on the Twelfth day
of December 1917 the plaintiff alleges that in the
month of May 1913 and from that month unto the
month of August 1914 you the said Frederick
Charles Coe frequently visited the said Florence
Maud Grasset at Cairns in the State of Queensland
and on divers of such occasions committed adultery
with the said Florence Maud Grasset AND TAKE
NOTICE that the Court has by Order dated the
Fifth day of January 1918 authorised service of the-
said writ and petition on you by insertion of this
Notice not less than twice in each of the following
newspapers; The Cairns Post and the Sydnev
Bulletin AND TAKE NOTICE ALSO that you are
required within six months after the date of the
last insertion of this advertisement to cause an:
appearance to be entered for you at the Registry
of the Supreme Court of Queensland at Townsville
and that in default of your so doing the Plaintiff
may proceed in this action and that judgment may
be given against you in your absence.
