SPORTING
NOTIONS
"NO TOP" WITH
MICK SIMMONS
SURE CATCH TACKLE
There was an announcement last week
that the N.S.W. Government (1) does not intend
to abolish the bookmaker, and (2) proposes to
remove some of the causes of dissatisfaction with
the system under which the totalisator is run, inci-
dentally “popularising” the machine. It can be
admitted that to abolish Ike and leave the tote
as it is known in N. S. Wales would be absurd—
unless the idea is to strangle racing. The whole
business has been botched from first to last, and so
long as the totalisator is viewed by Parliament as
an implement for levying taxation Ike is as safe as
if he were nailed down on his box. Meanwhile the
squeak from the country clubs at having to run a
machine from which the State extracts 7 per cent,
is loud and long, and unless the Ministry is imper-
vious to argument the figure will have to be altered.
The fundamentally wrong thing about the existing
method is in the 60, 20, 20 per cent.. system of
division on a 1-2-3 basis. To everybody concerned
except the State this is ruinous. It is no good to
the racing clubs because of their limited share in
the proceeds (some clubs actually lose money). That
it will bankrupt the punter is as certain as the
multiplication table. Premier Holman is reported to
contemplate setting matters right by having a
straight-out tote as well as a 1-2-3 instrument on
all courses. In the case of 90 per cent, of the clubs
racing in the State this would make the squeaks
louder than ever. In the case of the remaining big
clubs it would be a sound change, provided the place
dividend was on the basis of a flat div. on the three.
B\it having made the change Holman would discover
two things, among others—first, that unless he
abolished Ike the system would not be getting a
fair deal, because in competition the book will beat
the machine; and, next, that if he did give Ike the
couut, the System, assuming that the 7 per cent,
raid continued, would fall in a heap. As long as
two things are done wrong at once there will be a
mess.
♦ * * *
At Canterbury Park (Sydney) on Satur-
day the totalisator churned £11,232; at the ponies
£7762. At the horse meeting favorites arrived
twice; at the other a well-backed midget was in
front every race but one. Some of the place-betting
results were interesting samples of what the public
will risk. Of the 14 horses placed second or third
at Canterbury Park backers laid odds on 10, took
even money twice, took 9 to 2 once, and in the
remaining case lost money. At the ponies, of 24
second and thirds only two returned over 2 to 1,
four paid 6 to 2, there was about even money on
seven, and odds were laid on six. Five times the
backer got less than he put in.
* * * *
“Spreader”: The tote in the Leger at
Sydney pony courses has a way of its own of doing
things. In the Encourage Stakes at Victoria Park
the other day Nona was the best-backed horse in the
big field. Yet his number was bracketed with the
very long chance Swan Queen. As the two nags
belong to different owners (and trainers) it was hard
to see why the few punters who wished to invest on
the outsider were forced to take the dynamite odds
that backers of' the favorite were annexing. The
explanation is that there were not enough indicators
on the machine to give each horse a space, so the
contraption simply bracketed a couple here and there
haphazard. The fun they’re having makes me wake
up in the night to laugh.
* * * *
There was a boots-off rush to get on to a
good thing in the Flying at Canterbury Park (Syd-
ney) on Saturday. But no one was hurt when it
came to collecting. The ex-pony Some Boy (9st.)
and Eulaere (9st. 21b.) were backed for half a ton
or so at 2’s. Then there were heavy wads for Herbie
(Bst. 71b.), who was supposed to have found his
pace again. Someone threw in for all that was
going down to 3’s. At the end the three favorites
were in trouble. At the distance Eulaere looked
almost a certainty, but failed to hold her ground
when two minimum-weight outsiders swooped up
alongside. These two, Tressady Belle and Merv’s
Pride, had the finish to themselves, the former win-
ning by a head. Herbie just cut the fading Eulaere
down in the last stride or two. Tressady Belle,
who was steered by V. Hill, an apprentice, has been
showing good form. Three weeks ago she won over
six furlongs at Kembla with 7st. 71b. in “15.” There
was practically no money for her on Saturday,
though Ike was anxious to lay 20’s. The tote paid
32 to 1.
* * * *•
Dynasty and Mount Alf were both well
backed for the Newcastle Cup last week, but there
was nothing particularly encouraging about their
performances in the Canterbury Handicap on Satur-
day. Both were beaten out of place. Though what
the Dynasty people may have thought of this is
unknown to the writer, the Mount Alf stable cer-
tainly expected something very different, for they
backed him as if he couldn’t lose. Both were up-
sides when the run for the line began, and both
petered out to nothing when the proceedings intensi-
fied. They will have to do a lot better than this to
win at Newcastle. The cash was taken by the Pad-
dington mare Sinfielder (6st. 111 b.), who, after run-
ning into a place at a couple of country meetings,
was given a fly at Randwick last month and arrived
second to the winder of the Novice Handicap. She
is a smart sort of a lady, and; may consider the
.Newcastle Cup, in which she has 6st, 71b., worth
arguing. Bartelle (7st.), a fonr-year-olrl mare by
Bardolph, ran third at Warwick Farm the previous
week, and was just astern of Sipfielder’s withers in
the Canterbury. She ran like a stayer and ought to
be heard from on suburban courses again.
TV hat are some of the nags on Sydney
pony courses chasing? At Rosebery on Saturda}'
one event was cut up into three divisions and another
into four. In each event the prize money on offer
was £l4O. The cutting up meant that the winners
of each division in one event collected £33, the
second nags £8 and the third £5. In the other
the winners got £25, and the placed animals £6 os.
and £3 15s, apiece. Yet there is a' whole mob of
horses in training for races like this. This page
wonders why the owners of the show don’t take all
the money and put up a bag of peanuts for the
animals to dash after.
* * * ♦
Iv. Bracken tops the season’s list of N. S.
Wales jockeys with 23 wins so far, and he seems
likely to stay there. When L. A. Walker took a
holiday he gave Bracken a chance to go on alone.
Walker up till then had steered 20 winners. Bullock
is at the head of the practitioners in Vic. with 28.
Young Dempsey has not been winning anything like
so often of late as he did a while back.
* * * *
How’s this for stiff luck? McCrystal
dreamed that lie arrived at the course too late to see
the Cup run. It was just over, so he asked a by-
stander what was the result. The reply was “Number
21.” Mac kept the number steadily in view until
the meeting came round. Then he planked his shirt
on 21. His language was frightful when he realised
that the stranger of his dream must have said to him
“Number 20 won.”
* * # *
E. ,T. Watt is hauling down the dark-blue-
and-white stripes and packing away the dark-blue cap
—till the war is over. Watt, a heavily-gilded Maori-
lander with big pastoral interests in Bananaland, has
been racing at Rardwick off and on a dozen years or
so. Latterly his luck has not been anything to make
a noise about. A spell may drive the hoodoo off.
When the war peters out Red Pennant may not be
running in Cups, anyway. Meanwhile Watt has a
stud to go on with. Wolawa will be cock o’ the walk
there. All Red, -sire of Desert Gold, has been leased
to a Victorian breeder.
*.\ * * *
The result of the hurdle race at Caulfield
last Saturday was a reminder that the Nationals are
within thinking distance. The event fell to Fastolf,
who figured in the best jumping company last winter
and was reasonably expected to get nearer to the
front this year. He had recently returned to
work after a long spell, and was evidently supposed
to be short of condition. But he was quite fit enough
to win with only 9st. 41b. up. Probably his freshness
was worth more then the muscular condition of some
of the jumpers that have run too frequently of late.
* * * *
The handsome Eurobin had another of his
periodical revivals on Eight Hours Day at Moonee
Valley. With 9st. 81b. on board the gelding won a
six furlongs race in.lmin. I4sec.—a very good per-
formance. The Doncaster winner of two seasons ago
is not yet “aged,” and may be heard of again in the
best company.
* * * *
A steeplechase at Moonee Valley the other
Monday was the end of two out of seven starters.
One broke his neck at a jump, and another hurt his
spine so badly that a vet. killed him on the course.
If the oldest of the steeplechasers had something
more than horse-sense they would reckon themselves
darned lucky to be alive. In this one particular
event the odds against getting killed were only 7
to 2, as it happened. And still it appears that a
punter loses less money at backing horses over the
sticks than in trying to pick winners on the flat.
* * * *
The Baillieus are making it possible for
somebody to acquire a racehorse for a shilling, and
if you’re honest you can’t get a really good one
anywhere for less. Aleconner, whose record includes
firsts in the Standish, Epsom and Bourke Handicaps,
the Williamstown Cup and the October Stakes, has
been presented by the brothers to the Red Cross
and will be raffled under the auspices of the Commer-
cial Travellers’ Association of Victoria. Anybody
with a bob now has a chance of becoming owner of
the offspring of Malster and La France, alleged to be
sound in wind and limb, and insured for £5OO.
* * * *
The other day several hundred yearlings
were sold in Sydney at prices ranging from high to
low. Nearly all these nags have gone into training
stables, and those which take to galloping kindly will
be racing in about five months. Meantime the
results of the current season, which started off in the
same way, can be summed up. About half a dozen
of the current mob of two-year-olds are expected to
furnish into three-year-olds of top class. About
another dozen look like being smart handicappers. A
further dozen or two may be park-stakers that will
pay their way. The rest are either done with
already or soon will be. Some of these two-vear-olds
have been profitable animals. Two or three that were
Brilliant in the spring were all to pieces in the
autumn. Several that were not produced early were
doing best later. More than one in both these sec-
tions has been galloped off its legs. From the
breeders’ point of view the hanging-up of big prizes
for the best of the two-year-olds and the holding of
races every week for juveniles is, of course, a good
tldng. It excites buyers to part with their cash.
But from the point of view of the horse itself the
current practices spell blue murder.- It is true
enough that the racing track is the only place
to test the thoroughbred. But it is also true that
it is a place where the thoroughbred can be butchered.
The scandal of two-year-old racing is more than ripe
for the axe. What is going on is obviously indefen-
sible. Either the business should be stopped or the
number of times a youngster can be started severely
limited. The Australian horse is getting no sort of
chance. This page firmly believes that the continued
success of second-rate imported animals hereabouts
is due in large measure to the outrageous incitement
given by racing clubs to ruin the local product in its
infancy. » * *
Having pushed the big horse-meetings tem-
porarily out of the way, C. W T . Cropper, secretary of
the Australian .Toekey Club, packed his kit last week
and tripped to have another look at Westralia. Before
he succeeded Tom Clibborn in the A.J.C. job nearly
eight years ago, Cropper was running the business
side of the then prosperous Ivalgoorlie R.C. And
when more active than he looks nowadays, he won
the Sydney tennis club captaincy. That was 36
years ago, when the prize cost something in perspira-
tion to win.
They don’t think much in S. Aus. of
Plenty, the five-year-old lately bought in Sydney by
the Adelaide trainer Timmens. Nicely bred, by
Abundance —Style, he was dropped into the Gawle’r
Cup with 7st. 41b., and came home alone. Also he
is in the Adelaide Cup with 6st. 91b.; but the com-
pany there will be very different.
Boxing, which slid off the programme in
Sydney without a splash when the Stadium pulled
down the blinds, is going to be held up on its feet
again. But the proceedings will not have the
glory of old w T hen the Yankee specialist arrived and
seats were a guinea. The tariff is to be about a
couple of bob and the bash limited to 10 rounds.
It may go, but this page fears that the glory of the
wallop cn the point has vanished until the bottle-
holders at Armageddon are passed out. Can anyone
be really interested these times in what befel Mick
and Bill during a few minutes’ unpleasantness?
* * * *
A notable performance was put up by
young Fowler, of P.A.C., in the athletic contest
with S.P.S.C. at Adelaide. Competing in six events
he won the gold medal for each. Previously five
was the highest number of prizes which had fallen
to an individual at any of these meetings. Fowler ;
broke his own record in the 120yds. hurdles, reduc-
ing it to 16 l-ssec.; while his times in the 100yds.
flat and the 300yds. steeplechase were just outside the’
records. In the broad jump he leaped 20ft. 6jin.;
(one effort sufficed to win him this event), and* he
cleared sft. 5f in. in the high jump. His other win
was the 220yds. flat. Out of the 62 5-6 points gained -
by his school he contributed 36.
* * * •
Following are the names of the Brisbane
Grammar football team which visited Sydney in
August, 1914, with some remarks upon what has
happened since: A. S. West (lieut.) ; W. P. Simmonds
(corporal—killed in action 4/10/’l7) ; T. G. Gaydon
(corporal); C. M. Wrench (corporal) ; A. T. Ham-
mond (gunner) ; A. 11. Brown (lieut., twice
wounded) ; G. R. Sumner (private) ; E. B. Freeman
(lieut., wounded) ; A. R. Walton (trooper, wounded) ;
R. S. McNab (gunner) ; P. W. Hopkins (lieut.) ;
T. Lawson (officers’ school) ; A. D. L. Gibson
(trooper); K. B. Fraser, S. Fraser, G. F. Boyce
(completing their medical course before leaving).
Only one player who went to Sydney has not yet
enlisted; and he is reported to be doing so now.
Two members of the team went into camp at Lytton
a few days before the others went south —M. W. R,
Bowman (lieut., wounded) and L. A. A. Forbes, the
captain, who is now completing his medical course.
The full-back—H. C. Cocking—was unable to make
the trip. He is at the Front or on the way. The
only other Ist XV player, who did not make the trip,
was A. S. Patterson. After winning the Military
Medal he was killed in action on August 27, 1916.
Possibly this record can be equalled by other school
teams of 1914. It can hardly be beaten. It is
magnificent.
* * » *
Geoff Maitland, a hefty 17-year-old son of
Sydney’s sporting surgeon H. L. Maitland, is a.
versatile young athlete; but his favorite is rifle-shoot-
ing. This year his best effort was to add to dad’s
sideboard the Ralston Cup, in winning which he
scored 190 points out of 210. He wears the colors of
Sydney Grammar.
* » » *
“Sim”: Game across aboriginal Jerry
Jerome, one-time ped. and champion middleweight
boxer of Bananaland, the other day. Fight enthusi-
asts will remember his sensational battle with Dave
Smith at the Sydney Stadium, when Dave knocked
the black out in the eighteenth round. Jerry was
then at the top of his career. He still retains the
punch that made him famous, as I saw him demon-
strate recently. He was surveying the classic archi-
tecture of a northern city when a big drunk lurched
up and demanded to know what the etc., etc. A
broad grin spread across the abo.’s dial. The drunk
answered the grin with a clout. Jerry languidly
stepped back and produced a sleeping-pill. Jerome
has landed interest in Brisbane, but a paternal
Government has taken him in hand, and he is now
at Frazer Island.
“J.L.H.”: At the Tasmanian Associated
Schools’ recent sports meeting a couple of new'
records for school performances in the Speck were
put up. In the Open Championship R. M. Clark
(Church Grammar) cleared sft. 4jin., and in the
Under Fifteen High Jump J. Burbury (Hutchins)
went over sft. 1 jin.
* * * *
“Bail”: Like their brother ancients in
Sydney, representative cricketers of the long ago had
a bosker time this season in Melbourne. Warwick
Armstrong, who has made centuries against England,
South Africa, N.S.W., Bananaland and South Aus.,
put nearly 20-stone behind the bat, and came out top
with the great average of 139.66 ; Tom Warne (50.1)
was ahead for Carlton; J. Ryder (31.1) topped
Collingwood; F. Baring (40.41) and Peter McAlister
(25.30) were in the lead for East Melbourne; E. A.
Liddicutt (63.6) and L. A. Cody (44.3) led Fitzroy;
I-lartkopf (52.3) was aloft in Northcote. It was up
to them to keep cricket alive, and the old-timers.
took the bat in both hands.
* * * *
“Snicker": Of course some of the best
Maoriland batsmen are in France, but the remark-
ably good bowling average of W. S. Brice (Welling-
ton) is not explained away by that circumstance.
Many good bats are left, and, after all, it is sur-
prising how young players oome on when the chances
are wddened, not only in cricket but in all games.
Brice really can bowl well. He finished up the other
week by taking eight for eight and seven for 10
against Wellington East. East were last year’s
champions, and four of the team were in this year’s
reps., who were unbeaten. During the season Brice
took 93 for 668, Australia’s old friend Crawford 89
for 925. In two rep. matches against Auckland he
got 20 for 174. Taking a line through Crawford’s
figure?* Brice seems to have the goods.
* * * •#
Just to show the youngsters that half a
century of years is no bar to success if you run it
down on a motor-cycle, D. Y. Rennie, who for years
has kept an eye on the B.S.A. business in Australia,
got astride the firm’s machine and scored full points
in the recent Mortlake-Melbourne (Vic.) reliability
spin ; and in the 200-mile speed event finished fourth
with an average of 451 m.p.h. Before he dropped
into Australia Rennie was a first r flight road cyclist
in England.
Sydney football this season will be catered
for more lavishly than in 1917. The Rugby League
menu provides eight clubs (the same as last year) m
the first grade and a full bill in the lower grades, in
the Rugby Union, six clubs (one in excess of last
year) will line up to the whistle; and in the juniors
(under 21) and schoolboys a lio*de of about 6000
will pull on jerseys. There will also be play under
Australian and Soccer rules. The Union officially
kicked off on Saturday. The other brands begin this
week-end.
On Saturday Lily de Beaurepaire (Albert
Park) and Jean Shiminin (St. Kilda S.) battled it
out at the Melbourne City Baths for the title of
champion of the State. On New Year’s Day Lily,
who is Frank’s sister, downed youthful Jean in the
220yds. On March 16 the St. Kilda mermaid had
her revenge and beat Lilian in the 100yds. In
order to settle the argument as to who should have
the title, the Ladies’ Association decided to have a
440yds. championship. The baths were specially
warmed for the occasion. Miss D. Ruhl (St. Kilda)
also had a say—but a very small one —in the con-
test. Lilian and Jean got away well, but the latter
forged ahead and led at the first lap, covering the
first 100yds. in lmin. 22 l-ssec. Thereafterwards
Lily streaked away and left her opponent. The
watches showed 7min. 26sec. In the 100yds. breast-
stroke championship of Vic. Jean Lawson, a 13-year-
old from Albert Park, who was third to Mina Wylie
in the Australian championship, won the title in
lmin. 42sec. by a length and a half from two club-
mates. Jean, who is the Melbourne High School
champion, is a swan in the water—she glides like a
water bird.
•,* * *
“Spot”: Harriers assembled to the number
of 117 at Brighton Beach (Vic.) on Saturday after-
noon, when the 29th annual muster of the metrop.
clubs was held. There was the usual scamper
across country, the amateur peds. being taken over
seven miles in charge of veteran Geo. Blake, former
cross-country champion and Olympic Games runner,
who dug liimself out and showed the youngsters
that he could still run. St. Stephen’s (Richmond)
headed the poll with a turn-out of 25. Carlton had
19 and St. Thomas (Essendon) 15. The war has
shattered the membership of most clubs, with the
result that Essendon (for many years cross-country
champions) could not. send even one member. Mel-
burnians (one of the oldest clubs in Victoria),
Clifton and Northcote, Caulfield and Brunswick also
failed to answer the call when Sec. Harry Smith
asked “How many?” The usual 1000yds. dust-up
at the finish was a veritable scramble. B. J.
Linehan (East Melb.) just hitting the worsted by
a trifle from F. Corrigan (St. Thomas), with P. C.
Carroll (Carlton) third. By far the most of the
men competing were either returned soldiers or
youths under age and ineligibles. The other sort
were plainly shown they were wanted somewhere
else.

There was never a good thing that wasn't
put to a bad use. Belief in Australia’s great
natural resources is a good thing—one of the
best. But eve/ry few years the party poli-
ticians —taken all round the most immoral
class in the community—seize hold of that
good thing an/1 exploit it bv building a boom
upon it. By pushing the country into debt
they can produce the superficial evidences of
prosperity; and then, of course, they are
, statesmen. Presently the fact that the pros-
perity is bogus strikes somebody—generally
somebody who has done some of the lending
—and the ’bubble is pricked. That happened
in Melbourne 20-odd years ago; and it meant
smash for stcores of concerns that had bought
property at inflated prices, or lent far more
money on property than it was honestly
worth. Some of them struggled on to pay
their creditors and save a little for them-
selves ; others in despair sold what was left
to concerns such as the Freehold Assets Co.,
took down ftheir own signboards and tried to
forget. Naturally such cos. as this secured
the assets ' at prices much below the
old book values; and as 'the market recov-
ered, at first because the country got to work
and then because the politicians engineered
another boom, they unloaded at a profit. This
co. has thus 'been able to make a steady 9
p.c. div., and, in addition to the reserves
shown above, It has now £31,177 of expected
profit—that is to say. profit which will be
realised if the sales it has made are com-
pleted on the terms arranged. Last year’s
realised profit was £2834; but the expected
profit was reduced by £2403, which looks as
though very little property was sold during
the 12 months.
The next comparison shows the principal
movements since the present co. was formed :
1908. 1916. 1917.
Liabilities.. .. .. £89,954 £101,826 £83,859
Realisation suspense account 7,460 33,640 31,177
Property . . . . . . 76,438 30,808 30,604
Shares in other cos. . . 6,256 114,338 114,364
Balances owing .. .. 58,440 57,312 37,460
Other assets . . . . 46,589 37,848 39,172
As the assets originally secured have been
realised the co. has bought other wrecks,
mostly represented by shares. The largest
purchase was the Land Mortgage Bank, of
which it owns nearly all the stock. These
cos. nominally carry on business independ-
ently ; and obviously, until one knows how
they are panning out, one does not know
much about the assets behind this co., since
“shares” represent an amount equal to this
co.’s entire capital and reserves. So far as
the £85,000 or thereabouts invested in the
Land Mortgage Bank is concerned, there is
property to show for that much and £25,000
besides; but the time is coming when, owing
to increased taxation, carrying charges will
be heavy, and prospects of unloading pro-
perties at satisfactory prices are not Bright.

Charles Anderson & Co., Ltd., hat
manufacturers (Sydney)—the deficit to start
with represents the intangible item of good-

This is one of the manufacturing cos. that
deserve well of the Commonwealth: it is
covering Australian heads with hats made
by Australian labor from local material.
For about 15 years now the co. has been
making a success of its job, and, incident-
ally, giving the lie to the importers’ libel
that robust Australian labor cannot do as
well in manufactures as the under-paid,
poorly-fed and sweated worker overseas.
Certainly the Australian must be first
trained in new industries, and Australian
labor must be prepared not to raise a mur-
mur against the importation of six, or even
26, experienced hands from overseas fac-
tories if the Commonwealth is to free itself
of the foreign-trade article. The hat-
factories are working under the protection of
a 35 p,c. import duty; but we are not cold-
shouldering the imported headgear to any-
thing like the extent we should. In 1906
we imported hats and caps invoiced at
£504,193; and in 1916. £417,470. This lat-
ter total includes 47,876 dozen felts worth
£135,752, every one of which should have
been made in our own factories.
Still, even though the average Australian
is unconcerned as to whether he has a
foreign or a “Made in Australia” label
pasted in his hat. Chas. Anderson’s Federal
Mills are prospering. Liabilities and more
or less liquid assets have moved this way
in five years
1912. 1916. 1917.
Liabilities ..£33,185 £15,636 £14,546
Assets. —
Stock.. .. 25,459 46,824 55,456
Rook debts . . 18,196 20,158 24,499
Cash, etc. . . 21,495 10,073 295
During the past 12 months £lO,OOO of War
Loan has disappeared, the cash apparently
having been required for the business. How-
ever, in five years the figures show an in-
crease of over £33.000 in the surplus of
more or less liquid assets over liabilities.
That is not bad going.

IMPORTANT.
Look to Your Tooth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them. clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Ppst free. 1/3. Free sample posted.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped,
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Phil Q.: The surest way of getting MS.

All ye men of Australia,

That he doth'never cease,
After he once hath bitten,
’Til lie’s bitten off his piece.
Now the. British Bulldog’s bitten
As much as he can chew —
A bite of the German Empire,
And sore he needeth you.
Will you let the eagles tear him?
Let them rend him beak and claw?
Just because you funk it,
Just becabse you funk the war?

Where the policemen watch the waters beneath

“Melb”: Re G. B. Post and “The Mas-
querader”—the title under which “John Chil-
cote, was reprinted in America. Mrs.
Thurston, authoress of the novel, was a Miss
Madden, born in Cork. 1875. Related to Vic-
toria’s late Chief Justice Madden, whose
father began business as a solicitor in Cork,
Miss Madden married a novelist named
Thurston in 1901. She had then no thoughts
of writing, being noted rather as horsewoman
and swimmer. After her big success in 1904.
she published four ether novels, but none
caught on. In 1910 she divorced her husband
for cruelty, and next year she died. Thurs-
ton went on writing novels.
“Harrison O.” : Guy Bates Post has been
telling us how wonderfully “fortunate” Aus-
tralian theatregoers are in getting only “the
best of the hundreds of plays that are pro-
duced yearly”:—•
Americans have their pleasure in theatre-going
diluted by having to take the bad with the good.
After the sifting out has been done Australians
get the pick of the successes without being troubled
to any extent by the failures.
The chief effect of the “sifting out” is to
deprive Australians of the opportunity of
seeing plays which have been successful
artistically, but only moderately so finan-
cially ; and it is to the credit of our theatre-
goers that they occasionally show their re-
sentment by staying away in large numbers
from things like “A Little Bit of Fluff” and
“Fair and Warmer,” which were big over-
seas booms. The “sifting out” process has
given us dreadful things—failures from
every point of view, so far as Australia is
concerned —like “Baby Mine,” “Over There,”
“The Cinderella Man,” “The Woman,” “The
JLv a 1 n 1) o w, and
many more, while
depriving us of
some of the best,
not only of Sliaw.
Galsworthy and
writers of their
calibre, but even of
Pinero, Barrie,
Vachell and other
commercially - suc-
'cessful lesser lights.
If booksellers took
upon themselves a
simi 1 a r “sifting
out” we could rely
upon getting the
latest howling suc-
cesses of ’A 11
Caine, Char 1 e s
Garbage and Gene
Something Torter,
but would have no
chance of making
the acquaintance of
Galsworthy, Mase-
field, Thomas Burke
or Compton
Mackenzie. And if
would-be intelligent
readers were then
told that they ought
to give three cheers
for the high-minded
book-importers who
“De Luxe Annie," at
Sydney Palace.

Mrs. Gilmore, the
garrulous hoarding-

only let them have what was good for them,
they would naturally consider that insult
was being added to injury.
“Hamer’: Here, at the Front, the Aus-
tralians, eager as ever for amusement, rush
the few shows available. There is any amount
of talent forthcoming amongst the soldiers to
keep them going. The “Kangaroos” and the
Kookaburras” are two lively vaudeville
parties in our sector; and there are picture
shows, besides Y.M.C.A. entertainments and
lectures. They are all crowded ; and some of
the shows find it necessary to give two per-
formances—one commencing at 5 p.m. and
another after 7. There hasn’t been a play
put on since I’ve been handy, although,
knowing of several actors who are here-
abouts, I’m at present seeing if one can be
arranged. Australian writers who have
bright, laughable, broad-humored plays on
band might do worse than send them over to
somebody who would help the cause of
amusement along. So keen are some of the
men on any entertainment that comes to dis-
tract them that I have known men fresh from
the line finish up at “the show” after a
clean-up and a feed of fried eggs.
“Fax”: “The Madras House” won’t be
stolen from the Repertory to grace the com-
mercial stage in Australia. Two perform-
ances of Granville Barker’s comedy at Mel-
bourne Playhouse left the proper relations
between the sexes in the same old doubt;
and, seeing that there is no “sleeping in”
system at Melbourne drapery stores, the dis-
cussions that gather around the indiscretion
of Miss Yates sound more absurd than they
would seem even to London shopgirls. Any-
how, the idea of a drapery boss holding 'a
court of inquiry into the charge of kissing
a shopman, brought against Miss Yates by a
grim housekeeper, is a ridiculous excuse for
dragging four types into a repertory play.
Bernard Shaw excuses any sort of false situ-
ation by the impishness of his humor; Gran-
ville Barker doesn’t. In this play he is at his
best as a satirist of the querulous, boresome
wife who had lived in hope that her gay old
husband would at last return to his allegi-
ance and give her the opportunity to “close
his eyes in death.”
“Ino”: I was at a circus in aN. S. Wales
country town a few nights ago and watched
the way the amuse-
ment tax was
worked. The
charges for admis-
sion were 2s. 2d.
and 3s. 3d., the
pence being war-
tax, they said.
There were no
stamps on the tick-
ets or with them.
The tickets were
sold at a waggon
and taken at the
door. There was
no visible reason,
why the same tick-
ets could not be
used a dozen times.
I asked the local
policeman whether
it was his job to
watch the tax. He
told me he had
nothing to do with
it. A few nights
before, at a picture
show in the same
town, no tickets
were issued. The
doorkeeper took the
money and one
passed in. I could
not discover anyone
keeping tally. I take
it the showmen
collect the tax from

A note from E. C. Davidson, director of
“the Australian National Theatre” :
The prize offered by the A.ST.T. for the best play
falls to “The Moon Child,” by Jasper Wood, of
Perth. It is written in a slightly archaic form,
without much strong dramatic action, depending
rather on its rhythm of motion, color and music of
words. A most original play, and if re-written for
the commercial stage would make a pleasing addi-
tion to the Australian list. The following quote
from Poetry (America) regarding Cloyd Head’s
“Grotesques,” produced at the Little Theatre,
Chicago, is rather apt of “The Moon Child”: “If I
give this production precedence over Ibsen, Strind-
berg, Schnitzler, over Wilde, Synge and Yeats, over
Shaw and Allan Mohkhouse, over even the beautiful
productions of ‘The Trojan Women’ and ‘Medea,’
it is because the special purpose of the Little
Theatre seems to me to he to produce the modern and
native thing, to try the immediate experiment, rather
than to present old plays or foreign plays, which, in
many cases, have had other local productions.” The
second place was given to “The Gathering of Gold,”
by Chas. Carter (“Kavta” he prefers), of Mel-
bourne. This is a melodrama on safe lines; the
earlier part goes well enough, but it tapers off in
the latter part. It gains second place mainly by
reason of its acting qualities. I wish Australians
to believe we are perfectly sincere in this pioneer-
ing work. It is not a hobby—it is a religion. “Out
of pure love of the Art,” with little thought of self
or pelf, we are doing this pioneer experimental work,
which the professionals, pre-oceupied with both,
refuse to do.
Dr. Nil”: “J.F.11.” (B. 18/4/’18), writ-
ing from London of Ruhleben, “the big camp
on the Berlin Racecourse,” says that the
prisoners there, “including many Aus-
tralians,” are producing a different play
every week, and gives particulars as to the
authors, etc. He goes on to say: “Now
they are appealing to us here in London for
fresh material.” All this is bewildering to me,
who have only heard of Ruhleben through
the medical journals as a place where Eng-
lish and Australasian prisoners were deliber-
ately infected with typhus, typhoid and other
contagious diseases, and left to die thereof
without medical attendance, and were also
denied sufficient clothing and bedding to pre-
vent them from dying of sheer exposure and
cold ! Is “J.F.H.” sure that the “appeals”
are not German camouflage?
Dorothy Brunton is coming back from
America. She finds that there are worse
places than home. Her success in Australia
was immense. The ’Murkans did not take so
violently to the little lady.
From a girl in New York :
Charles Schwabe, millionaire, was “tickled to
death” when he heard about Melba’s Australian sing-
ing class. He insisted upon giving a scholarship, and
the cheque which he handed over to the Dame was big
enough to last two years. Peggy Centre, from Hono-
lulu, is to have the handsome gift. Last Sunday
Stella Power, chaperoned by Melba, sang amidst the
glories of the Schwabe home. There were three
millionaires to the square rod, and they all applauded
violently. Just when Stella was going home the host
took her aside and gently thrust a 500-dollar bill
into her hand. It was his way of saying he liked the
song.
k red Collier, having made a “go” of his
small popular concerts last winter, has
larger ideas for this year. He offers Satur-
day ‘Chappell” concerts in the Auditorium,
with local stars on the programme, and feels
certain that he will launch some future
prima-donnas.

ANY SUBURBAN WIFE AT ANY PICTURE-SHOW.
The WILD CAT COLUMN
We live a humdrum sort of life,
Devoid of worry or of strife;
I am, I hope, a faithful wife,
My husband’s love requiting;
But when each Thursday night we gaze
At our suburban picture-plays,
I oft regret that movie-ways
Are so much more exciting.
To my regret, I’ve never been
Importuned by a lover keen
To fly with him and be his queen—
Of course, I’d not be willing.
But if he chased me round with zest,
And strained me fainting to his breast
At midnight, it must be confessed
I’d find it rather thrilling.
I’ve never found a millionaire
Insisting I his rooms should share—-
And if he did, I wouldn’t dare:
I love my husband-man more.
Though to those rooms I’d like to go
At one a.nj. to tell him so,
And strike an attitude. .. .but, no!
What would they think in Stanmore?
Though if I went, be sure I’d go
In something clinging close, cut low;
For when you give your shape a show
No villain dares to hurt you.
I’ve noticed when the heroine
(Joes out “That Night” to make a scene,
It’s always in her erfipe de chine
She best protects her virtue.
I’m never asked to sacrifice
My virtue, as the villain’s price,
To save my husband. It’s not nice
To think of, but I’m nettled
That though our bills we cannot pay,
No handsome grocer calls to say
That if I’ll be a trifle gay
He’ll call the month’s bill settled.
I don’t unfaithful want to be;
And if the iceman wickedly
Declared his fiery love for me,
I’m sure I’d not return it.
I wouldn’t even sell my soul
To Don Juan who brings the coal.
Though no one wants me for that role
I’d love the chance to spurn it!
N.S.W.
A. H. A.
THE LOST DASH.
The WILD CAT COLUMN
“Tbe dashing air that goes with tights.”
—Sundry Showman.
Five years ago I saw her prance
And skip and frisk across the stage ;
Acknowledged leader of the dance,
I'o me she earned the whole crowd’s wage.
The way she spoke her one lone line
A lesson was to neophytes ;
She showed a form acclaimed divine,
And had —how could she fail to shine?
The dashing air that goes with tights.
Her rise was rapid ; in a year
A speaking part was hers. More joy
Came to the front seats: ‘‘What a dear,
That super-shapely second ‘boy’!”
And when the principal fell ill
My heroine to topmost heights
Soared easily ; she filled the bill,
And filled the clothes, retaining still
The dashing air that goes with tights.
Then she “retired.” The well-worn wheeze
In her case marriage meant. To whom ?
For such a bride whoever sees
On this earth a deserving ’groom?
We never knew the lucky beau;
Disconsolate we sat o’ nights,
Watching her tame successor’s show—
A girl all pads, who lacked the “go,”
The, dashing air that goes with tights.
There is a pub gt Dingo Flats.
In the bar-parlor, on the wall,
A photo hangs, a picture that’s
The pride of Dingo Flat-ites all:
The “Boy” ! The draw of yesterday
In drawing beer to-day delights!
She’s stout and slow, the erstwhile fay,
And we may guess has lost for aye
The dashing air that goes with tights.
VICTORIA.
E. T.

ESTABLISHED 1852


London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Bea uty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olga Amsdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
Grey Hair —Home Remedy.
There are plenty of reasons why grey hair
is not desirable, and plenty of reasons why
hair dyes should not be used. But, on the
other hand, there is no reason why you should
have grey hair if you do not want it. To turn
the hair back to a natural color is really a
very simple matter. One lias only to,get from
the chemist two ounces of concentrate of tam-
malite and mix it with three ounces of bay
rum. Apply to the hair with a small sponge
for a few nights and the greyness will gradu-
ally disappear. This liquid is not sticky or
greasy, and-does not injure the hair in any
way. It has been used for generations with
most satisfactory results by those who have
known the formula.
\ Blackheads Fly Away.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into gen-
eral use in the boudoir. It is very simple,
harmless and pleasant. Drop a stymol tablet
obtained at the chemist’s, in a tumbler full of
hot water. After the effervescence has sub-
sided bathe the face in the liquid, using a
small sponge or soft cloth. In a few minutes
dry the face and the offensive blackheads will
come right off on the towel. Also the large
oily pores immediately close up and efface'
themselves naturally. The greasiness dis-
appears and the sltin is left smooth, soft and
cool. This simple treatment is then repeated
a few times at. intervals of four or five days
to ensure the permanence of the result.
Emergency Complexions.
, Even a complexion which Is hopelessly dull
or greasy can be made beautiful in a moment
by a perfectly harmless home recipe. If you
have no cleininite in the house, get about an
ounce from your chemist, and add only
enough water to dissolve it. The process de-
fies detection, and the result is an immediate
appearance of velvety, youthful “bloom”
which every woman desires. The effect will
last all day under the most trying conditions,
indoors or out, and renders powdering quite
unnecessary. This simple substance is also
very good for the skin, and no possible harm
can come from its regular use.
Effectually Killing Superfluous Hair.
Many women know how to remove ugly
growths of superfluous hair temporarily, but
few know how to remove it permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered plieminol may be
used. Get about an ounce from your chemist
and apply a little directly to the objection-
able hair. The purpose of the recommended
treatment is not merely to remove the super-
fluous hair instantly, but also to kill the hair
roots completely in a comparatively short
time.
How Nature Makes New Complexions.
It is well known that the human skin is
constantly undergoing a tearing down and
building up jjrocess. With advancing years or
waning vitality this tissue-change lags: the
lifeless, soiled surface skin stays on so long
that its owner gets a “poor complexion.”
Commonsense tells us this dead skin cannot
be enlivened or beautified by any cosmetic,
lotion or powder. The natural thing to do is
to actually remove the offensive skin—remove
the bad complexion. It has been found that
ordinary mercolized wax completely absorbs
the devitalised skin, in minute particles, so
gently, gradually, as to cause no inconveni-
ence. Mercolized wax, wdiich any chemist can
supply, is put on at night like cold cream,
and washed off in the morning. If you would
have a brilliantly beautiful and youthful com-
plexion, just use this simple treatment.
How to Have Thick anti Pretty Hair.
Soaps and artificial shampoos ruin many
beautiful heads of hair. Few people know
that a teaspoonful of good stallax dissolved
m a cup of hot water has a natural affinity
for the hair and makes the most delightful
shampoo imaginable. It leaves the hair bril-
liant, soft and wavy, cleanses the scalp com-
pletely, and greatly stimulates the hair-
growth. The only drawback is that stallax
seems rather expensive. It comes to the
chemist only in sealed quarter-pound pack-
ages, which retail at about four shillings.
However, as this is sufficient for 25 or 30
shampoos, it really works out very cheaply
in the end. ' [4

heroic, but to suffer needlessly
is idiotic. While most sufferers
regard pain as due to the
dispensation of Providence,

neglect—the neglect of simple
rules of health.
OF all rheumatic afflictions acute lumbago is the
most painful. It arises from a gradual accumu-
lation of excess uric acid in the system ; the muscles of
the lower back become impregnated until the muscu-
lar fibres stiffen and every movement causes agony.

morning cleanses the system of excess uric acid, braces
the nerves and gives to the user the daily enjoyment
of natural vigorous health. Get the Kruschen habit.

Fred Johns, the biographer, has been
digging up the history of the Buffalo, the
vessel which brought Governor Hindmarsh
to found S. Aus. -on the historic 28th of
December, 1836. She knew much more of
Sydney Cove than of Holdfast Bay, having
been on duty there in the'days of Hunter and
King. Also she brought to King George’s
Sound (W.A.) the first Government Resi-
dent, Spencer, in 1833. She was finally
wrecked in Mercury Bay (M.L.) in 1840,
and a piece of her oak has long been in the
possession of Owen Smyth, of Adelaide. He
lately gave it to Johns, who had it made
into a baton for presentation to some lodge
of which he is Grand Llama.
“Adelaide”: The Bulletin remarks
(11/4/’18) that South Aus. has been first
to “experiment with nearly all the things
that a conference of very earnest young
Sunday-school superintendents would be
likely to advocate.” Three most striking in-
stances are not mentioned. It has not been
possible to buy a drink (lawfully) in S.
Aus. at any hour on Sunday for —well, it
would puzzle most Adelaide citizens to recall
the date of that experiment. And Isaac
Shortodds and Flossie, have both long ago
been abolished by Act of Parliament. I
don’t say they are extinct; but they are
both equally and absolutely unlawful.
“Jim Carew”: I tried again to-day for
the nth time to enlist. The man who tested
my eyesight was not satisfied with .me and
took me to the M.O. He examined my eyes
again and told ine I was rejected on account
of defective vision; but he would not give
me a certificate, unless I produced a certi-
ficate from the eye specialist who had pre-
viously attended me. I growled, but it was
no use. To procure the necessary certifi-
cate would cost me one or two guineas.
Why should I be called upon to pay this?
Am I, because I refuse to pay a guinea or
two, to have nothing to show that I am not
a cold-foot?
“Sparrowhawk”: Ted Dyson founded his
fancy on fact when he wrote that recent
Bulletin verse about the battlefield
reconnoitre of the ex-pushite and the ex-
policeman. In the' Ballarat East (Vie.)
police court a ’tec. stated that the man in
the dock was one of the new edition of the
Eureka Push. “Where are the old ones?”
asked his Washup. “They’ve all gone to
the war,” was the reply; “and so have
practically all the other pushes.”
“Copabella” writes: —
The shire of Upper Yarra (Vic.), where the big
timber sprouts, is appealing to its brother shires to
help it in securing an independent railway-complaints
tribunal to inquire into losses-in-transit, short-
deliveries and the difficulty waybaekers experience in
getting a fair deal in the way of trucks for their
produce. A clause in Upper Yarra’s round-robin dis-
closes the undesirable colored person in the wood-
pile. The Upper Yarra folk say that some municipal
action is necessary to bring about reform, as indivi-
duals dare not make complaint for fear of victimisa-
tion t It is typical of the conduct of Public Enter-
prise by Barnacle and Stiltstalking that the com-
munity’s own unfortunate customer, losing money
daily over freight-delays and demurrages, dare not
enter his complaint for fear that where he gets no
trucks now he will get still less in the future.
“The Old Soldier”: Re long-range guns.
During the siege of Gibraltar (1779-1783)
General George Augustus Elliot got the
greatest recorded range out of a battery of
long 32-pr. smooth-bores, with which he
destroyed the Spanish naval dockyard at
Algeziras, six miles away, across the bay.
That range has never been beaten by smooth-
bores.
Interesting possibilities are attached to a
Melbourne case of manslaughter. An able-
bodied laborer had a row with another
fellow outside a pub, just after closing time,
and knocked him down so hard that he died
of the fall. A friend of the deceased spoke
of him as one Greaves, electrical engineer,
from Washington, U.S.A. Amongst papers
found in his possession were two seamen’s
discharges in the name of A. Rief and
Adolphus Rief. The dead man is said to
have recently been employed on Australian
coastal steamers, and it is to be hoped that
the connection, if any, between the alleged
Greaves from Washington and Adolphus
Rief, the seaman, will be traced.
“Umntu” introduces another “General”:
Hertzog, whose fiery speech at the Paarl (S’Africa)
was an attempt to regain complete leadership of the
rebel party, no longer receives the courtesy title of
“General” in that country, though English and Aus-
tralian papers continue to give it to him. The
declaration of De Wet at the outbreak of the 1914
rebellion,' that Hertzog was not a soldier—that he
was a lawyer, and would fight for them in Parlia-
ment-made the continuance of the title farcical.
Since the abortive rebellion Hertzog has been more
or less in the shade. He failed the rebels as a
lawyer, since > he could not get one Dutch judge to
take his view that an “armed protest” was a legal
way of carrying on the political party game, nor
could he get any members of Parliament, outside his
own section, to side with him ; and the rebel leaders
did not escape various terms of imprisonment. It
was the talk of the Johannesburg clubs at the time
of their trial that De Wet had said he would take
his punishment like a man, but that he would dearly
love to have five minutes with Hertzog and a gun.
“Wart”: Maybe, as “O’Phimerty” (B.
14/3/’18) says, I was wrong in credit-
ing Dogherty, the hangman, with a record
bag of nine in one day; and maybe
his best string was 13. I was partly
going on some remarks made by Chap-
lain Bedford, who, by the way, pro-
bably holds a world’s record himself. In
a speech in Hobart .in 1832, he said he had
attended between 300 and 400 executions,
and he continued to be chaplain for some
years after that. When he was asked to
examine a new gallows which had just been
built, he measured it and remarked: “If
there are nine of them they will hang in
comfort, but 10 will be a crowd.” So if
Dogherty finished off 13 in one day they
must have been uncomfortably crowded or
else the gallows must have worked two
shifts.

The Inky Way.
“Copy Boy”: It is the fervent hope of the
press gang that Hughes will return from his
next visit to the duchesses with a sweeter
temper and better manners. It is doubtful
if any man in a public position has ever
treated journalists more rudely than Hughes.
Some allowance is made for him on account
of his innards, but a good deal of his offen-
siveness springs from his diseased egotism.
By comparison the discourtesies which
caused the daily paper men on the State
departmental round to combine in sending
Tommy Bent to Coventry on several occa-
sions were the momentary lapses of a
Chesterfield. /
* * * *
“Rosna” (London) : When Lieut. “Philo”
Harris produced the first number of Aussie,
he reckoned he would be doing very well
if he sold out the 10,000 copies; but the
issue was rushed and for the next chirrup
of the little wattle-colored infant no less
than 60,000 orders were booked. Harris
was editor of one of the first troopship
papers, The Honk. One of his subs, then
was Lieut. Imlay, now a Colonel. This
war is a fast cat.
* * * *
“Billy T.”: In connection with the re-
newal of orders to artists for oil paintings
for the Commonwealth Memorial Gallery,
the ruling powers display the egregious
egotism of elected persons in their conclu-
sion that none but politicians deserve to
come in. There is David Syme, for in-
stance, and there are one or two other big-
minded Australian journalists, who did more
for Australian Federation than all the mem-
bers of the present Ministry lumped. One
is impelled to ask where does Hughes come
in as a father of Federation, and what is
the excuse for including the G.-G. in a gal-
lery that was established to commemorate
the builders of the Commonwealth?
There was something of a re-union at a
Melbourne official lunch the other day, when
three men who 25 years ago ran the three
farthest-out papers in N. S. Wales found
themselves in a bunch—Millen, Minister for
Repatriation, then editor of the Rourke
thunderer; Heney, now editor of the S. M.
Herald, then of the Wilcannia Grazier ;
and another Sydney editor who made his
crust on the Broken Hill Miner when the
world was a quarter-century more innocent.
* * * *
“Chink”: Apropos the observations of
“V.” (B. 4/4/’18) about journalists Ran-
dolph Bedford and 11. E. Sizer, the remark
that Sizer strikes “V” as a joke suggests a
lopsided sense of humor. With 10 years of
knocking about Australia to give "him an
affection for the land, the new member for
Nundah showed punch enough to fight for
it on Gallipoli and appreciation enough to
return to it when the Turk blew him out.
Moreover, a man who could substantially
reduce McDonald’s majority in Kennedy,
and follow this up by puliing off a State
seat with a big margin to spare, doesn’t
strike me as being far out of touch with
“Australian conditions and ideals.” Cer-
tainly there was a vast difference in the jobs
taken on by Sizer and Bedford. The former
was up against a political nonentity for
Nundah; but in whiskery, genial old Donald
Gunn* Randolph tackled the breeziest and
best battler for the bush folk that the
Bananaland Parliament knows.
* * * *
“ J.F.H.”: Latest issue of News of the
World, a London weekly, claiming a circu-
lation of between two and three millions,
has a page proclaiming the virtues of a hair-
wash. It says £ll5O was the price of it, and
boasts that this is the “largest amount
ever paid for the single insertion of an advt.
in any newspaper.”
LO THE POOR ROUNDSMAN!
The WILD CAT COLUMN
“A quarter,” says the Chief of Staff,
“And not another line!”
They cut it to a paragraph,
And tell me not to whine:
It might have made, in telling phrase,
A column spread, or more ;
But what’s one murder nowadays,
When all the wmrld’s at war?
I would I were the Sporting Man 1
However tight is space,
The paper’s like a pelican
To give his notes a place:
His brimming Cups the public still
Convey unto their lips,
The vacuum in their heads to fill
With scratchings and with tips.
Oh, War and Sport! Oh, Sport and War 1
Between these two there is
No room to practise as of yore
The Roundsman’s stirring biz.
The biggest fire that ever flamed
Is not a circumstance
To something in the betting named
At Randwick or in Prance.
But still I suffer not alone:
Beneath the Censor’s spell
“Commercial” is a naked bone,
And Shipping’s gone to Hell.
Though art is long, yet space is short
For fakes and facts and shams,
When half the staff attends to sport
And half to cablegrams.
A footnote on the super-gun
Does more to bring content
To those for whom the paper’s run
Than an embezzlement.
The Rounds are dead: if Doomsday came,
Its crack would leave no scar;
And in the Book they’d log my name
For “Judgment Day—(ft par.”
VICTOItU.
ROTUNDA.
“PEACE HATH HER VICTORIES.”
The WILD CAT COLUMN
I wrote some verses when a lad
And later judged them very bad.
Though they were very dear to me
I tore them into pieces three
And cast them from the window ledge
Because they set my teeth on edge.
I do not want to brag or blow
But it was worth a D.S.O.
K.s.w.

still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.

membrane to a healthy action, and cleanses and invigorates
the whole digestive tract.

the direct auspices of
Mi*. J. 0. Williamson
himself, and her progress
since then has delighted
all her many admirers.

peared with marked suc-
cess as Virginia Bulli-
vant in “Grumpy,” the
play which has become
famous throughout Aus-
tralasia. Miss Cumming
writes us as follows:
“I want to tell you
what I think about
your Remedy for
Coughs and Colds—
Dr. Sheldon’s New Dis-
covery. At various
times I have tried
many things when
troubled with a cold,
and I want to say that
you have the best by
far of any I have ever
come across.
“It lias always cured
me in a remarkably
short time. I am only
too glad to add my
testimony and wish
you every success with
it.

The Sailer.
TO MISS ELEANOR MORDAUNT.
The WILD CAT COLUMN
OH, I would be in a sailing ship, no
engine ship for me ;
Oh, I would be in a sailing ship,
the sister of the sea!
With following wind and wave astern, a-
running fast and free,
And stuns’ls set and scuppers wet—that’s
where I want to be!
Oh, I would be in a bonnie barque, close-
hauled, in half a gale,
With every inch of canvas spread ; yes, every
rag of sail!
The wind a-singing up aloft, through stay
and brace and brail,
And sheets and tacks as tight as wax, and
foam along the rail!
Oh, I would be in a brigantine, hove-to, aft
Table Bay,
Where Mollie-Auks and Albatross and
Mother Careys play;
A-swinging through the live long night and
through the live long day!
If I must drown let me go down with a
sailing ship, I say !
SOUTH AUSTRALIA.
C. H. SOUTER.
Wax and Clay.
The WILD CAT COLUMN
THERE had been trouble—over and
above monetary difficulty—in the
Great Australian Dramatic Company.
Aladdin Beddulfson found his forces at Uran-
quinty with another general in possession of
the field, this being the error of Marshal
Montgomery, the advance agent—the sort of
error which made Napoleon lose Waterloo by
reason of the unexpected arrival of the
Prussians. The enemy in this case was
Hamstinger’s Waxworks and Vaudeville
Company, which often performed in a tent,
but was now in possession of the Shire Hall.
Montgomery had booked the less important
hall —Abbott’s- —and had further complicated
matters by billing the town for the second
night of Hamstinger’s two-night season. Mr.
Hamstinger was a grave, melancholy man of
fair complexion and long yellow Viking mous-
taches, enjoying his own. tribulations
solemnly, punctuating the description of his
adversities with something between laughter
and snivelling.
Montgomery had left them with a night
vacant, and that was the opening night of
Hamstingef’s Waxworks and vaudeville.
Hamstinger extended to the Great Australian
Dramatic Company the courtesy of “The Pro-
fession.” Its members visited the Shire Hall,
and prior to the vaudeville turns inspected
the Waxworks. It consisted of a score of
dummy figures-—mere pegs to hang clothes on
- —thirty heads, interchangeable on the dummy
figures aforesaid, and much wardrobe.
Hamstinger’s opening night was very poor.
“Dot bore thad five pouds id,” said Trewhella
Jordan, gloomily. “A dice place to brig a
legitibate cobady, I dod’t thigk.”
“There’s a great make-up,” cried Birdie,
shrilly, pointing to the wigged, knee-breeched
figure purporting to represent Robespierre.
She had addressed Saxby Leicester, but he
was deeply interested in the figure of Lord
Beaconsfield, or rather in the dress-suit
which covered it.
“That’s Gladstone,” explained the morose
Hamstinger. “An’ that's Ned Kelly. ’Ere
we have Mary Queen o’ Scots before her
marriage with Philip o’ Spain. That’s Deem-
ing ! No! not that one—that’s the Lord
Mayor. There’s President Garfield an’ the
Anarchists that asinated him. Here we have
Florence Nightingale, Henery Parkes, Daniel
O’Connell and Lord Edward Fitzgerald. On
the other side is Captain Cook, who discov-
ered Australia just before the First Fleet.
This group, represents the Judgment of Solo-
mon. .. .’Scuse me, the orchestra’s stopped.”
He put a new needle in the gramophone
arm, wound up the clock-work and restarted
the old record —a peculiarly lugubrious re-
production from “The Bohemian Girl.”
“When other lips an’ other tarts,” he said
in explanation, “by Mick W. Balfe. There’s
a lot of Irish here to-night. An’ now, Mr.
Bluffson, if you’ll sit down an’ take your
company we’ll get to the vaudeville. Take
the front row. None o’ this hungry town
push have paid more than two bob, and the
front seats look empty,”
He showed them the front row where they
were so neighborless that, as Aladdin said,
they felt as lonely as an egg in a cathedral.
Hamstinger then walked to the left side of
the hall, while Birdie gave him sotto vocc
stage directions, reversing positions against
the auditorium.
“Straw Mo goes right—speaks to ole bloke
what’s seen better days.”
He had. They saw the old man addressed
by H&mstinger lift his head and show a face
until then hidden by an old-style Paris hat—
its silk brushed the wrong way by weather
and neglect. When he stood up to reply to
Hamstinger they saw his clothes —of a past
magnificence and present condition to suit the
hat.
“That there frock-coat was just the thing
when Victoria was crowned,” said the cruel
and irrepressible Birdie. “An’ his pants was
at Eureka Stockade.”
“Be quiet,” whispered Aladdin, for the
man himself was terribly pathetic.
“Go on yourself,” replied Birdie impu-
dently. “That ole hat belongs to the vintage
of 1878.”
“If you don’t stop buttin’ it,” threatened
Saxby, “we’ll put you in the Chamber of
Horrors and label you Ananias and Sap-
phira.”
The old man, carrying his battered hat at
his hip. moved towards the little stage, and
they saw him walking very delicately, as if
he were afraid of falling. His slight figure
was defined in all its thinness by the lightly-
buttoned frock-coat; its seams shining and
its spaces dull. The silk had been worn off
the buttons so that they shone too. Silvered
hair and a silvered beard accentuated the
venerableness of the face. The Waxworks
show had not been able to rob him, even in
age, of his inherent dignity—a dignity which
was mainly kindliness and simplicity, and so
had not suffered by adversities which would
have made mere pompousness a craven long
ago. As he walked to the stage they saw
that he was ill. A dry little cough shook his
spareness, and when he mounted the four
wooden steps from the auditorium he held his
hand to his left breast as if merely breathing
were pain.
Birdie opened her mouth for another wit-
ticism, but all her fellow-strollers turned on
her irritably and she became silent. The old
man crossed the stage before the curtain, and
entered at the little door on the prompt side
of the proscenium.
Hamstinger followed him, having adjusted
the gramophone to a very welcome silence. A
minute later the curtain rose in folds as
slowly and indecisively as if a child were
furling it. And then they knew that the
slight old man was at the curtain ropes,
and that he was very, very weak.
There were two pairs of wings of hessian,
with wall-paper pasted over it, and a back-
cloth showing an impossible gum-tree like a
grape-vine, framing a castle with Lake Como
in the foreground, and two blobs of red paint
pretending to be Neapolitan fishermen. Right
centre, a four-square painted canvas frame
like a small bathing-box, and centre and a
little up-stage a dressing-table, trying to hide
itself under a red suburban tablecloth. On
the table two wooden bars shaped like A and
bearing graduated lengths of metal; and two
little wooden mallets. Also and behind the
wooden bars, a cornet of very brilliant brass.
“Oh, my stars!” said Birdie, unchecked
now that poor old Fathos was hidden in tlje
prompt entrance. “Straw Mo has graciously
consented to do a cavatina on the sillyphone,”
Hamstinger had. lie graciously eriterecj ;
as proud and self-conscious as if he were
prime tenor at La Seal a, singing a new opera
before the crowned faces of Europe. He
walked majestically to his place behind the
table, seized a mallet in either hand, and
played most of the music which was newest
when gold was first discovered at Ballarat.
“Hold me,” said Birdie, “Here’s all the
tunes that’s got whiskers on ’em.”
With a more than becoming reverence for
his job, Mr. Hamstinger played “The Blue
Bells of Scotland,” “The Wearing of the
Green,” “Ring the Bell. Watchman,” and
“Silver Threads Among the Gold.”
Answering some misguided applause from
the boys in the back seats, he played “The
Mocking Bird,” and having imitated a peal
of bells with more or less fidelity he laid
down the mallets and bowed to his audience
so majestically that the deadheads in the
front seats felt compelled to clap their hands,
part of the etiquette of “The Profession,”
which never criticises until after the per-
formance:
“I knew it,” said Birdie again. “The
sillyphone turn’s finished, an’ now he’ll
shorten our lives with that bit of brass pipe.
Oh, mother, mother!”
Hamstinger’s air of devotion to the cornet,
his respectful handling of it, said more
plainly than words that the cornet was
sacred. He became no mere musician. He
was the Hierophant of Blare; the Arch- ,
priest of Noise.
He slowly elevated the cornet to the level
of his mouth and lipped it as if he were
about to drink it. His eyes bulged as he
blew. The pathos of love abandoned became
a blatant scream that sought the listeners’
bones:
When oth-er lips
And oth-er-r hearts
Theyar tale of love shall tell-yell-hell—•
“Oh, me!” said Birdie. “The Micks are
gettin’ old Ireland to-night, but it’s givin’
me goose flesh.”
“Ssh !” said Aladdin. “You’re a guest.”
“Is that any reason why he should turn
me blood inter sawdust?”
“Quiet!”
Hamstinger concluded with a triumphant
shriek to the effect of “You will remember
me,” which was merely stating the obvious,
and then in response to non-existent encores,
he played other old favorites—which would
be even greater favorites if they were given
Christian burial. And then he made his
announcement that his company would play
oil one more occasion in this beautiful little
town; that the show was refined and could
not offend the most fastidious: that the
Waxworks were full of highly moral teach-
ing (he was looking at that moment directly
at Deeming. Ned Kelly and the uncomfort-
able reversed infant of the Judgment of
Solomon waiting for the arbitrament of the
sword). Finally he said that the curtain
would fall for a moment, and rise on the
concluding part of the entertainment. Then
he bowed with tremendous importance and
the •curtain fell on him falteringly and un-
evenly. as if the old man’s hands were not
sure of the weight.
It rose again with a rush, for Hamstinger
was on it this time. Then they saw that
another curtain had risen also—a curtain in
the upper part of that draped frame which
looked like a small bathing-box.
The dry little cough came from within the
frame. Two marionettes danced and
threatened upon the six-inch-wide stage
above. The cracked voice of the sick man
began to play all the parts in the immortal
drama of Pontius Pilate, or Punch and
.Judy, as you will.
While the general audience laughed and
applauded, the Great Australian Dramatic
Company in the front, seats was silent. Even
Birdie found pity for the hidden comedian —
coughing between his simulated chuckles,
struggling for the elusive breath and bat-
tling bravely to the end.
Suddenly, when Punch, having killed Judy,
is evading arrest by the murder of the
policeman, the front seaters were conscious
of a change in the invisible cast.
“That’s the way to do it,”'the marionette
should have squeaked; but it uttered the
callous impudence as if it were saddest
tragedy.
“That’s the way—to do —to do it.”
Then the stricken brain felt that here had
been an unforgivable liberty with the text,
and attempted to return upon itself. “That’s
—that’s the way” The draped side of
the booth was forced outward. There was
no noise of a fall, but only as if something
(Continued on next page.)

RALPH ABERCROMBY,
Denison House,
George Street, SYDNEY,
very frail had collapsed upon itself—only
the rustling of falling clothes.
Hamstinger dropped the curtain in
haste. Aladdin, followed by the Great Aus-
tralian Dramatic Company, ran to the stage
and found there the old man with blood
upon his beard.
"Oh—the poor tiling !” cried Birdie. “An’
me chippin’ him about his pants.”
They hurried her and the other women
off the stage while Hamstinger dismissed
the audience with an invitation to come
to-morrow night and to the matinee in the
afternoon. Aladdin, seeing that here was
no passing indisposition, ran to the grocer’s
shop and borrowed the grocer’s cart, and
having laid the old man therein drove him
to the hospital.
There were preliminaries in the matter of
admission, but Aladdin was so energetic
and authoritative that precedent removed
itself and regulation found itself forgotten.
Aladdin, before leaving, saw the old gentle-
man laid in a bed of snowy linen and sink
into it as if he tried to sink out of the
world.
Yet he gave a long look to his clothes as
they were taken from the ward—the rusty-
edged frock-coat and the Paris hat brushed
the wrong way.
“He didn’t seem to care for anything but
his E flat wardrobe—what?” said Saxby
Leicester, who had noted the look.
Aladdin did not reply. He knew that
the look was not solicitude but farewell.
* * * *
It’s an unfortunate affair,” said Ham-
stinger. “But no man can arrange his life.
Sometimes, when I am travelling to bush
towns in good weather, with a nice tent
and a couple of good freaks, the sun setting
and turning the gum-trees to gold, a ten-
pound house certain and me playing the
corn -et in front of the tent to bring them in,
I feel like praying that it should go on for
ever. But I know it can’t. Change and
decay in all around I see! Now, it struck
me just now that as we cannot play the
drama of Punch and Judy to-morrow, the
vaudeville will be represented only by the
xylophone and the corn-et. Now, instead
of us playing opposition to one another to-
morrow night, why not join forces, send the
bellman out, and let your company provide
the variety part of the entertainment?”
“Yes—sharing equally.”
“You’re on. I’ll get the bellman out in
the afternoon.”
They rehearsed next morning, Birdie at
bor song and dance, Athene Pallas reciting
“Ostler Joe” and playing in a scratch farce
with Kemble and Trewhella Jordan, and
Mildred Floyd and Saxby Leicester in a
sketch of dull soliloquy and elephantine
humor, with burnt-cork marks on their
cheeks to give point to a kiss in the dark.
Trewhella was also to read a sketch from
Dickens.
It was noted later that Trewhella Jordan
and Saxby Leicester had been deeply inter-
ested that afternoon in the figures of Henry
Parkes and Beaconsfield.
In the monotony of their work and wan
dering from township to township, where-
of all seemed built to the same plans and
specifications, the change to “variety” from
the drama” excited them all as if it were
the first night of a new play, though they
pretended that they were suffering a loss of
caste.
“Bit of a come down,” said Birdie, “play-
in’ in vodvil.”
“Voderveel, my child,” corrected Athene
Pallas.
“It all depends whether your university
was Oxford or Cambridge,” replied Birdie,
pertly. “I’ll ask ’em all on the stage.”
She ■ did, and returned with many vari-
ants in pronunciation.
“Accordin’ to all you voice-producers,”
said Birdie, “it’s voydyvill, an’ voadvil, an’
you say vodyveel, an’ Hamstinger calls it
vaudville. I say vodvil an’ I think it’s
vodevil. It’s as good as any other, an’ they
can’t it against a girl anyway.”
“It’s a great house,” said Hamstinger to
Aladdin.
“Fourteen pounds six. Your people are
doing all right. They dress their sketches
well too.”
Aladdin agreed, and laughed with all the
joke to himself. Trewhella Jordan in’ a
very new evening suit had read “The Death
of Little Nell”—-a rather amusing item,
considering his inability to pronounce m’s
or n’s. And at that moment Saxby Leices-
ter came on the stage. He wore a fine new
frock-coat. His boots were highly varnished,
though his trousers were at half-mast
by reason of having been made for shorter
legs than his. He was magnificent.
Aladdin, sitting with Hamstinger. rather
liked the nonchalant character Saxby gave
to his part. Hamstinger was openly enthu-
siastic, and hinted at a permanent amalga-
mation of forces.
Saxby, persistently debonair, crossed his
left leg over his right knee, and exposed
the sole of his varnished boot.
Hamstinger looked, and looked again, and
as the horrible truth came to him, he
clutched Aladdin’s arm, and uttered a cry
of pain.
“What’s the matter?” asked Aladdin.
“Look at his boots.”
“Well, I’m looking.”
“There’s a little square hole in the middle
of the heels.’
‘‘Yes.”
‘That’s where the rod goes through to
hold the figure upright on the floor.”
“He’s taken the boots off the figure? What
a lark!”
“Lark! Lark! Taking my wardrobe! I
b leeve they’ve got more. I'll see.”
He left Aladdin, who did not see him
again until the audience had gone home.
Then he was found in the hall, ready with
£7 3s. as the share of the Great Australian
Dramatic Company, and full of cold fury
at that company’s conduct in the matter of
wardrobe. He sat among his figures, strip-
ping them and packing them for removal to
the next town —wardrobe in a trunk on the
left of him, wax heads in a great box to the
right, uprights and body frames in front
to await their crates.
“Seven pounds three. Mr. Bluffson,” he
said. “There it is. You’re a gentleman,
but I want nothing more to do with actors
like those two fellows. Yes, I’ve got the
wardrobe again, but imagine my feelings.
Thinking all was right with the show, and
after I find out about the boots I search
for Henry Parkes and Beaconsfield. Where
d° I find them, sir? Under the stage, Mr.
Bluffson—even their shirts taken off—those
two great statesmen, sir, stark naked.”
He stopped for a moment to look at the
busts nearest to his hand—the wax imita-
tions of Robespierre, Mary of Scotland,
Deeming, President Garfield and Ned Kelly
and resumed his speech and his packing.
“‘I know what ingratitude is, Mr. Bluff-
son. I know it as well as these figures here.
I have known trouble, like all of them” —he
indicated the martyrs and the murderers,
and the queen who was more murderer than
martyr, and sighed resignedly, as one for
whom all the bitterness of death is past.
“They understand me, and I understand
them —because both myself and them have
been up against it, all of us victims of
treachery and ingratitude.”
So Aladdin left him with his sympathisers,
and as his departure next day was to be
early, he quitted wax and went to the hospi-
tal to see clay. The personality of the old
gentleman—whose past or family none knew
of, not even Ilamstinger, but who had known
better days—was now represented by a mere
shell of a man fluttered by despairing breath.
He would never buffoon again ; never cover
up the slaying of Judy with the murder of
a policeman. He lay there quiet and con-
tent ; glad to be in hospital and die in clean
cool sheets.
“That’s the way to do it,” said Aladdin,
and laughed as if he were afraid he would
cry.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Cough Medicine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ A NY sick?” 0?
/-\ The hut door bangs open, and
from two lines of blanket-shrouded
forms comes the chorus: “Shut that blanky
door!”
“Come on, now—any men for sick par-
ade?’’ repeats the orderly corporal.
“Yes, me!” in a hoarse voice from the
end of the hut.
“Number?’’
“Four-one-five Privit Brown W. Forty
’levun months C. of E.”—all in one breath
from the hoarse voice.
“Righto, Brown. Parade outside orderly
room in five minutes. Show a leg there, you
others!”
“YVotcher swingin’ it on this time,
Brownie?”
“Swingin’ it be damned!• If you cows
was as bad as me ye’d be in yer boxes!”
“’E wasn’t too bad to go into Wilton
last night, was ’e, Joe?” says Jim Harris.
“Ah, leave the cow alone,” growls Joe.
“This is Tuesday, and the route march this
after’s got ’irn bluffed!”
Brown by this time has got his trousers
on and is struggling into his half-frozen
boots. It’s a bitter cold morning, and the
hut is full of mutterings about life in gen-
eral.
“Cut out that growling and get dressed,
you men,” says the hut-commander. “You’ll
get worse than this on the other side.”
A bugle blares and he turns his attention
t 6 Brown. “Shake it up, you for sick parade
—there goes the call.”
“Will I ’ave to put me putties on, Corp.?”
“Of course you will. You should know
that by now; you go ‘sick’ often enough.
Shake it up and cut the grousing out!”
By the time he is dressed sick parade has
fallen in, and Brown is greeted with “Fall
in on the left there. Sick p’rade, s’hun!
Ri’ turn! At ease, quick march!” and in
three minutes there are 50 aspirants for
“No Duty” outside the A.M.C. hut.
Of course the doctor is late, and after
vainly trying to “hot” a fag, Brownie gives
himself up to cursing the army in general,
and “quacks” in particular. The men shuffle
up to attention as the doc. appears. 'He
passes into _ the hut, and with a cheery
“Good morning” to the sergeant, says: “Big
parade to-day; what’s doing?”
“Route march this afternoon, sir.”
“Hum Well, trot ’em along.”
One after the other the men file in and
tell of “terrible pains in both my legs, sir,”
“ ’Orrible cramp in me back,” or “Me feet’s
that bad I can’t march, sir.”
The doctor is a miracle-worker. On the
magic words “No duty” the drawn face
brightens, the dull eye gleams and the bent
back straightens. Private Smith, who has
limped up on the left leg. hobbles awav on
the right. But let the aside be “Medicine
and duty” the sick man has a relapse. The
cough becomes consumptive, the limp be-
comes acute lameness, and you can hear a
man wince with the agony of his rheu-
matism.
“Private Brown !”
“ ’Ere, sir/’ comes a husky whisper, and
a wreck shuffles in.
“Well, Brown, what’s the trouble?” A
fit of coughing prevents the reply from
being heard at first.
“Got a horful cough, sir,” he manages to
say at last. _ “An’ I knock up on a march
very easy, sir. Me feet ain’t as good as
they might be. Me boots”
“Yes, anything more?”
“No, sir.” wheezes Brown.
“Give him a number nine and a dose of
soda sal.. Sergeant. Put him on light duty
to-day. Next.”
* * * *
“’Ow d’jer get on, Bill?” his mate asks
as he enters the hut.
“’E give me Light' Duty—settles the
blanky route march, any’ow.”
“Come a bit of a mucker, haven’t you?”
remarks Jim Harris.
“Who’s come a mucker?” asks Brown,
defiantly.
tv ‘l We A, the march is off, and the Light
I'uty blokes have got to put new straw in
tae sacks on the bay’pet course an’ then
swill out the empty huts!”

SOMETHING
BETTER!
If Engineers and Steam-plant Owners would only
realise the importance of EFFICIENTLY insulating
their Boilers, Steam Pipes, etc., more attention might
be paid to this particular matter. Great economies
can be effected in this direction, in some cases the coal
consumption being reduced as much as 50%. But the
insulating material must be of the very best.
Carbonate of Magnesia is to-day recognised as the best
non-conductor of heat. Newall's Coverings are com-
posed of 85% of this material, and 15% Asbestos—the
Asbestos being used for binding purposes only—and in
addition possess innumerable advantages over all other
forms of lagging. Newall’s Coverings are used, practi-
cally exclusively, by the British Admiralty, N.S.W.
and Victorian Governments, and have been used
throughout the Ultimo. White Bay, Melbourne, Perth
and Canberra Power Stations.
